Objection on Indication of 'Ordering' (Tartib)
Objection and response on the mode of indication of 'ordering' (tartib) on the form (hay'a).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the objection and response regarding the mode of indication of 'ordering' on the form, distinguishing between direct (mutabaqa) and implicative (iltizam) indication.

Core Idea
Cause indicates effect more clearly than effect indicates cause.

In defining the form of thought, the author claimed that 'ordering' indicates the form directly (mutabaqa). An objection argued that since the form is an effect of ordering, the indication should be implicative (iltizam), not direct. This lesson examines the objection and the response that uses a causal principle to defend the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the mu'tarid's claim: the form is an effect, so indication is implicative (iltizam), not direct.
2
Understand the basis of the objection
The objection implicitly relies on the weakness of effect-to-cause indication.
3
Analyze the response
The response reverses the relationship: ordering is the cause, form is the effect, and cause-to-effect indication is stronger.
4
Apply the causal principle
A specific cause indicates a specific effect, while a specific effect indicates only some cause. Thus ordering (cause) indicates the form (effect) specifically and strongly.
5
Conclude on the author's usage
The author used 'mutabaqa' to highlight the strength and clarity of the indication, as if it were direct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) اعترض عليه بأن صورة الفكر - كما اعترف به - هي الهيأة الاجتماعية، ولاشك أنها ليست نفس الترتيب، بل هي معلولة له فيكون دلالة الترتيب عليها التزامية كدلالته على المرتب. ويمكن أن يقال : إن دلالة الترتيب على الهيأة - التي هي المعلولة له - أظهر من دلالته على المرتب - الذي هو فاعله - لأن دلالة العلة على معلولها أقوى وأظهر من دلالة المعلول على علته، لأن العلة المعيّنة تدل على معلول معين والمعلول المعين يدلّ على علة ما، فأراد التنبيه على ذلك فعبر بالمطابقة على معنى أن دلالة الترتيب على الهيأة كالمطابقة في الظهور (شريف).
An objection was raised against him: that the form of thought—as he acknowledged—is the composite structure (hay'a ijtima'iyya), and there is no doubt that it is not the ordering itself, but rather an effect of it; thus the indication of 'ordering' on it is implicative (iltizamiyya), like its indication on the orderer. It can be said: the indication of 'ordering' on the form—which is its effect—is more apparent than its indication on the orderer—which is its agent—because the indication of the cause on its effect is stronger and more apparent than the indication of the effect on its cause, for a specific cause indicates a specific effect, while a specific effect indicates some cause. So he intended to draw attention to this, and therefore expressed it as 'direct' (mutabaqa) meaning that the indication of 'ordering' on the form is like direct indication in clearness. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response regarding the mode of indication (dalala) of the term 'ordering' (tartib) in the definition. The objection argues that since the form (hay'a) is the effect of ordering, the indication of 'ordering' on the form is by implication (iltizam), like its indication on the agent. Therefore, the author's claim that it indicates the formal cause directly (mutabaqa) is problematic. The response counters that the indication of the cause on its effect is stronger and more apparent than the indication of the effect on the cause, because a specific cause indicates a specific effect, while a specific effect indicates only some cause. So the author used 'mutabaqa' to emphasize the strength and clarity of the indication of 'ordering' on the form, as if it were direct.

Key Terms
المطابقة
الالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is the indication of 'ordering' on the form (hay'a) considered more apparent than on the agent?
A) Because the form is the same as ordering
B) Because the cause indicates a specific effect, while the effect indicates only some cause
C) Because the agent is unknown
D) Because the form is internal to thought

Key Takeaway
The indication of a cause on its effect is stronger and more specific than the reverse, which justifies treating the cause's indication as direct (mutabaqa) in certain contexts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the relationship between 'creating' (khalq) and 'the created' (makhluq). Is the indication of 'creating' on the created direct or implicative? Justify using the principle from this lesson.
?
In a debate about the attributes of Allah, a mutakallim claims that 'power' (qudra) directly indicates its effect (the act). An opponent objects that it is implicative. Which side does the principle of this lesson support? Explain.

Refuting the Entailment from Conformity to Implication
Author refutes the claim that mutabaqa entails iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why mutabaqa does not automatically entail iltizam and apply the condition of mental necessary concomitant.

Core Idea
Conceiving an essence does not guarantee conceiving its otherness, so conformity does not imply implication.

In this passage, the author tackles a critical issue: does merely coining a word for an essence automatically bring to mind some necessary concomitant? The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argued yes, but the author provides a simple counterexample that unravels the claim, with major implications for how we understand signification by implication and inclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of dalalat
Recall that words signify by mutabaqa (whole essence), tadammun (a part), and iltizam (something external but necessary). In this passage, we are testing whether mutabaqa always yields iltizam.
These types are defined in earlier lessons.
2
State the condition for iltizam
The author sets a necessary condition: the meaning must have a mental necessary concomitant (lazim dhihni), such that conceiving the meaning necessitates conceiving that concomitant. Without this, there is no iltizam.
This is a strict logical condition, not mere psychological association.
3
Analyze the Imam's claim
The Imam argued that every essence has at least the concomitant that it is not other than itself. Therefore, if a word signifies the essence by mutabaqa, it also signifies that concomitant by iltizam.
This seems plausible at first glance.
4
Understand the refutation and its consequence
The author denies the premise: we often conceive essences without thinking of their otherness. Therefore, the condition is not met, and mutabaqa does not entail iltizam. By extension, tadammun also does not entail iltizam, because composite essences may lack a mental necessary concomitant.
The author also notes a subtle imprecision in the wording of the original text: it proves not that tadammun does not entail iltizam, but that it is not proven to entail it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون لمعنى اللفظ لازم، بحيث يلزم من تصوّر المعنى تصوره، وكون كل ماهيّة بحيث يوجد لها لازم كذلك غير معلوم، لجواز أن يكون من الماهيات مالا يستلزم شيئاً كذلك، فإذا كان اللفظ موضوعاً لتلك الماهية كان دلالته عليها مطابقة، ولا التزام لانتفاء شرطه - وهو اللزوم الذهني. وزعم الإمام أنّ المطابقة مستلزمة للالتزام، لأن تصوّر كلّ ماهية يستلزم تصور لازم من لوازمها وأقله أنها ليست غيرها، واللفظ إذا دل على الملزوم بالمطابقة دلّ على اللازم في التصوّر بالالتزام. وجوابه أنا لانسلّم أن تصوّر كل ماهيّة يستلزم تصوّر أنها ليست غيرها، فكثيرا ما نتصور ماهيات الأشياء ولم يخطر ببالنا غيرها - فضلاً عن أنها ليست غيرها - ومن هذا تبين عدم استلزام التضمن الالتزام، لأنه كما لم يعلم وجود لازم ذهني لكلّ ماهية بسيطة، لم يعلم أيضاً وجود لازم ذهني لكل ماهيّة مركبة، لجواز أن يكون من الماهيات المركبة ما لا يكون له لازم ذهني، فاللفظ الموضوع بإزائه دال على أجزائه بالتضمن دون الالتزام. وفي عبارة المصنف تسامح، فإنّ اللازم مما ذكره ليس تبين عدم استلزام التضمن الالتزام، بل عدم تبين استلزام التضمن الالتزام، والفرق بينهما ظاهر. (شريف: مبناه على أن سلب الغير لازم ذهني لكل من المعاني بحيث يلزم من حصوله في الذهن حصوله فيه. وليس بصحيح، فإن نتصور كثير من المعاني مع الغفلة عن سلب غيرها عنها، ولوصح لاستلزم كل تصور تصديقاً، وهو باطل قطعاً. نعم سلب الغير لازم بين بالمعنى الأعم، وهو أن يكون تصور اللزوم مع تصور اللازم كافياً في الجزم بينهما باللزوم، واللزوم المعتبر في الالتزام هو اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص، وهو أن يكون تصوّر الملزوم مستلزماً لتصوّر اللازم.) (شريف: قد يتوهم أن مفهوم الكلية والجزئية - بل مفهوم التركيب - لازم ذهني لكل معنى مركب فيكون التضمّن مستلزماً للالتزام، وهو باطل؛ لأنا قد نتصور معنى مركباً مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً وعن مفهوم الكلية والجزئية، فليس شيء منها لازماً ذهنياً يلزم من تصوّر الملزوم تصوّره، وقد يدعى هاهنا أيضاً أنا نجزم بجواز تعقل بعض المعاني المركبة مع الغفلة عن جميع المفهومات الخارجية، على قياس ماقيل في المطابقة، فلايكون التضمن مستلزما للالتزام.)
That the utterance's meaning has a necessary concomitant, such that the conception of the meaning necessitates its conception, and that every essence has a necessary concomitant like that is not known, because it is possible that among essences there is that which does not necessitate anything like that. So if the word is coined for that essence, its signification of it is conformity, and no implication due to the absence of its condition, which is mental necessity. The Imam claimed that conformity entails implication, because the conception of every essence necessitates the conception of a necessary concomitant of its concomitants, the least of which is that it is not other than itself, and if the word signifies the entailed by conformity, it signifies the necessary concomitant in conception by implication. The response: we do not concede that the conception of every essence necessitates the conception that it is not other than itself, for often we conceive the essences of things and it does not occur to our minds other than them, let alone that they are not other than them. From this it becomes clear that inclusion does not entail implication, because just as the existence of a mental necessary concomitant for every simple essence is not known, so also the existence of a mental necessary concomitant for every composite essence is not known, because it is possible that among composite essences there is that which has no mental necessary concomitant. So the word coined for it signifies its parts by inclusion without implication. In the author's expression there is laxity, for what is necessary from what he mentioned is not the clarification of the non-entailment of inclusion to implication, but rather the non-clarification of the entailment of inclusion to implication, and the difference between them is apparent. (Sharif: Its basis is that negation of the other is a mental necessary concomitant for every meaning, such that its occurrence in the mind necessitates its occurrence in it. This is not correct, for we conceive many meanings while heedless of the negation of other than them. If it were correct, it would entail that every conception is an assent, which is absolutely false. Yes, negation of the other is a necessary concomitant evident in the general sense, which is that the conception of the necessity together with the conception of the necessary concomitant is sufficient for certain judgment between them regarding necessity, and the necessity considered in implication is the evident necessary concomitant in the specific sense, which is that the conception of the entailed necessitates the conception of the necessary concomitant.) (Sharif: One might mistakenly think that the concept of universality and particularity, nay the concept of composition, is a mental necessary concomitant for every composite meaning, thus inclusion would entail implication. This is false, for we may conceive a composite meaning while heedless of its being composite and of the concept of universality and particularity, so none of them is a mental necessary concomitant such that the conception of the entailed necessitates its conception. It might also be claimed here that we are certain of the possibility of understanding some composite meanings while heedless of all external concepts, analogous to what was said about conformity, so inclusion does not entail implication.)
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage critically examines the relationship between the three types of signification: mutabaqa (conformity), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The author begins by noting that for a word to signify by implication, the meaning must have a necessary concomitant (lazim) that is mentally inseparable. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argued that mutabaqa automatically entails iltizam because any conception of an essence includes a minimal concomitant that it is not other than itself. The author refutes this by providing a counterexample: we often conceive essences without any thought of what they are not. This shows that the necessary mental concomitant is not universal. Consequently, tadammun does not entail iltizam either, because composite essences may lack a necessary mental concomitant. The author concludes that the author of the text (likely al-Qazwini) was imprecise: his statement proves not that tadammun does not entail iltizam, but that it is not proven to entail it. The marginal notes by Sharif elaborate: the negation of otherness is not a necessary mental concomitant in the sense required for iltizam, and even the concepts of universality and composition are not necessary for every composite meaning. This rigorous analysis guards against conflating psychological association with logical implication.

Key Terms
المطابقة
التضمن
الالتزام
اللازم الذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does mutabaqa not entail iltizam?
A) Because iltizam requires a linguistic convention, not mental connection.
B) Because some essences have no mental necessary concomitant, as we conceive them without thinking of otherness.
C) Because conformity only applies to simple essences.
D) Because the Imam's argument was about tadammun, not mutabaqa.

Key Takeaway
Mutabaqa does not entail iltizam because not every essence has a mental necessary concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'salat' (prayer) in the Qur'an. Does it signify bowing and prostrating by inclusion (tadammun) or by implication (iltizam)? Apply the condition of mental necessary concomitant to justify your answer.
?
A student claims that because a word signifies an essence by mutabaqa, it automatically signifies every property of that essence by iltizam. Use the author's counterexample to refute this claim.

Al-Sharif's Refinement: Two Types of Evident Concomitant
Al-Sharif refines the condition for iltizam with two types of evident concomitant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lazim bayyini al-'amm and lazim bayyini al-khas, and apply this distinction to evaluate claims about tadammun entailing iltizam.

Core Idea
For iltizam, the concomitant must be evident in the specific sense, not just in the general sense.

In the marginal notes, al-Sharif expands on the author's refutation by clarifying the precise type of necessary concomitant required for implication. He distinguishes between two senses of 'evident' concomitant: one where the conception of the entailed together with the concomitant suffices for certainty, and another where the conception of the entailed alone necessitates the concomitant. Only the latter, he argues, is the condition for iltizam.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for iltizam
The earlier part of the passage established that iltizam requires a mental necessary concomitant (lazim dhihni). But what exactly counts as such? Al-Sharif now refines this.
Focus on the distinction he draws.
2
Understand the two types of evident concomitant
Al-Sharif introduces two senses: 1) Evident in the general sense (lazim bayyini al-'amm): when the conception of the necessity alongside the conception of the concomitant is sufficient to judge that the concomitant follows. 2) Evident in the specific sense (lazim bayyini al-khas): when the conception of the entailed alone suffices to necessitate the conception of the concomitant. Only the latter is the condition for iltizam.
The distinction turns on whether additional reasoning is needed.
3
Apply to the negation of otherness
Al-Sharif notes that 'negation of the other' is only evident in the general sense: we can judge that it is a concomitant when we reflect, but it is not automatically conceived whenever we conceive the essence. Therefore, it does not satisfy the condition for iltizam.
This is why the Imam's claim fails.
4
Apply to composition and universals
The second note addresses a potential objection: might not the concept of composition or universality be a necessary concomitant of every composite meaning? Al-Sharif says no, because we can conceive a composite meaning without reflecting on its composition or universal properties. So tadammun does not entail iltizam.
This reinforces the author's conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يكون لمعنى اللفظ لازم، بحيث يلزم من تصوّر المعنى تصوره، وكون كل ماهيّة بحيث يوجد لها لازم كذلك غير معلوم، لجواز أن يكون من الماهيات مالا يستلزم شيئاً كذلك، فإذا كان اللفظ موضوعاً لتلك الماهية كان دلالته عليها مطابقة، ولا التزام لانتفاء شرطه - وهو اللزوم الذهني. وزعم الإمام أنّ المطابقة مستلزمة للالتزام، لأن تصوّر كلّ ماهية يستلزم تصور لازم من لوازمها وأقله أنها ليست غيرها، واللفظ إذا دل على الملزوم بالمطابقة دلّ على اللازم في التصوّر بالالتزام. وجوابه أنا لانسلّم أن تصوّر كل ماهيّة يستلزم تصوّر أنها ليست غيرها، فكثيرا ما نتصور ماهيات الأشياء ولم يخطر ببالنا غيرها - فضلاً عن أنها ليست غيرها - ومن هذا تبين عدم استلزام التضمن الالتزام، لأنه كما لم يعلم وجود لازم ذهني لكلّ ماهية بسيطة، لم يعلم أيضاً وجود لازم ذهني لكل ماهيّة مركبة، لجواز أن يكون من الماهيات المركبة ما لا يكون له لازم ذهني، فاللفظ الموضوع بإزائه دال على أجزائه بالتضمن دون الالتزام. وفي عبارة المصنف تسامح، فإنّ اللازم مما ذكره ليس تبين عدم استلزام التضمن الالتزام، بل عدم تبين استلزام التضمن الالتزام، والفرق بينهما ظاهر. (شريف: مبناه على أن سلب الغير لازم ذهني لكل من المعاني بحيث يلزم من حصوله في الذهن حصوله فيه. وليس بصحيح، فإن نتصور كثير من المعاني مع الغفلة عن سلب غيرها عنها، ولوصح لاستلزم كل تصور تصديقاً، وهو باطل قطعاً. نعم سلب الغير لازم بين بالمعنى الأعم، وهو أن يكون تصور اللزوم مع تصور اللازم كافياً في الجزم بينهما باللزوم، واللزوم المعتبر في الالتزام هو اللازم البين بالمعنى الأخص، وهو أن يكون تصوّر الملزوم مستلزماً لتصوّر اللازم.) (شريف: قد يتوهم أن مفهوم الكلية والجزئية - بل مفهوم التركيب - لازم ذهني لكل معنى مركب فيكون التضمّن مستلزماً للالتزام، وهو باطل؛ لأنا قد نتصور معنى مركباً مع الذهول عن كونه مركباً وعن مفهوم الكلية والجزئية، فليس شيء منها لازماً ذهنياً يلزم من تصوّر الملزوم تصوّره، وقد يدعى هاهنا أيضاً أنا نجزم بجواز تعقل بعض المعاني المركبة مع الغفلة عن جميع المفهومات الخارجية، على قياس ماقيل في المطابقة، فلايكون التضمن مستلزما للالتزام.)
That the utterance's meaning has a necessary concomitant, such that the conception of the meaning necessitates its conception, and that every essence has a necessary concomitant like that is not known, because it is possible that among essences there is that which does not necessitate anything like that. So if the word is coined for that essence, its signification of it is conformity, and no implication due to the absence of its condition, which is mental necessity. The Imam claimed that conformity entails implication, because the conception of every essence necessitates the conception of a necessary concomitant of its concomitants, the least of which is that it is not other than itself, and if the word signifies the entailed by conformity, it signifies the necessary concomitant in conception by implication. The response: we do not concede that the conception of every essence necessitates the conception that it is not other than itself, for often we conceive the essences of things and it does not occur to our minds other than them, let alone that they are not other than them. From this it becomes clear that inclusion does not entail implication, because just as the existence of a mental necessary concomitant for every simple essence is not known, so also the existence of a mental necessary concomitant for every composite essence is not known, because it is possible that among composite essences there is that which has no mental necessary concomitant. So the word coined for it signifies its parts by inclusion without implication. In the author's expression there is laxity, for what is necessary from what he mentioned is not the clarification of the non-entailment of inclusion to implication, but rather the non-clarification of the entailment of inclusion to implication, and the difference between them is apparent. (Sharif: Its basis is that negation of the other is a mental necessary concomitant for every meaning, such that its occurrence in the mind necessitates its occurrence in it. This is not correct, for we conceive many meanings while heedless of the negation of other than them. If it were correct, it would entail that every conception is an assent, which is absolutely false. Yes, negation of the other is a necessary concomitant evident in the general sense, which is that the conception of the necessity together with the conception of the necessary concomitant is sufficient for certain judgment between them regarding necessity, and the necessity considered in implication is the evident necessary concomitant in the specific sense, which is that the conception of the entailed necessitates the conception of the necessary concomitant.) (Sharif: One might mistakenly think that the concept of universality and particularity, nay the concept of composition, is a mental necessary concomitant for every composite meaning, thus inclusion would entail implication. This is false, for we may conceive a composite meaning while heedless of its being composite and of the concept of universality and particularity, so none of them is a mental necessary concomitant such that the conception of the entailed necessitates its conception. It might also be claimed here that we are certain of the possibility of understanding some composite meanings while heedless of all external concepts, analogous to what was said about conformity, so inclusion does not entail implication.)
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage critically examines the relationship between the three types of signification: mutabaqa (conformity), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (implication). The author begins by noting that for a word to signify by implication, the meaning must have a necessary concomitant (lazim) that is mentally inseparable. The Imam (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) argued that mutabaqa automatically entails iltizam because any conception of an essence includes a minimal concomitant that it is not other than itself. The author refutes this by providing a counterexample: we often conceive essences without any thought of what they are not. This shows that the necessary mental concomitant is not universal. Consequently, tadammun does not entail iltizam either, because composite essences may lack a necessary mental concomitant. The author concludes that the author of the text (likely al-Qazwini) was imprecise: his statement proves not that tadammun does not entail iltizam, but that it is not proven to entail it. The marginal notes by Sharif elaborate: the negation of otherness is not a necessary mental concomitant in the sense required for iltizam, and even the concepts of universality and composition are not necessary for every composite meaning. This rigorous analysis guards against conflating psychological association with logical implication.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sharif, what type of evident concomitant is required for iltizam?
A) The general sense, where reflection on the relationship suffices.
B) The specific sense, where the conception of the entailed alone necessitates the concomitant.
C) The absolute sense, where the connection is established by definition.
D) The inclusive sense, where the concomitant is a part of the essence.

Key Takeaway
Only concomitants that are evident in the specific sense, where conceiving the entailed alone brings the concomitant to mind, count as mental necessary concomitants for iltizam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A student claims that the term 'body' implies 'occupying space' by iltizam because we cannot conceive body without space. Use al-Sharif's two types of evident concomitant to evaluate this claim. Is 'occupying space' a lazim bayyini al-'amm or al-khas? Justify.
?
Consider the term 'ilm' (knowledge). Does it imply 'life' (being alive) by iltizam? Apply the condition of lazim bayyini al-khas.

The Four Relations Between Universals
Author defines the four relations between universals and answers an objection about nonexistent terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relations holds between any two given Islamic science terms and justify your answer using the definitions.

Core Idea
Every pair of universals has exactly one of four relations; mastering them is essential for precise definitions and valid inferences.

In Islamic logic, understanding how universals relate is crucial for building definitions and syllogisms. The author presents four exhaustive relations: equality, absolute generality, partial generality, and mutual exclusivity. This lesson will help you classify any two terms and avoid common pitfalls, such as misclassifying hypothetical terms that never apply.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Find the two terms being compared. For example, 'insan' (man) and 'natiq' (rational), or 'hayawan' (animal) and 'abyad' (white). Ensure they are universals (kulliyat).
2
Test for equality (tasawi)
Check if each term applies to all instances of the other. If yes, they are equal. Example: 'insan' and 'natiq' are equal because every man is rational and every rational being is a man (in the Aristotelian framework).
Equality is the strongest relation.
3
Test for absolute generality (umum wa khusus mutlaq)
If one term applies to all instances of the other but not vice versa, then there is absolute generality and particularity. The broader term is general, the narrower is particular. Example: 'hayawan' is general to 'insan' because every man is an animal but not every animal is a man.
The broader term is often the genus.
4
Test for partial generality (umum wa khusus min wajh)
If each term applies to some but not all instances of the other, then there is generality from one aspect. Example: 'hayawan' and 'abyad' – some animals are white, some white things are animals, but not all.
This is also called 'intersection'.
5
If no overlap, they are mutually exclusive (tabayun)
If neither term applies to any instance of the other, they are mutually exclusive. Example: 'insan' and 'faras' (horse). However, remember the caveat: terms like 'la shay' (nonexistence) that never apply to anything are excluded from this classification.
Mutual exclusivity is complete separation.

[۲۸ - التساوي والتباين والعموم والخصوص
قال : الثالث الكلّيان متساويان، إن صدق كل واحد منهما على كلّ ما يصدق عليه الآخر، كالإنسان والناطق.

[28 - Equality, Mutual Exclusivity, Absolute Generality and Partial Generality]
He said: Third: Two universals are equal if each one is true of everything of which the other is true, like man and rational.
And between them there is absolute generality and particularity if one of them is true of everything of which the other is true without the converse, like animal and man.
And between them there is generality and particularity from one aspect if each of them is true of only some of that of which the other is true, like animal and white.
And they are mutually exclusive if neither of them is true of anything of which the other is true, like man and horse.
I say: The relations between two universals are restricted to four: equality, absolute generality and particularity, generality and particularity from one aspect, and mutual exclusivity.
That is because when a universal is related to another universal, either they are both true of the same thing or they are not; if they are not true of anything at all, then they are mutually exclusive.
(1) An objection was raised against this: that non-existence and the impossible by general possibility are not true of anything at all – neither externally nor in the mind. If they are considered mutually exclusive, then there must be between their contradictories a partial mutual exclusivity – as will come – and that is false, because 'thing' and 'general possible' are equal; and if they are not included among the mutually exclusive, then something not from them has entered into the definition.
And it was answered by restricting the claim to universals that are true in reality of something or things, or that can be true as such. Then hypothetical universals whose truth in reality is impossible for any thing externally or mentally are excluded. So it is as if it were said: 'The two universals...'
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage defines the four possible relations between two universals. The original text (likely a matn) lists: equality (like man and rational), absolute generality-particularity (like animal and man), partial generality-particularity (like animal and white), and mutual exclusivity (like man and horse). Then the commentator adds his own clarification that these four exhaust all possibilities, because either the two universals share some instance or not; if they share nothing, they are mutually exclusive. An objection follows: what about terms like 'nonexistence' (لا شيء) and 'the impossible by general possibility' (اللاممكن بالإمكان العام)? These are not true of anything, so if we consider them universals, they would be mutually exclusive, but then their contradictories ('thing' and 'general possible') would have partial mutual exclusivity according to another rule, which is false. Alternatively, if they are not considered mutually exclusive, then the definition of mutual exclusivity would include things that are not true of anything but still not captured. The answer restricts the definition to universals that are actually or possibly true of something, excluding hypothetical universals like these that can never be true. This preserves the correctness of the fourfold classification.

The Four Relations Between Universals: Parsing Equality, Opposition, and Generality
Author defines the four relations between universals with examples and logical forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four relations holds between any two given universals and express that relation using the correct categorical proposition forms.

Core Idea
Four relations, four proposition patterns: memorize which pattern yields which relation.

In this passage, al-Qutbi defines the four possible relations between the extensions of two universals. These relations are foundational for understanding immediate inference and definition. You will learn to recognize each relation by its characteristic combination of universal and particular, affirmative and negative propositions. Mastering these patterns will allow you to analyze definitions and arguments in Islamic logic with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Determine the two terms (e.g., 'human' and 'animal'). Note that each is a universal (kulli) applicable to multiple individuals.
2
Determine the quantitative relation
Check if one applies to all of the other, some, or none. Do they coincide, overlap, or are they exclusive?
3
Match to one of the four patterns
Use the logical form patterns: tabayn (two universal negatives), tasawi (two universal affirmatives), umum mutlaq (one universal affirmative + one particular negative), umum min wajh (one particular affirmative + two particular negatives).
4
Write the corresponding categorical propositions
Produce the exact sentences as given in the passage (e.g., 'Every A is B', 'No A is B', 'Some A is B', 'Some A is not B').

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلقاً كالإنسان والحيوان، فإن الإنسان حيوان وليس كل حيوان إنساناً. وإن لم يصدق كان بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، وكل واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه وأخص من وجه، فإنهما لما صدقا على شيء ولم يصدق أحدهما على كلّ ما صدق عليه الآخر كان هناك ثلاث صور: إحداها ما يجتمعان فيها على الصدق، والثانية ما يصدق فيها هذا دون ذاك، والثالثة ما يصدق فيها ذاك دون هذا - كالحيوان والأبيض فإنهما يصدقان معاً على الحيوان الأبيض، ويصدق الحيوان بدون الأبيض على الحيوان الأسود؛ وبالعكس في الجماد الأبيض – فيكون كل واحد منهما شاملاً للآخر ،وغيره، فالحيوان شامل للأبيض وغير الأبيض، والأبيض شامل للحيوان وغير الحيوان؛ فباعتبار أن كل واحد منهما شامل للآخر وغيره يكون أعم منه ; وباعتبار أنه مشمول له يكون أخص منه. فمرجع التباين إلى سالبتين كليتين من الطرفين، كقولنا «لا شيء مما هو إنسان فهو فرس» و «لا شيء مما هو فرس فهو إنسان». والتساوي إلى موجبتين كلّيّتين، كقولنا : «كل ما هو إنسان فهو ناطق» و «كل ما هو ناطق فهو إنسان». والعموم المطلق إلى موجبة كلّيّة من أحد الطرفين وسالبة جزئية من الطرف الآخر ، كقولنا «كل ما هو إنسان فهو حيوان» و «ليس بعض ما هو حيوان فهو إنسان». والعموم من وجه إلى سالبتين جزئيتين وموجبة جزئية، كقولنا : «بعض ما هو حيوان هو أبيض» و «ليس بعض ما هو حيوان هو أبيض» و «ليس بعض ما هو أبيض هو حيوان».
Absolutely, like man and animal: for man is an animal but not every animal is man. And if it does not apply, there is between them generality and specificity from one perspective, and each one of them is more general than the other from one perspective and more specific from another. For when they both apply to something and one of them does not apply to everything the other applies to, there are three cases: first, where they both apply together; second, where this one applies without that one; third, where that one applies without this one – like animal and white: they both apply together to a white animal, and animal applies without white to a black animal; and conversely in the case of a white inanimate object. Thus each one of them includes the other and other things: animal includes white and non-white, and white includes animal and non-animal. So from the perspective that each includes the other and other things, it is more general than it; and from the perspective that it is included by it, it is more specific. So the reference of mutual opposition (tabayn) is to two universal negatives from both sides, like our saying: 'Nothing that is a human is a horse' and 'Nothing that is a horse is a human.' And equality (tasawi) is to two universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is human.' And absolute generality (umum mutlaq) is to a universal affirmative from one side and a particular negative from the other side, like our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some animal is not human.' And generality from one perspective (umum min wajh) is to two particular negatives and one particular affirmative, like our saying: 'Some animal is white' and 'Not every animal is white' and 'Not every white is animal.'
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the four relations that can hold between two universals: mutual opposition (tabayn), equality (tasawi), absolute generality (umum mutlaq), and generality from one perspective (umum min wajh). The author first illustrates absolute generality with the example of man and animal: every man is an animal, but not every animal is a man. Then he explains that if neither universal applies to all of the other, there is generality from one perspective (umum min wajh), and gives the example of animal and white: they intersect (e.g., a white animal) but each also applies where the other does not (e.g., animal applies to a black animal, white applies to a white inanimate object). He then provides the logical forms for each relation: tabayn is two universal negatives; tasawi is two universal affirmatives; umum mutlaq is one universal affirmative and one particular negative; umum min wajh is one particular affirmative and two particular negatives. The explanation emphasizes that these relations are defined by the quantity and quality of the propositions that describe the extension of the two universals. Students should memorize these patterns, as they are foundational for understanding categorical propositions and syllogistic reasoning.

The four relations between universals are defined by the quantity and quality of categorical propositions that describe their extensions: tabayn (two universal negatives), tasawi (two universal affirmatives), umum mutlaq (one universal affirmative and one particular negative), and umum min wajh (one particular affirmative and two particular negatives).

Why the Four Relations Only Apply Between Universals
Author defends the restriction of the four relations to universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the four relations (equality, mutual opposition, absolute generality, generality from one perspective) are restricted to universals and resolve the objection about two particulars being conjunct.

Core Idea
The four relations only fully apply when both terms are universals — particulars cannot share the range of relations.

The author has just defined the four relations between universals. But why only between universals? In this passage, he explains the rationale and responds to potential objections, including a clever case where two descriptions seem to refer to the same particular. This lesson will walk you through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three possible pairs of concepts
Identify that two concepts (mafhumayn) can be either both universals, both particulars, or one universal and one particular. The author uses this trichotomy to test where the four relations apply.
Universal = applies to many; particular = applies to one individual.
2
Check the four relations against each pair
For two particulars, only mutual opposition (tabayn) is possible because each is a unique individual. For a universal and a particular, only two relations can occur (e.g., the particular falls under the universal or not). Only for two universals do all four relations fully apply.
The author emphasizes that if the phrase 'two concepts' had been used instead of 'two universals', one might wrongly assume all four relations apply to all pairs.
3
Resolve the objection about conjunct particulars
Consider the objection: 'This laughing one' and 'this writer' appear to be two particulars that are conjunct (muttasadiqan) — i.e., they share the same referent. So they would not be mutually exclusive. The author clarifies: if they refer to different individuals (Zayd vs 'Amr), they are mutually exclusive. If they refer to the same individual (Zayd), then there is only one real particular with two attributes, not two distinct particulars. Hence the four relations do not apply.
This shows that the attributes do not create a new particular; distinction must be real, not merely conceptual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اعتبرت النسب بين الكلّيّين دون المفهومين ، لأن المفهومين إما كليان أو جزئيان أو كلّي وجزئي، والنسب الأربع لا تتحقق في القسمين الأخيرين : أما الجزئيان فلأنهما لا يكونان إلا متباينين، وأما الكلي والجزئي فلايوجد فيهما إلا قسمان فقط، وفي الجزئيين إلا قسم واحد؛ فلوقال : «المفهومان المتساويان... - إلى آخر التقسيم - لربما توهم جريان جميع هذه الأقسام الأربعة في كل واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة، فلما قال : «الكليان علم أن ليس حال القسمين الأخيرين كذلك، وإلا لكان التخصيص لغواً. فإن قلت: قد علم مما ذكر عدم جريان النسب الأربع فيهما، لكن لم يعلم ماذا فيهما من تلك النسب ؟ قلت: يعلم ذلك بالمقايسة بأدنى التفات على أن المقصود الأصلي معرفة أحوال النسب الكليات بعضها مع بعض (شريف). فإن قلت: «هذا الضاحك» و «هذا الكاتب» جزئيان متصادقان فلايكونان متباينين. قلت: إن كان المشار إليه بـ «هذا الضاحك» زيداً مثلاً، وبهذا الكاتب عمراً فهناك جزئيان متباينان، وإن كان المشار إليه بهما «زيداً» مثلاً، فليس هناك إلا جزئي حقيقي واحد هو ذات زيد لكنه اعتبر معه تارة اتصافه بالضحك، وأخرى اتصافه بالكتابة، وبذلك لم يتعدّد الجزئي الحقيقي تعدّداً حقيقياً ولم يتغاير حقيقياً، بل هناك تعدّد وتغاير بحسب الاعتبارات، والكلام في
And the relations are considered between the two universals, not between the two concepts, because the two concepts are either both universals, both particulars, or one universal and one particular. And the four relations do not obtain in the last two divisions: as for two particulars, they are only mutually exclusive; and as for a universal and a particular, only two divisions exist, and for two particulars only one division exists. So if he had said 'the two equal concepts...' to the end of the division, it might be imagined that all these four divisions apply in each of the three divisions. But when he said 'the two universals' it is known that the case of the last two divisions is not so, otherwise the specification would be pointless. If you say: It has been learned from what was mentioned that the four relations do not apply in them, but it is not known what [relations] exist in them? I say: That is known by comparison with the slightest attention, and the primary aim is to know the states of universal relations with each other (Sharif). If you say: 'This laughing one' and 'this writer' are two particulars that are conjunct, so they are not mutually exclusive. I say: If the referent of 'this laughing one' is, for example, Zayd, and of 'this writer' is 'Amr, then there are two mutually exclusive particulars. And if the referent of both is, for example, Zayd, then there is only one real particular, which is the essence of Zayd, but it is considered sometimes with the attribute of laughing and sometimes with the attribute of writing; and by that the real particular is not multiplied in a real multiplication nor really distinct; rather there is multiplication and distinction according to considerations. Commentary
This passage clarifies why the four relations (mutual opposition, equality, absolute generality, generality from one perspective) are explicitly said to be between universals (kulliyyan) and not between any two concepts. The reason is that if we consider two concepts, they could be: two universals, two particulars, or one universal and one particular. The four relations only fully apply to two universals. For two particulars, they are always mutually exclusive (tabayn) because each particular is a unique individual; they cannot be equal or have generality relations. For a universal and a particular, only two of the four relations are possible (namely, the particular can be under the universal or not, giving either umum mutlaq or tabayn? Actually the text says only two divisions exist; presumably the particular is always a subset of the universal if it falls under it, so only umum mutlaq and tabayn are possible). The author then responds to two objections. First objection: If the four relations don't apply to the other cases, how do we know what relations do apply? The author says it is known by analogy (qiyas, i.e., reasoning by comparison) with minimal attention. Second objection: Two particulars like 'this laughing one' and 'this writer' might be thought to be conjunct (muttasadiqan) and thus not mutually exclusive. The author clarifies: if they refer to different individuals (e.g., Zayd and 'Amr), they are indeed mutually exclusive. If they refer to the same individual (Zayd), then they are not two distinct particulars but one particular with two attributes, so the four relations are not applicable.

Rule 1: Contradictories of Co-Extensive Terms
Author states the first rule about co-extensive universals and proves it by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that if two universals are co-extensive, their contradictories are also co-extensive.

Core Idea
The contradictories of co-extensive terms are also co-extensive — a metalogical symmetry.

Definition is rarely only about the defined term — it also constrains its negation. In this passage, the author extends the logic of co-extensive universals to their contradictories. Understanding how negation works with equal terms is crucial for building valid syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Identify the claim: if two universals are co-extensive (apply to exactly the same things), then their contradictories are also co-extensive.
2
Assume the contrary
Suppose for contradiction that the contradictories are not co-extensive. Then there exists an instance where one contradictory is true and the other false.
3
Derive the consequence
If one contradictory is true and the other false for some individual, then the corresponding original universal (the one whose contradictory is false) must be true of that individual, while the other original universal is false.
Because if a term's contradictory is false, the term itself is true.
4
Contradiction to co-extensiveness
This means the original universals differ in extension — one is true where the other is false — contradicting the given that they are co-extensive.
5
Conclude the rule holds
Since the assumption leads to a contradiction, the contradictories must be co-extensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ونقيضا المتساويين متساويان، وإلا لصدق أحدهما على بعض ما كذب عليه الآخر، فيصدق أحد المتساويين على ما كذب عليه الآخر وهو محال. ونقيض الأعم من شيء مطلقاً أخص من نقيض الأخص مطلقاً لصدق نقيض الأخص على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم من غير عكس؛ اما الأول فلانه لولا ذلك لصدق عين الأخصّ على بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الأعم وذلك مستلزم لصدق الأخص بدون الأعم وأنه محال؛ وأما الثاني فلانه لولا ذلك لصدق نقيض الأعمّ على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأخص وذلك مستلزم لصدق الأخص على كل الأعم وهو محال. والأعم من شيء من وجه ليس بين نقيضيهما عموم اصلاً، لتحقق مثل الجزئين المتغايرين تغايراً حقيقياً
He said: And the two contradictories of two co-extensive [universals] are co-extensive, otherwise one of them would be true of some of what the other is false of, so one of the co-extensive would be true of what the other is false of, which is impossible. And the contradictory of the unconditionally broader of something is unconditionally more specific than the contradictory of the unconditionally more specific, because the contradictory of the more specific is true of everything of which the contradictory of the broader is true, without the converse. As for the first [rule], because if it were not so, then the more specific itself would be true of some of what the contradictory of the broader is true of, which would entail the truth of the more specific without the broader, and that is impossible. And as for the second [rule], because if it were not so, then the contradictory of the broader would be true of everything of which the contradictory of the more specific is true, which would entail the truth of the more specific of all the broader, and that is impossible. And between the two contradictories of two [universals] that are mutually inclusive from one aspect, there is no universal relation at all, because there exist [cases] like two particular individuals that are truly diverse.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents three key rules about the relations between the contradictories (negations) of universal terms, as part of a commentary on the Shamsiyya. The first rule: If two universals are co-extensive (e.g., 'human' and 'rational animal'), their contradictories ('non-human' and 'non-rational animal') are also co-extensive. The proof is by contradiction: if not, one contradictory would be true of some instance the other is false of, implying the original co-extensive terms would differ, which is impossible. The second rule: For unconditionally broader and narrower universals (e.g., 'animal' broader, 'human' narrower), the contradictory of the broader ('non-animal') is unconditionally more specific than the contradictory of the narrower ('non-human'). That is, everything that is non-animal is also non-human, but not vice versa (e.g., a stone is non-animal and non-human, but a plant is non-human but not non-animal). The proof shows that if the reverse held, the narrower would be true of all the broader, impossible. The third rule: For universals related by mutual inclusion from one aspect (e.g., 'white' and 'animal'), their contradictories have no universal relation (neither broader nor narrower nor co-extensive), because one can find two truly diverse particulars that satisfy them. In Islamic logic, these rules are used in syllogistic contexts, especially with conversion and contradiction. For example, in the demonstration that the world has a creator, the contradictory of 'originated' and 'eternal' are handled carefully.

متساويان

C) non-A is narrower than non-B
D) There is no universal relation

Key Takeaway
The rule ensures that co-extensiveness is preserved under negation, which is vital for consistent definitions.

Rule 2: Contradictory of the Broader is More Specific
Author states the second rule about the contradictory of an unconditionally broader term being more specific than the contradictory of the narrower, with a two-part proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the relation between contradictories of broader and narrower universals and verify the direction of implication.

Core Idea
The negation flips the broader-narrower relation: the contradictory of the broader is narrower.

When one universal contains another, their negations behave in the opposite direction. Understanding this inversion is key to handling syllogisms with denied terms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
If universal A is unconditionally broader than B (every B is A, and not vice versa), then the contradictory of A (non-A) is unconditionally more specific than the contradictory of B (non-B). That is, non-A implies non-B but not vice versa.
Unconditionally broader means all instances of B are A, but some A are not B.
2
Understand the implication direction
Non-A is narrower: everything that is non-A is also non-B, but not everything non-B is non-A.
Example: non-animal (stone) is also non-human, but non-human (plant) is not necessarily non-animal.
3
Proof by contradiction - first part
Assume non-A does not imply non-B, i.e., there is something that is non-A but not non-B. Then that thing is B. So B is true without A, contradicting that A is broader.
Thus non-A implies non-B holds.
4
Proof by contradiction - second part
Assume non-B implies non-A (the reverse). Then suppose something is non-B. Then it is non-A, so it is not A. But there exist instances that are non-B yet A (since A is broader). This contradicts the assumption.
Thus non-B does not imply non-A.
5
Conclude asymmetry
Therefore non-A is narrower than non-B, with implication only one way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rule 3: No Universal Relation for Mutually Inclusive Terms
Author states the third rule about mutually inclusive universals, using a counterexample to show no universal relation between their contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify situations where the contradictories of two universals have no universal relation and explain why.

Core Idea
Not all pairs of universals yield a neat relation in their negations — mutual inclusion proves messy.

Sometimes universals partly overlap and partly differ. In such cases, their negations do not have a simple broader-narrower or co-extensive relation. This nuance prevents hasty generalisation in logical arguments. The passage explains why the contradictories of mutually inclusive terms have no universal relation.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'mutual inclusion from one aspect'
Two universals are mutually inclusive from one aspect (al-aʿamm min wajh) when they partially overlap: each has instances the other lacks, and they share some instances.
Example: 'white' and 'animal' – a white horse shares both, a white stone is white but not animal, a black horse is animal but not white.
2
State the rule
The contradictories of two such universals have no universal relation: neither is broader, narrower, nor co-extensive with the other.
3
Reason with a counterexample
The author says 'because there exist [cases] like two particular individuals that are truly diverse'. That is, we can find two individuals such that one satisfies the first contradictory but not the second, and another satisfies the second but not the first.
This breaks any universal implication.
4
Apply to 'white' and 'animal'
Take 'non-white' and 'non-animal'. Consider a black horse (non-white, but animal, so not non-animal) and a white stone (non-animal, but white, so not non-white). These show that neither contradictory implies the other universally.
Thus no universal relation.
5
Conclude the rule's importance
This rule prevents false generalisations when dealing with overlapping terms in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Contradictories of Equal Terms and an Objection on Negative Predicates
Commentator expounds the rule for equals and addresses an objection about negative predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that contradictories of equal terms are equal and identify the logical nuance in the objection that 'not non-writer' does not always entail 'writer'.

Core Idea
Equal terms have equal contradictories, but the proof's assumption about negation requires careful handling of modified predicates.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key rule about the relationship between contradictories of equal terms. He provides a proof by contradiction and then anticipates an objection that challenges a step in the proof. The objection introduces an important distinction between simple and modified predicates, which is crucial for understanding logical entailment in Islamic logic. This lesson will guide you through the proof and the objection, clarifying when 'not non-X' can be safely equated with 'X'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule being explained
The rule states: if two terms (e.g., 'human' and 'rational') are equal (coextensive), then their contradictories ('non-human' and 'non-rational') are also equal. This means they apply to exactly the same set of individuals.
2
Follow the proof by contradiction
Assume the contradictory of one equal term does not apply to something the other does. This leads to a chain: something is not non-rational but is non-human, which implies one equal term applies without the other – a contradiction. Thus, the original rule holds.
The proof shows that if the equal terms are truly coextensive, their negations must also be coextensive.
3
Spot the assumption in the proof
The proof assumes that if 'not non-rational' is false of some X, then 'rational' must be true of X. This is an immediate inference from a negative with a modified predicate to a simple affirmative.
The objection will challenge this assumption.
4
Understand the objection
The objection argues that 'X is not non-writer' does not entail 'X is a writer', because X might not exist. In that case, both 'X is writer' and 'X is non-writer' are false, so the negation of the latter does not force the former.
This reveals that the inference is only valid if we assume the subject exists (existential import).
5
Note the commentator's response
The commentator acknowledges the objection and promises a later discussion. This shows that the issue is nuanced and requires a deeper treatment of modified propositions.
The full response would clarify when the entailment holds (e.g., when the subject is assumed to be existing).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : لما فرغ من بيان النسب الأربع بين العينين شرع في بيان النسب بين النقيضين.
فنقيضا المتساويين متساويان، أي يصدق كل واحد من نقيضي المتساويين على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الآخر، وإلا لكذب أحد النقيضين على بعض ما صدق عليه نقيض الآخر، لكن ما يكذب عليه أحد النقيضين يصدق عليه عينه - وإلا لكذب النقيضان – فيصدق عين أحد المتساويين على بعض ما يصدق عليه نقيض الآخر، وهو يستلزم صدق أحد المتساويين بدون الآخر - هذا خلف - مثلاً يجب أن يصدق «كل لا إنسان لا ناطق» و «كل لا ناطق لا إنسان» وإلا لكان «بعض اللا إنسان ليس بلا ناطق فيكون بعض اللا إنسان لا ناطقاً» و «بعض الناطق لا إنساناً» - وهو محال.
1) أورد عليه أن صدق بعض اللا إنسان ليس بلا ناطق» لا يستلزم صدق «بعض اللا إنسان ناطق» لما سيأتى من أن السالبة المعدولة المحمول أعم من الموجبة المحصلة المحمول، الاترى أن صدق قولك ليس زيد بلاكاتب لايستلزم صدق قولك «زيد كاتب الجواز أن يكون زيد معدوماً فلايكون كاتباً ولا لا كاتباً
Statement: When he finished explaining the four relations between the [positive] terms themselves, he began explaining the relations between the [mutual] contradictories. So the contradictories of two equal [terms] are equal, i.e., each of the contradictories of the two equal terms is true of everything of which the contradictory of the other is true; otherwise, one of the two contradictories would be false of something of which the other contradictory is true. But that of which one contradictory is false has its correlative [positive] term true—otherwise both contradictories would be false—hence one of the equal terms would be true of something of which the other's contradictory is true, which entails one of the equal terms being true without the other—this is a contradiction. For example, 'Every non-human is non-rational' and 'Every non-rational is non-human' must be true; otherwise, 'Some non-human is not non-rational' would be true, and thus 'Some non-human is rational' and 'Some rational is non-human'—which is impossible. [Objection:] It is objected that the truth of 'Some non-human is not non-rational' does not entail 'Some non-human is rational', because it will come later that a universal negative with a modified predicate is more general than an affirmative with an amplified predicate. Do you not see that the truth of your statement 'Zayd is not non-writer' does not entail the truth of your statement 'Zayd is a writer', for it is possible that Zayd does not exist, so he is neither a writer nor a non-writer.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the previous matn. The commentator begins by reminding the reader that after discussing the four relations between positive terms (equality, etc.), the author now turns to the relations between their contradictories. He then re-explains the rule for equals: if two terms are equal, their contradictories are also equal. He provides a more detailed proof by contradiction and gives the specific example: if 'human' and 'rational' are equal, then 'non-human' and 'non-rational' must be coextensive. The proof shows that denying this leads to impossible consequences. Then an objection is raised: the proof assumes that 'not non-rational' entails 'rational', but this is not always true because the negative with a modified predicate ('not non-writer') is more general than the simple affirmative ('writer'); a non-existent entity is neither writer nor non-writer. The commentator acknowledges this and promises a later discussion (implying a response). This shows careful attention to the nuances between simple and modified negation.

الموجبة المحصلة المحمول

The rule that equal terms have equal contradictories holds, but the proof's step from 'not non-rational' to 'rational' requires the subject to exist; otherwise, the inference fails, as shown in the objection.

The Problem of Absolutely Universal Coextensive Terms
Author explains why proof fails for absolutely universal terms and distinguishes two considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the standard rule that contradictories of coextensive terms are coextensive fails and explain why using the example of 'thing' and 'possible'.

Core Idea
If a term applies to everything, its contradictory applies to nothing—breaking the proof of coextension.

The rule that contradictories of coextensive terms are themselves coextensive seems straightforward. But the author reveals a hidden flaw: when the terms are absolutely universal, their contradictories have no subject to be true of. This lesson unpacks that problem and the key distinction between concepts in themselves and concepts considered as predicated of something.

Guided Path
1
Understand the existence requirement
Affirmation (positive predication) requires the subject to exist; negation does not. This is the basis of the problem.
The author states: 'الإيجاب يستلزم وجود المحكوم عليه'.
2
Recognize coextension as two affirmatives
Coextension between A and B means 'Every A is B and every B is A'—two universal affirmative propositions, both requiring subject existence.
3
Identify absolutely universal terms
Terms like 'thing' and 'general possible' are true of every concept; their contradictories 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' have no subject at all.
The author gives this example explicitly.
4
Follow the objection and reply
An objector argues: if 'possible' and 'not-possible' are contradictories, if one is not true of something, the other must be. The author replies by distinguishing concepts in themselves from concepts considered as predicated.
The key: the contradictory of 'possible is true of Zayd' is 'not (possible is true of Zayd)', not 'not-possible is true of Zayd'.
5
Conclude why the proof fails
For absolutely universal terms, the standard proof that contradictories are coextensive fails because the required affirmative propositions with the contradictories as subjects have no existing subject.
This sets up the two solutions in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الكلى والجزئي
والسر في ذلك أنّ الإيجاب يستلزم وجود المحكوم عليه، ضرورة أن ثبوت مفهوم وجودي أو علمي لشيء يستلزم وجود ذلك الشيء، بخلاف السلب. فإن قلت : إذاكان الموضوع موجوداً فالسالبة المعدولة والموجبة المحصلة متلازمان - كماسيأتي - والحال فيما نحن فيه كذلك، لأن «اللا إنسان» صادق على موجودات محققة كالفرس وغيره. قلت : ذلك لا يجديك نفعاً، إذ ليس الكلام في خصوص هذا المثال، بل في نقيضي المتساويين مطلقاً، فإذا لم يصدق نقيضاهما على شيء أصلاً فهناك لايتم البرهان قطعاً كنقيضي «الشيء» و «الممكن العام» فإن «الشيء» و «الممكن العام» لما وجب صدقهما على كل مفهوم بحسب الأمر امتنع صدق «اللاشيء» و«اللاممكن» بحسبها على مفهوم من المفهومات ، فإذا قلت : «لولم يصدق «كـلّ لاشيء لاممكن» لصدق نقيضه، وهو بعض اللاشيء ليس بلا ممكن فيكون بعض اللاشيء ممكناه لاتجه المنع المذكور.
فإن قلت مفهوم «الممكن نقيض لمفهوم اللاممكن» فإذا لم يصدق أحدهما على شيء وجب أن يصدق عليه الآخر، وإلا لارتفع النقيضان معاً .
بديهة - فإن أورد عليه المنع كان مكابرة غير مسموعة. قلت : هذان المفهومان متناقضان إذا اعتبرا في أنفسهما هكذا منفردين من غير اعتبار صدقهما على شيء، وأما إذا اعتبر صدقهما على شيء حصل هناك قضيتان موجبتان : إحداهما معدولة والأخرى محصلة، كقولك «زيد ممكن» و «زيد لا ممكن» ولا تناقض بينهما، لأن نقيض صدق الممكن على شيء سلب صدقه عليه، لا صدق سلبه عليه، ولاشك أن المتساويين اعتبر صدقهما على شيء، إذ مرجع التساوي إلى موجبتين كليتين وأطراف القضايا اعتبر فيها الصدق على ذات الموضوع. فإذا قلت : «كلّ إنسان ناطق و كل ناطق إنسان» فقد اعتبرت صدقهما على أفرادهما، وكذلك إذا قلت : كل لا إنسان لا ناطق» فقد اعتبرت صدق
ونقيض الأعم من شيء مطلقاً أخص من نقيض الأخص مطلقاً، أي يصدق نقيض الأخص على كلّ ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم، وليس كلّ ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم.
«اللاناطق» على ذات «اللاإنسان» فإذا اخذت نقيضه بهذا الاعتبار كان هو سلب صدق اللاناطق عليه، وهو معنى قولنا بعض اللا إنسان ليس بلاناطق» لا صدق «الناطق» عليه، لأن الناطق» نقيض «اللا ناطق» في حالة الإفراد من غير اعتبار الصدق على شيء، لا في حالة اعتبار صدقه عليه ؛ فقد اشتبه عليك نقيضه باعتبار الصدق بنقيضه لا باعتبار الصدق، فوضعت أحدهما مكان الآخر، فالمنع متجه بلا مكابرة.
والمخلص أن يقال : إنا نأخذ نقيضي المتساويين باعتبار الصدق على شيء، فيكون نقيضاهما سلبيين هكذا : كلّ ما ليس بإنسان فهو ليس بناطق» و «كـلّ ماليس بناطق فهو ليس بإنسان فيحصل قضيتان موجبتان سالبنا الطرفين، والموجبة السالبة الطرفين لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع ، بخلاف المعدولة الطرفين - وقد حقق ذلك في موضعه - ولنا أيضاً أن تخص البحث بما إذا لم يكن المتساويان شاملين لجميع الأشياء ذهناً وخارجاً ، فإن نقيضيهما حينئذ يصدقان على موجود - إما خارجي أو ذهني - فيتم البرهان بلا اشتباه.
لا يقال: يلزم تخصيص القواعد. لأنا نقول : تعميمها إنما هو بحسب المقاصد ، وليس لنا زيادة غرض في معرفة أحوال نقائض الأمور العامة، إذ ليس في العلوم الحكمية قضية موضوعها أو محمولها نقيض الأمور الشاملة، وهذا الفنّ آلة لتلك العلوم، فلابأس بإخراجها عن قواعده ؛ بل اعتبارها يوجب اختلالا في حصر النسب كما مرّ، وفي تساوى نقيض المتساويين كما ذكرنا آنفا، وفي كون نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم - إلى غير ذلك وإصلاح هذا الاختلال يوجب تكلفات بعيدة (شريف).
Topics of the universal and the particular. And the secret in that is that affirmation necessitates the existence of the subject of judgment, necessarily because the establishment of an existential or conceptual attribute for something necessitates the existence of that thing, unlike negation. If you say: 'If the subject exists, then the modified negative and the unmodified affirmative are coextensive — as will come — and the case in what we are discussing is likewise, because 'not-human' is true of actual existents like horse and others.' I say: 'That does not benefit you, for the discussion is not about this particular example, but about the two contradictories of coextensive terms absolutely, so if their two contradictories are not true of anything at all, then the proof is definitely incomplete, like the two contradictories of 'thing' and 'general possible'. For since 'thing' and 'general possible' must be true of every concept according to the matter, it is impossible for 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' to be true of any concept. So if you say: 'If it were not that 'every not-thing is not-possible' is true, its contradictory would be true, namely 'some not-thing is not not-possible' so that some not-thing is possible, then the mentioned objection would not apply.' If you say: 'The concept of 'possible' is the contradictory of the concept of 'not-possible'. So if one of them is not true of something, the other must be true of it, otherwise both contradictories would be absent together — self-evident. If an objection is raised against that, it would be outrageous sophistry not to be heard.' I say: 'These two concepts are contradictory when considered in themselves, thus separate, without consideration of their truth about something. But when their truth about something is considered, there result two affirmative propositions: one modified and the other unmodified, like your saying 'Zayd is possible' and 'Zayd is not possible'. There is no contradiction between them, because the contradictory of the truth of 'possible' about something is the negation of its truth about it, not the truth of its negation about it. And there is no doubt that coextensive terms are considered with respect to their truth about something, for the reference of coextension is to two universal affirmatives, and in the terms of propositions, truth is considered with respect to the essence of the subject. So when you say: 'Every human is rational and every rational is human', you have considered their truth about their individuals, and likewise when you say: 'Every not-human is not-rational', you have considered the truth of 'not-rational' about the essence of 'not-human'. And the contradictory of the more general of something absolutely is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific absolutely, i.e., the contradictory of the more specific is true of everything of which the contradictory of the more general is true, but not everything of which the contradictory of the more specific is true is the contradictory of the more general true of it. So: 'not-rational' about the essence of 'not-human'. If you take its contradictory with this consideration, it is the negation of the truth of 'not-rational' about it, which is the meaning of our saying 'some not-human is not not-rational', not the truth of 'rational' about it, because 'rational' is the contradictory of 'not-rational' in the state of isolation without consideration of truth about something, not in the state of considering its truth about it. So you have confused its contradictory in terms of truth with its contradictory without consideration of truth, and you have put one in place of the other, so the objection is valid without sophistry. And the deliverance is to say: we take the two contradictories of the coextensive terms with consideration of truth about something, so their two contradictories are two negatives thus: 'Everything that is not human is not rational' and 'Everything that is not rational is not human', resulting in two affirmative propositions negative in both extremes. And the affirmative proposition negative in both extremes does not require the existence of the subject, unlike the one modified in both extremes — and that has been verified in its place. And we also have that we can restrict the discussion to when the coextensive terms are not inclusive of all things mentally and externally, for then their two contradictories are true of an existent — either external or mental — so the proof is completed without confusion. It cannot be said: 'This entails a restriction of the rules.' For we say: 'Their generalization is only according to the purposes, and we have no further goal in knowing the states of the contradictories of universal matters, for there is no proposition in the philosophical sciences whose subject or predicate is a contradictory of universal matters, and this art is a tool for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding them from its rules. Rather, considering them necessitates a disturbance in the enumeration of relations as has passed, and in the equality of the contradictory of the coextensive as we mentioned, and in the fact that the contradictory of the more specific is more general than the contradictory of the more general — and so on. And rectifying this disturbance necessitates far-fetched difficulties (Sharif).'
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis within the commentary on Shamsiyya. The author addresses a subtle problem regarding the rule that the contradictories of coextensive terms are themselves coextensive. The discussion begins by explaining why affirmation requires the existence of the subject: if one affirms a concept of something, that thing must exist. Negation does not have this requirement. The opponent objects by pointing out that if the subject exists, the modified negative and unmodified affirmative are coextensive, as in the example of 'not-human' which applies to real existents like horses. The author replies that this example is not the issue; the issue is when the coextensive terms are absolutely universal, like 'thing' and 'general possible', which are true of every concept. In that case, their contradictories ('not-thing' and 'not-possible') have no subject at all, so the standard proof (burhan) that the contradictories are also coextensive fails. The author then presents an objection from the opponent: if two concepts are contradictories, if one is not true of something, the other must be true; otherwise both would be absent, which is impossible. The author answers by distinguishing between considering concepts in themselves (abstractly) and considering their truth about something. When we consider them as related to something, we have two affirmative propositions (one modified, one unmodified) like 'Zayd is possible' and 'Zayd is not possible', which are not contradictory because the contradictory of 'possible' is the negation of its truth, not the truth of its negation. Since coextension is about truth of subjects, the contradictories must be taken as negative propositions (e.g., 'Everything that is not human is not rational') which are affirmative in form but have negative subject and predicate. Such propositions do not require actual existence of the subject. The author also offers a second solution: restrict the discussion to coextensive terms that are not absolutely universal, so their contradictories will have a subject. Finally, the author defends this restriction by noting that logic is a tool for philosophical sciences, and no philosophical proposition involves the contradictories of universal terms, so excluding them does no harm, while including them creates difficulties.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does the standard proof that contradictories of coextensive terms are coextensive fail for terms like 'thing' and 'general possible'?
A) Because those terms are not truly coextensive.
B) Because their contradictories have no subject to be true of.
C) Because the proof requires the subject to exist, but these terms are mental only.
D) Because the contradictories become modified propositions.

Key Takeaway
The rule that contradictories of coextensive terms are coextensive fails when the terms are absolutely universal because their contradictories lack a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify two terms from the science of Kalam that are coextensive and check whether their contradictories have any subject. If they are absolutely universal, explain why the standard rule that contradictories are coextensive fails.
?
In the passage, the objector gave the example 'not-human is true of a horse'. Using the author's reasoning, explain why this example does not rescue the general rule for absolutely universal terms.

Immediate Inference
advanced
10 min
Two Solutions: Negative Propositions and Restriction
Author gives two solutions for the problem and defends the restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two solutions—using propositions negative in both extremes (saalibat al-tarafayn) and restricting the discussion to non-universal terms—to preserve the rule of contradictories for coextensive terms.

Core Idea
The rule is saved by reinterpreting contradictories as negative-subject propositions or by limiting the domain to non-universal terms.

After diagnosing the problem with absolutely universal terms, the author offers two practical solutions. The first involves transforming the contradictories into affirmative propositions with negative subject and predicate, which do not require the subject to exist. The second is a methodological restriction: since logic serves the sciences, we may simply exclude these problematic terms from the scope of the rule. This lesson walks through both solutions and their justification.

Guided Path
1
Recall the requirement
Affirmative propositions require subject existence; this is why the standard proof fails for absolutely universal terms. We need a form that avoids this requirement.
2
First solution: Use saalibat al-tarafayn
Take the contradictories as affirmative propositions with negative subject and predicate, e.g., 'Everything that is not human is not rational'. Such propositions are affirmative in quality but have negative terms on both sides; they do not presuppose existence of the subject.
The author calls this 'الموجبة السالبة الطرفين' (affirmative negative in both extremes).
3
Verify the example
For the coextensive pair 'human' and 'rational', the contradictories become 'Everything not human is not rational' and 'Everything not rational is not human'. These are true without requiring that 'not-human' or 'not-rational' have actual instances.
The author states: 'الموجبة السالبة الطرفين لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع'.
4
Second solution: Restrict to non-universal terms
Alternatively, restrict the discussion to coextensive terms that are not absolutely universal. Then their contradictories will have subjects (either external or mental), and the standard proof works.
The author says: 'تخص البحث بما إذا لم يكن المتساويان شاملين لجميع الأشياء'.
5
Evaluate the defense
The author defends the restriction by noting that logic is a tool for sciences that do not use propositions with contradictories of universal terms. Therefore, excluding them does no harm and avoids needless complications.
He adds that including such terms would disturb other logical rules like the enumeration of relations.
