مباحث الكلى والجزئي
والسر في ذلك أنّ الإيجاب يستلزم وجود المحكوم عليه، ضرورة أن ثبوت مفهوم وجودي أو علمي لشيء يستلزم وجود ذلك الشيء، بخلاف السلب. فإن قلت : إذاكان الموضوع موجوداً فالسالبة المعدولة والموجبة المحصلة متلازمان - كماسيأتي - والحال فيما نحن فيه كذلك، لأن «اللا إنسان» صادق على موجودات محققة كالفرس وغيره. قلت : ذلك لا يجديك نفعاً، إذ ليس الكلام في خصوص هذا المثال، بل في نقيضي المتساويين مطلقاً، فإذا لم يصدق نقيضاهما على شيء أصلاً فهناك لايتم البرهان قطعاً كنقيضي «الشيء» و «الممكن العام» فإن «الشيء» و «الممكن العام» لما وجب صدقهما على كل مفهوم بحسب الأمر امتنع صدق «اللاشيء» و«اللاممكن» بحسبها على مفهوم من المفهومات ، فإذا قلت : «لولم يصدق «كـلّ لاشيء لاممكن» لصدق نقيضه، وهو بعض اللاشيء ليس بلا ممكن فيكون بعض اللاشيء ممكناه لاتجه المنع المذكور.
فإن قلت مفهوم «الممكن نقيض لمفهوم اللاممكن» فإذا لم يصدق أحدهما على شيء وجب أن يصدق عليه الآخر، وإلا لارتفع النقيضان معاً .
بديهة - فإن أورد عليه المنع كان مكابرة غير مسموعة. قلت : هذان المفهومان متناقضان إذا اعتبرا في أنفسهما هكذا منفردين من غير اعتبار صدقهما على شيء، وأما إذا اعتبر صدقهما على شيء حصل هناك قضيتان موجبتان : إحداهما معدولة والأخرى محصلة، كقولك «زيد ممكن» و «زيد لا ممكن» ولا تناقض بينهما، لأن نقيض صدق الممكن على شيء سلب صدقه عليه، لا صدق سلبه عليه، ولاشك أن المتساويين اعتبر صدقهما على شيء، إذ مرجع التساوي إلى موجبتين كليتين وأطراف القضايا اعتبر فيها الصدق على ذات الموضوع. فإذا قلت : «كلّ إنسان ناطق و كل ناطق إنسان» فقد اعتبرت صدقهما على أفرادهما، وكذلك إذا قلت : كل لا إنسان لا ناطق» فقد اعتبرت صدق
ونقيض الأعم من شيء مطلقاً أخص من نقيض الأخص مطلقاً، أي يصدق نقيض الأخص على كلّ ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم، وليس كلّ ما صدق عليه نقيض الأخص يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم.
«اللاناطق» على ذات «اللاإنسان» فإذا اخذت نقيضه بهذا الاعتبار كان هو سلب صدق اللاناطق عليه، وهو معنى قولنا بعض اللا إنسان ليس بلاناطق» لا صدق «الناطق» عليه، لأن الناطق» نقيض «اللا ناطق» في حالة الإفراد من غير اعتبار الصدق على شيء، لا في حالة اعتبار صدقه عليه ؛ فقد اشتبه عليك نقيضه باعتبار الصدق بنقيضه لا باعتبار الصدق، فوضعت أحدهما مكان الآخر، فالمنع متجه بلا مكابرة.
والمخلص أن يقال : إنا نأخذ نقيضي المتساويين باعتبار الصدق على شيء، فيكون نقيضاهما سلبيين هكذا : كلّ ما ليس بإنسان فهو ليس بناطق» و «كـلّ ماليس بناطق فهو ليس بإنسان فيحصل قضيتان موجبتان سالبنا الطرفين، والموجبة السالبة الطرفين لا تقتضى وجود الموضوع ، بخلاف المعدولة الطرفين - وقد حقق ذلك في موضعه - ولنا أيضاً أن تخص البحث بما إذا لم يكن المتساويان شاملين لجميع الأشياء ذهناً وخارجاً ، فإن نقيضيهما حينئذ يصدقان على موجود - إما خارجي أو ذهني - فيتم البرهان بلا اشتباه.
لا يقال: يلزم تخصيص القواعد. لأنا نقول : تعميمها إنما هو بحسب المقاصد ، وليس لنا زيادة غرض في معرفة أحوال نقائض الأمور العامة، إذ ليس في العلوم الحكمية قضية موضوعها أو محمولها نقيض الأمور الشاملة، وهذا الفنّ آلة لتلك العلوم، فلابأس بإخراجها عن قواعده ؛ بل اعتبارها يوجب اختلالا في حصر النسب كما مرّ، وفي تساوى نقيض المتساويين كما ذكرنا آنفا، وفي كون نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم - إلى غير ذلك وإصلاح هذا الاختلال يوجب تكلفات بعيدة (شريف).
Topics of the universal and the particular. And the secret in that is that affirmation necessitates the existence of the subject of judgment, necessarily because the establishment of an existential or conceptual attribute for something necessitates the existence of that thing, unlike negation. If you say: 'If the subject exists, then the modified negative and the unmodified affirmative are coextensive — as will come — and the case in what we are discussing is likewise, because 'not-human' is true of actual existents like horse and others.' I say: 'That does not benefit you, for the discussion is not about this particular example, but about the two contradictories of coextensive terms absolutely, so if their two contradictories are not true of anything at all, then the proof is definitely incomplete, like the two contradictories of 'thing' and 'general possible'. For since 'thing' and 'general possible' must be true of every concept according to the matter, it is impossible for 'not-thing' and 'not-possible' to be true of any concept. So if you say: 'If it were not that 'every not-thing is not-possible' is true, its contradictory would be true, namely 'some not-thing is not not-possible' so that some not-thing is possible, then the mentioned objection would not apply.' If you say: 'The concept of 'possible' is the contradictory of the concept of 'not-possible'. So if one of them is not true of something, the other must be true of it, otherwise both contradictories would be absent together — self-evident. If an objection is raised against that, it would be outrageous sophistry not to be heard.' I say: 'These two concepts are contradictory when considered in themselves, thus separate, without consideration of their truth about something. But when their truth about something is considered, there result two affirmative propositions: one modified and the other unmodified, like your saying 'Zayd is possible' and 'Zayd is not possible'. There is no contradiction between them, because the contradictory of the truth of 'possible' about something is the negation of its truth about it, not the truth of its negation about it. And there is no doubt that coextensive terms are considered with respect to their truth about something, for the reference of coextension is to two universal affirmatives, and in the terms of propositions, truth is considered with respect to the essence of the subject. So when you say: 'Every human is rational and every rational is human', you have considered their truth about their individuals, and likewise when you say: 'Every not-human is not-rational', you have considered the truth of 'not-rational' about the essence of 'not-human'. And the contradictory of the more general of something absolutely is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific absolutely, i.e., the contradictory of the more specific is true of everything of which the contradictory of the more general is true, but not everything of which the contradictory of the more specific is true is the contradictory of the more general true of it. So: 'not-rational' about the essence of 'not-human'. If you take its contradictory with this consideration, it is the negation of the truth of 'not-rational' about it, which is the meaning of our saying 'some not-human is not not-rational', not the truth of 'rational' about it, because 'rational' is the contradictory of 'not-rational' in the state of isolation without consideration of truth about something, not in the state of considering its truth about it. So you have confused its contradictory in terms of truth with its contradictory without consideration of truth, and you have put one in place of the other, so the objection is valid without sophistry. And the deliverance is to say: we take the two contradictories of the coextensive terms with consideration of truth about something, so their two contradictories are two negatives thus: 'Everything that is not human is not rational' and 'Everything that is not rational is not human', resulting in two affirmative propositions negative in both extremes. And the affirmative proposition negative in both extremes does not require the existence of the subject, unlike the one modified in both extremes — and that has been verified in its place. And we also have that we can restrict the discussion to when the coextensive terms are not inclusive of all things mentally and externally, for then their two contradictories are true of an existent — either external or mental — so the proof is completed without confusion. It cannot be said: 'This entails a restriction of the rules.' For we say: 'Their generalization is only according to the purposes, and we have no further goal in knowing the states of the contradictories of universal matters, for there is no proposition in the philosophical sciences whose subject or predicate is a contradictory of universal matters, and this art is a tool for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding them from its rules. Rather, considering them necessitates a disturbance in the enumeration of relations as has passed, and in the equality of the contradictory of the coextensive as we mentioned, and in the fact that the contradictory of the more specific is more general than the contradictory of the more general — and so on. And rectifying this disturbance necessitates far-fetched difficulties (Sharif).'

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis within the commentary on Shamsiyya. The author addresses a subtle problem regarding the rule that the contradictories of coextensive terms are themselves coextensive. The discussion begins by explaining why affirmation requires the existence of the subject: if one affirms a concept of something, that thing must exist. Negation does not have this requirement. The opponent objects by pointing out that if the subject exists, the modified negative and unmodified affirmative are coextensive, as in the example of 'not-human' which applies to real existents like horses. The author replies that this example is not the issue; the issue is when the coextensive terms are absolutely universal, like 'thing' and 'general possible', which are true of every concept. In that case, their contradictories ('not-thing' and 'not-possible') have no subject at all, so the standard proof (burhan) that the contradictories are also coextensive fails. The author then presents an objection from the opponent: if two concepts are contradictories, if one is not true of something, the other must be true; otherwise both would be absent, which is impossible. The author answers by distinguishing between considering concepts in themselves (abstractly) and considering their truth about something. When we consider them as related to something, we have two affirmative propositions (one modified, one unmodified) like 'Zayd is possible' and 'Zayd is not possible', which are not contradictory because the contradictory of 'possible' is the negation of its truth, not the truth of its negation. Since coextension is about truth of subjects, the contradictories must be taken as negative propositions (e.g., 'Everything that is not human is not rational') which are affirmative in form but have negative subject and predicate. Such propositions do not require actual existence of the subject. The author also offers a second solution: restrict the discussion to coextensive terms that are not absolutely universal, so their contradictories will have a subject. Finally, the author defends this restriction by noting that logic is a tool for philosophical sciences, and no philosophical proposition involves the contradictories of universal terms, so excluding them does no harm, while including them creates difficulties.

Key Terms
سالبة الطرفين
متساويان
نقيض

The rule of coextensive contradictories is preserved by using propositions with negative terms that avoid the existence requirement, or by restricting the rule to non-universal coextensive terms.

Immediate Inference

Negation of More General is More Specific: The Rule
Author presents the rule and clarifies a confusion about truth-negation vs. term-negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that the negation of a more general term is more specific than the negation of a more specific term, and identify the confusion between term-negation and truth-negation.

Core Idea
The negation flips the hierarchy: the negation of the general is more specific.

This lesson introduces a key rule in immediate inference: when you negate a more general term like 'animal', the result ('non-animal') is more specific than the negation of a more specific term like 'human' ('non-human'). This may seem counterintuitive, so we will walk through the reasoning and address a common confusion about negating concepts vs. negating truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general and specific terms
Pick a pair of terms where one is more general than the other, e.g., 'animal' (a'am) and 'human' (akhas). 'Animal' includes all animals, 'human' only humans.
2
Form their negations
Add 'la' (non-) to each: 'non-animal' (la hayawan) and 'non-human' (la insan).
In Arabic, the negation is often expressed as a single word with 'la'.
3
Compare extensions of the negations
Determine which set is larger: 'non-human' includes all non-humans, including animals, so it is more extensive. 'Non-animal' excludes animals, so it is narrower. Hence, the negation of the more general (animal) is more specific (akhas).
The rule states: naqeed al-a'am akhas min naqeed al-akhas.
4
Understand the confusion about truth
The passage warns against confusing the negation of a concept itself (e.g., 'non-speaking' as a term) with the negation of its truth on a subject. For example, the negation of 'non-speaking' as a concept is 'speaking', but the negation of its truth is 'not being non-speaking' i.e., 'speaking' in a different sense. This mix-up can lead to false objections.
5
Apply the remedy for coextensive terms
If two terms are coextensive (e.g., 'human' and 'rational animal'), their negations can be handled by considering truth-negation: 'Everything that is not human is not rational' and vice versa. These propositions are true but do not require the existence of subjects.
This preserves the rule without existential commitment.

The negation of the more general with respect to anything absolutely is more specific than the negation of the more specific absolutely, i.e., the negation of the more specific applies to everything to which the negation of the more general applies, but not everything to which the negation of the more specific applies does the negation of the more general apply. 'The non-speaking' upon the essence of 'the non-human'. If you take its negation in this regard, it is the denial of the truth of 'non-speaking' upon it, which is the meaning of our saying 'some non-human is not non-speaking' – not the truth of 'speaking' upon it, because 'speaking' is the negation of 'non-speaking' in the state of isolation without considering truth upon something, not in the state of considering its truth upon it. So you confused its negation with respect to truth with its negation not with respect to truth, and you placed one in the place of the other. Therefore the prevention is directed without stubbornness. The remedy is to say: We take the two negatives of two equal terms with respect to truth upon something, so their two negatives are negative thus: 'Everything that is not human is not speaking' and 'Everything that is not speaking is not human', giving two affirmative propositions with negative terms on both sides. The affirmative with both sides negative does not require the existence of the subject, unlike the modifed with both sides negative – and that has been verified in its place. And we have also that we specify the inquiry to when the two equal terms are not inclusive of all things mentally and externally, for then their two negatives apply to something existent – either external or mental – so the proof is completed without confusion. It cannot be said: This entails specialization of the rules. Because we say: Their generalization is only relative to the purposes, and we have no further aim in knowing the states of the negations of universal things, since in the philosophical sciences there is no proposition whose subject or predicate is the negation of universal things, and this art is a tool for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding them from its rules; rather, considering them causes confusion in the enumeration of relations as already mentioned, and in the equality of the two negatives of equal terms as we just mentioned, and in the negation of the more specific being more general than the negation of the more general – and so on. And rectifying this confusion requires far-fetched efforts (Sharif). For example, you say: 'Every non-animal is non-human' applies, otherwise some non-animal would be human, so some human is non-animal – this is a contradiction. As for the second, because if our saying 'Not everything that the negation of the more specific applies to does the negation of the more general apply' were not true, then the negation of the more general would apply to everything to which the negation of the more specific applies, so the very specific would apply to every general – by reversal of the negation – which is impossible. Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical discussion on the relationship between the negations of general and specific terms. It begins by stating the rule: The negation of a more general term (e.g., 'non-animal') is more specific than the negation of a more specific term (e.g., 'non-human'). In other words, the set of things that are 'non-human' is narrower than the set of things that are 'non-animal'? Actually, the rule says the opposite: the negation of the more general (la hayawan) is more specific (akhas) than the negation of the more specific (la insan). But logically, 'non-animal' excludes more things than 'non-human', so 'non-animal' is actually more specific? Wait: 'non-animal' applies to everything that is not an animal, which includes stones, plants, etc., while 'non-human' applies to everything that is not human, which includes animals, stones, etc. So 'non-human' is actually broader than 'non-animal' because 'non-human' includes all non-humans including animals, whereas 'non-animal' excludes animals. So 'non-animal' is more specific (less extension) than 'non-human'. The rule states that the negation of the general (a'am) is more specific (akhas) than the negation of the specific (akhas). So if we take a general term like 'animal' and a specific term like 'human', then 'non-animal' is more specific than 'non-human' because 'non-animal' applies to fewer things (everything except animals) while 'non-human' applies to more things (everything except humans). Yes. So the rule is: For any two terms where one is more general than the other, the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. The author then clarifies: This means that the negation of the more specific (e.g., 'non-human') applies to everything that the negation of the more general (e.g., 'non-animal') applies to, but not vice versa. So every non-animal is non-human? Is that true? Every non-animal includes plants, minerals, etc., which are indeed not human. Yes, that's true. But not every non-human is non-animal, because some non-humans (e.g., dogs) are animals. So the rule holds. The author then addresses a potential confusion: Suppose we take the example of 'non-speaking' (la natik) and 'non-human' (la insan). 'Non-speaking' is a wider term? Actually, 'non-speaking' includes many things that are not human, like animals. But the author discusses taking the negation of 'non-speaking' with respect to truth on something. He explains that if we consider the negation of 'non-speaking' as the denial of its truth, then it becomes 'speaking'. But the original rule is about negations considered as terms themselves, not about the truth of negations. There is a confusion between the negation of a concept in itself and the negation of its truth. The remedy is to consider the two negatives of equal terms (mutasawiyan) with respect to truth on something, yielding negative propositions with negative terms on both sides, which do not require the existence of the subject. Alternatively, we can restrict the discussion to terms that are not all-encompassing (like 'thing' or 'existent'), so that their negations will have actual subjects. The author then addresses an objection: This would specialize the rules, but replies that such specialization is acceptable because the logical art is a tool for philosophical sciences, and universal terms like 'thing' are rarely used as subjects or predicates in those sciences. Moreover, including all-encompassing terms would cause confusion in various logical principles. The passage then provides proofs (أما الأول وأما الثاني) for the two parts of the rule, using reduction to absurdity. For the first part, if the negation of the specific did not apply to everything the negation of the general applies to, then the specific itself would apply to some of that, which would entail the specific without the general, impossible. For the second part, if the negation of the general applied to everything the negation of the specific applied to, then the specific would apply to every general, also impossible. Finally, there is a further discussion about an objection concerning empty terms like 'non-thing', and the reply reiterates the distinction between the negation of a concept in itself and its negation with respect to truth, referring back to the earlier remedy.

Immediate Inference
advanced
10 min
Proving the Rule and Handling Objections
Author provides proofs for the rule and addresses objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the rule that the negation of the more general is more specific using reductio ad absurdum, and resolve objections concerning coextensive and all-encompassing terms.

Core Idea
The rule is proven by contradiction and survives objections through truth-negation or domain restriction.

While the rule seems intuitive, it requires rigorous proof, and objections arise when terms are coextensive or all-encompassing. This lesson presents the author's proofs and the remedies that safeguard the rule, including the crucial distinction between negating a concept and negating its truth.

Guided Path
1
State the two parts of the rule
Part 1: The negation of the more specific applies to everything the negation of the more general applies to. Part 2: But not everything the negation of the more specific applies to, does the negation of the more general apply.
These are the two halves of the rule that need proof.
2
Prove Part 1 by contradiction
Assume the negation of the more specific does NOT apply to everything the negation of the more general applies to. Then the specific itself would apply to some of those things, implying the specific without the general, which is impossible. Example: if 'every non-animal is non-human' is false, then some non-animal is human, so some human is non-animal – contradiction.
This uses the principle that the specific cannot exist without the general.
3
Prove Part 2 by contradiction
Assume the negation of the more general does apply to everything the negation of the more specific applies to. Then the specific would apply to every general, which is impossible. Example: if 'every non-human is non-animal' were true, then every human would be an animal? Actually, the contrapositive: if the negation of the general applies to all that the negation of the specific applies, then the specific applies to all the general, which is false.
The text uses the phrase 'by reversal of the negation' (bi-aks al-naqeed).
4
Address the objection of all-encompassing terms
If terms like 'thing' (shay') are used, their negations have no subjects (e.g., 'non-thing' refers to nothing), so the proofs fail because they rely on existential import. The author argues we can restrict the domain to terms that are not all-encompassing, since such terms are rarely used in philosophical sciences.
This specialization of the rules is justified by the purpose of logic as a tool for sciences.
5
Reiterate the remedy for coextensive terms
For coextensive terms, use truth-negation: 'Everything not human is not rational' and 'Everything not rational is not human'. These are true and do not require existential import, preserving the rule.
This avoids the objection that coextensive terms would make their negatives equal.

(۱ يرد عليه الاعتراض المورد على نقيضي المتساويين - كما أشرنا إليه. فإذا قلت : «لولم يصدق كل لاشيء لا إنسان» لصدق «بعض اللاشيء ليس بلا إنسان فيلزم صدق بعض اللاشيء إنسان»: أتجه أن يقال : السالبة المعدولة المحمول أعم من الموجبة المحصلة المحمول، فلا تستلزمها - كما مر . وإن تمسكت بأن «الإنسان» مثلاً نقيض «اللاإنسان» فإذا لم يصدق أحدهما على شيء صدق الآخر عليه، وإلا ارتفع النقيضان. ردّ بما عرفته من أن نقيض مفهوم في نفسه يغاير نقيضه باعتبار صدقه والمخلص مامر - فتأمل (شريف).
The negation of the more general with respect to anything absolutely is more specific than the negation of the more specific absolutely, i.e., the negation of the more specific applies to everything to which the negation of the more general applies, but not everything to which the negation of the more specific applies does the negation of the more general apply. 'The non-speaking' upon the essence of 'the non-human'. If you take its negation in this regard, it is the denial of the truth of 'non-speaking' upon it, which is the meaning of our saying 'some non-human is not non-speaking' – not the truth of 'speaking' upon it, because 'speaking' is the negation of 'non-speaking' in the state of isolation without considering truth upon something, not in the state of considering its truth upon it. So you confused its negation with respect to truth with its negation not with respect to truth, and you placed one in the place of the other. Therefore the prevention is directed without stubbornness. The remedy is to say: We take the two negatives of two equal terms with respect to truth upon something, so their two negatives are negative thus: 'Everything that is not human is not speaking' and 'Everything that is not speaking is not human', giving two affirmative propositions with negative terms on both sides. The affirmative with both sides negative does not require the existence of the subject, unlike the modifed with both sides negative – and that has been verified in its place. And we have also that we specify the inquiry to when the two equal terms are not inclusive of all things mentally and externally, for then their two negatives apply to something existent – either external or mental – so the proof is completed without confusion. It cannot be said: This entails specialization of the rules. Because we say: Their generalization is only relative to the purposes, and we have no further aim in knowing the states of the negations of universal things, since in the philosophical sciences there is no proposition whose subject or predicate is the negation of universal things, and this art is a tool for those sciences, so there is no harm in excluding them from its rules; rather, considering them causes confusion in the enumeration of relations as already mentioned, and in the equality of the two negatives of equal terms as we just mentioned, and in the negation of the more specific being more general than the negation of the more general – and so on. And rectifying this confusion requires far-fetched efforts (Sharif). Topics of the Universal and the Particular 179 As for the first, because if the negation of the more specific did not apply to everything to which the negation of the more general applies, then the very specific would apply to some of what the negation of the more general applies to, so the specific would apply without the general, which is impossible. For example, you say: 'Every non-animal is non-human' applies, otherwise some non-animal would be human, so some human is non-animal – this is a contradiction. As for the second, because if our saying 'Not everything that the negation of the more specific applies to does the negation of the more general apply' were not true, then the negation of the more general would apply to everything to which the negation of the more specific applies, so the very specific would apply to every general – by reversal of the negation – which is impossible. So not every non-human is non-animal, otherwise (2) (1) The objection raised against the two negatives of equal terms applies to it – as we pointed out. If you say: 'If not every non-thing were non-human', then 'some non-thing is not non-human' follows, so 'some non-thing is human' follows: It is understood that the negative modified in predicate is more general than the affirmative simple in predicate, so it does not entail it – as previously. And if you insist that 'human' for example is the negation of 'non-human', so if one of them does not apply to something, the other applies to it, otherwise the two contradictories are removed, it is refuted by what you have learned: that the negation of a concept in itself is different from its negation with respect to its truth, and the remedy is as before – so ponder (Sharif).

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical discussion on the relationship between the negations of general and specific terms. It begins by stating the rule: The negation of a more general term (e.g., 'non-animal') is more specific than the negation of a more specific term (e.g., 'non-human'). In other words, the set of things that are 'non-human' is narrower than the set of things that are 'non-animal'? Actually, the rule says the opposite: the negation of the more general (la hayawan) is more specific (akhas) than the negation of the more specific (la insan). But logically, 'non-animal' excludes more things than 'non-human', so 'non-animal' is actually more specific? Wait: 'non-animal' applies to everything that is not an animal, which includes stones, plants, etc., while 'non-human' applies to everything that is not human, which includes animals, stones, etc. So 'non-human' is actually broader than 'non-animal' because 'non-human' includes all non-humans including animals, whereas 'non-animal' excludes animals. So 'non-animal' is more specific (less extension) than 'non-human'. The rule states that the negation of the general (a'am) is more specific (akhas) than the negation of the specific (akhas). So if we take a general term like 'animal' and a specific term like 'human', then 'non-animal' is more specific than 'non-human' because 'non-animal' applies to fewer things (everything except animals) while 'non-human' applies to more things (everything except humans). Yes. So the rule is: For any two terms where one is more general than the other, the negation of the more general is more specific than the negation of the more specific. The author then clarifies: This means that the negation of the more specific (e.g., 'non-human') applies to everything that the negation of the more general (e.g., 'non-animal') applies to, but not vice versa. So every non-animal is non-human? Is that true? Every non-animal includes plants, minerals, etc., which are indeed not human. Yes, that's true. But not every non-human is non-animal, because some non-humans (e.g., dogs) are animals. So the rule holds. The author then addresses a potential confusion: Suppose we take the example of 'non-speaking' (la natik) and 'non-human' (la insan). 'Non-speaking' is a wider term? Actually, 'non-speaking' includes many things that are not human, like animals. But the author discusses taking the negation of 'non-speaking' with respect to truth on something. He explains that if we consider the negation of 'non-speaking' as the denial of its truth, then it becomes 'speaking'. But the original rule is about negations considered as terms themselves, not about the truth of negations. There is a confusion between the negation of a concept in itself and the negation of its truth. The remedy is to consider the two negatives of equal terms (mutasawiyan) with respect to truth on something, yielding negative propositions with negative terms on both sides, which do not require the existence of the subject. Alternatively, we can restrict the discussion to terms that are not all-encompassing (like 'thing' or 'existent'), so that their negations will have actual subjects. The author then addresses an objection: This would specialize the rules, but replies that such specialization is acceptable because the logical art is a tool for philosophical sciences, and universal terms like 'thing' are rarely used as subjects or predicates in those sciences. Moreover, including all-encompassing terms would cause confusion in various logical principles. The passage then provides proofs (أما الأول وأما الثاني) for the two parts of the rule, using reduction to absurdity. For the first part, if the negation of the specific did not apply to everything the negation of the general applies to, then the specific itself would apply to some of that, which would entail the specific without the general, impossible. For the second part, if the negation of the general applied to everything the negation of the specific applied to, then the specific would apply to every general, also impossible. Finally, there is a further discussion about an objection concerning empty terms like 'non-thing', and the reply reiterates the distinction between the negation of a concept in itself and its negation with respect to truth, referring back to the earlier remedy.

Proof of Asymmetric Relation between Negation of General and Specific
Author provides multiple proofs and responds to criticisms about the relationship between negations of general and specific terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove that the negation of a general term is not equivalent to the negation of a specific term using contrapositive and reductio arguments.

Core Idea
The negation of a general is more specific than the negation of the specific — they are not interchangeable.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a key principle of immediate inference: the negation of a general term and the negation of a specific term are not convertible. The discussion involves three proofs and a dialectical exchange about the validity of one of the proofs. Understanding this asymmetry is crucial for correctly applying contrapositive conversion in Islamic logic, especially in legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and the terms involved
The claim is that 'not-A' (where A is specific) is not equivalent to 'not-B' (where B is general). For example, 'not-man' is not the same as 'not-animal'. The author gives three proofs to establish this asymmetry.
The Arabic uses 'nāqiḍ al-a'amm' (negation of general) and 'nāqiḍ al-akhass' (negation of specific).
2
Analyze the first proof (contrapositive)
Assume the contrary: every not-man is not-animal. By contrapositive conversion, this yields 'every animal is man', which is clearly false. Therefore, not-man cannot be equivalent to not-animal.
This uses 'aks al-naqid' – conversion by contrapositive.
3
Analyze the second proof (reductio)
If every negation of the specific were also a negation of the general, then the two negations would be equivalent, implying the two referents are equivalent. This is impossible, so the assumption is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Analyze the third proof (partial truth)
The general term (e.g., animal) is true of some negations of the specific (e.g., not-man, such as a horse). So some negation of the specific is not the negation of the general but rather the general itself. Thus they are not equivalent.
This proof appeals to the fact that 'animal' is true of non-human animals.
5
Examine the objection and response
A critic objects that the phrase 'the truth of the negation of the specific over everything to which the negation of the general applies without the converse' is circular. The commentator responds that the conversion is intuitive and the claim is actually a definition, not part of the proof. This dialectical exchange highlights the need for precise formulation in logical arguments.
This shows awareness of the fallacy of begging the question (muṣādara).

لكان كلّ لا إنسان لاحيوان، وينعكس إلى كل حيوان إنسان.
أو نقول أيضاً : قد ثبت أن كل نقيض الأعم نقيض الأخص، فلو كان كل نقيض الأخص نقيض الأعم لكان النقيضان متساويين، فيكون العينان متساويين - هذا خلف.
أو نقول أيضاً : العام صادق على بعض نقيض الأخص تحقيقاً للعموم فليس بعض نقيض الأخص نقيض الأعم بل عينه.
وفي قوله «الصدق نقيض الأخص على كل ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم من غير عكس» تسامح ، لجعل الدعوى جزء من الدليل، وهو مصادرة على المطلوب.
سياتي - فكيف يستدل به على إثبات ما ادعاه ؟ وأيضاً الاستدلال به بيان بما لم يتبين بعد. وأجيب بأن الشارح نظر إلى الواقع - وهو صحة تلك الطريقة - ولم يكتف أيضاً بعكس النقيض في الاستدلال، بل استدل بما يصح التمسك به عند المصنف أيضاً.

(1) أجيب بأن المدعى كون نقيض الأعم مطلقاً أخص مطلقاً من نقيض الأخص، وما جعله جزء من الدليل هو تفسير وتعريف للمدعى – لا عينه – فهو بالحقيقة استدلال بثبوت الحد على ثبوت المحدود وما بعده استدلال على ثبوت الحد. ولا يخفي عليك أن المقصود تفصيل المدعى إلى جزأين ليستدلّ على كل واحد منهما على حدته، فالأولى أن يجعل تفسيراً له، ويقال: «أي يصدق نقيض الأخص على كلّ ما يصدق عليه نقيض الأعم من غير عكس، ففي الكلام تسامح يجعل التفسير بمنزلة جزء الدليل صورة (شريف).
It would be that every not-man is not-animal, and it converts to every animal is man. Or we also say: It has been established that every negation of the general is a negation of the specific. So if every negation of the specific were a negation of the general, then the two negations would be equivalent, and thus the two referents would be equivalent — this is a reductio. Or we also say: The general is true of some negation of the specific, achieving generality, so some negation of the specific is not the negation of the general but rather its very referent. And in his statement 'The truth of the negation of the specific over everything to which the negation of the general applies, without the converse' there is looseness, because he made the claim part of the proof, which is begging the question. It will come — so how can he use it to prove what he claimed? Also, using it as evidence is explaining with what has not yet been clarified. And it is answered: The commentator considered the reality — which is the soundness of that method — and he did not rely solely on contrapositive conversion in the inference, but rather argued with what can be held to [also] according to the author. As for your saying 'This is explaining with what has not yet been clarified,' the answer is that the mentioned conversion is close to human nature; a minimal indication suffices for it. (Sharif). (1) It is answered: The claim is that the negation of the general is absolutely more specific than the negation of the specific, and what he made part of the proof is an interpretation and definition of the claim — not its very self — so it is in reality an inference from the existence of the definition to the existence of the defined, and what follows is an inference for the existence of the definition. And it is not hidden from you that the intention is to detail the claim into two parts in order to infer each one separately, so it is better to make it an interpretation of it, and it is said: 'That is, the negation of the specific applies to everything to which the negation of the general applies without the converse; thus in the statement there is looseness, making the interpretation in the form of part of the proof (Sharif).'
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange concerning the relationship between the negation of a general term and the negation of a specific term. The author first presents proofs that the negation of a general term is not convertible with the negation of a specific term. The first proof uses an example: if every not-man were not-animal, then it would convert to every animal is man, which is false. The second proof is a reductio: if every negation of the specific were a negation of the general, then the two negations would be equivalent, leading to the falsehood that the two referents are equivalent. The third proof observes that the general is true of some negation of the specific, so the negation of the specific is not the same as the negation of the general but is rather the very referent of the general. Then a criticism is raised about a statement that says 'the truth of the negation of the specific over everything to which the negation of the general applies without the converse' — this is considered loose (tasamuh) because it makes the claim part of the proof, which is begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub). The critic asks how one can use this statement to prove the claim when it has not yet been established. The answer is that the commentator (sharih) had the actual reality in mind and did not rely solely on contrapositive conversion; he used what is acceptable to the author. Another objection is that the argument explains with what has not yet been clarified. The response is that the conversion mentioned is intuitive and needs only minimal prompting. The discussion continues with an explanation that the claim is actually that the negation of the general is absolutely more specific than the negation of the specific, and what was presented as part of the proof is merely an interpretation of the claim, not the claim itself. Thus the inference is from the definition to the defined, and then from that to the definition. The intention is to break the claim into two parts and infer each separately.

نقيض الأخص
عكس النقيض

The negation of a general term is more specific than the negation of a specific term, and they are not convertible; this asymmetry is proven by contrapositive, reductio, and partial truth arguments.

Understanding Partial Opposition between Contradictories
Author clarifies partial opposition and critiques the original wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify partial opposition between two terms and between their contradictories, using examples from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Partial opposition means each term can be true without the other—this holds for both the terms and their contradictories.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the nature of partial opposition (mubāyana juz'iyya). He shows that when two terms are mutually exclusive (mutabāyinān), their contradictories are also partially opposed. This is illustrated with examples like 'not-human' and 'not-horse' which both apply to stones. Understanding this relation is essential for analyzing logical relations between terms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify two mutually exclusive terms
Find a pair of terms that cannot both apply to the same thing, e.g., 'human' and 'horse'.
These are called mutabāyinān.
2
Check each term's truth without the other
If each term can be true of something without the other, they are partially opposed.
3
Produce their contradictories
Add 'not-' to each term to get their negations, e.g., 'not-human' and 'not-horse'.
4
Determine if the contradictories are also partially opposed
See if both contradictories can be true of the same thing (like stones) or neither can. The passage says both cases yield partial opposition.
If they both apply, that is partial opposition; if neither applies, that is total opposition, but partial opposition still holds between them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أن النسبة بينهما المباينة الجزئية ، لأن العينين إذا كان كل واحد منهما بحيث يصدق بدون الآخر، كان النقيضان أيضاً كذلك، ولا نعني بالمباينة الجزئية إلا هذا القدر ؛ ونقيضا المتباينين متباينان تبايناً جزئياً، لأنهما إما أن يصدقا معاً على شيء - كاللا إنسان واللافـرس الصادقين على الجماد - أو لا يصدقا - كاللاوجود واللاعدم – فلاشيء مما يصدق عليه اللاوجود» يصدق عليه اللاعـدم» وبالعكس؛ وأيا ما كان يتحقق التباين الجزئي بينهما.
أما إذا لم يصدقا على شيء أصلاً، كان بينهما تباين كلّي، فيتحقق التباين الجزئي بينهما قطعاً.
وأما إذا صدقا على شيء كان بينهما تباين جزئي، لأن كل واحد من المتباينين يصدق مع نقيض الآخر، فيصدق كل واحد من نقيضيهما بدون نقيض الآخر، فالتباين الجزئي لازم جزماً.
وقد ذكر في المتن هاهنا ما لا يحتاج إليه وترك ما يحتاج إليه :
أما الأول فلأن قيد «فقط» بعد قوله «ضرورة صدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر» زائد لاطائل تحته.
·
وأما الثاني فلانه وجب أن يقول «ضرورة صدق كل واحد من المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر»، لأنّ التباين الجزئي بين النقيضين صدق كلّ واحد منهما بدون الآخر، لاصدق واحد منهما بدون الآخر، وليس يلزم من صدق أحد الشيئين مع نقيض الآخر صدق كل واحد من النقيضين بدون الآخر؛ فترك لفظ «كل» ولابد منه.
(1) أجيب عنه بأن معنى كلام المصنف : «إن أحد المتباينين يصدق مع نقيض الآخر فقط» أي لا يصدق مع عين الآخر، فبصدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر ظهر صدق أحد النقيضين بدون النقيض الآخر ؛ وبعدم صدق أحد المتباينين مع عين الآخر ظهر صدق نقيضه مع عين الآخر. فمن مجموع كلام المصنف ظهر صدق كل من نقيضي المتباينين بدون الآخر، فقيد «فقط» لابد منه ؛ وليس معناه أن المباين الآخر لا يصدق مع نقيض الأول، وإلا لكان فاسداً - لا خالياً عن الفائدة فقط - ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا التوجيه - وإن كان دقيقاً مصححاً للمطلوب - إذ حاصله أن قيد «فقط» منضماً إلى ما تقدم يفيد معنى صدق كل من المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر - إلا أن ترك لفظ «كل» مع كونه مفيداً للمعنى المقصود إفادة ظاهرة والعدول إلى إيراد هذا القيد المحوج إلى تدقيق النظر وحمل اللفظ على خلاف المتبادر تكلّف ظاهر لكن الخلل حينئذ متعلق بالعبارة دون المعنى (شريف).
Know that the relation between them is partial opposition, because when each of the two mutually exclusive things is such that it is true without the other, then their contradictories are also likewise. And we mean by partial opposition nothing but this measure. And the contradictories of two mutually exclusive things are mutually exclusive with partial opposition, because they either both apply to something — like 'not-human' and 'not-horse' applying to an inanimate object — or they do not both apply — like 'not-existence' and 'not-nonexistence' — so nothing that 'not-existence' applies to has 'not-nonexistence' applied to it, and vice versa. Whichever it is, partial opposition is realized between them. As for if they do not apply to anything at all, then between them is total opposition, so partial opposition is definitely realized between them. And as for if they apply to something, then between them is partial opposition, because each of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other, so each of their two contradictories is true without the contradictory of the other. Thus partial opposition is necessarily entailed. And the original text mentioned here what is not needed and omitted what is needed. As for the first: because the restriction 'only' after his statement 'necessarily, one of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other' is superfluous with no benefit underneath it. As for the second: because he had to say 'necessarily, each of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other', because partial opposition between the two contradictories is that each of them is true without the other, not that one of them is true without the other. And it does not follow from the truth of one of two things together with the contradictory of the other that each of the two contradictories is true without the other. So he omitted the word 'each', and it is necessary. (1) It was answered that the meaning of the author's statement is: 'One of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other only', that is, it is not true together with the other itself. So by the truth of one of the two mutually exclusive things together with the contradictory of the other, it appears that one of the two contradictories is true without the other contradictory. And by the lack of truth of one of the two mutually exclusive things together with the other itself, it appears that its contradictory is true together with the other itself. So from the total of the author's statement, it appears that each of the contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things is true without the other. So the restriction 'only' is necessary. And it does not mean that the other mutually exclusive thing is not true together with the contradictory of the first; otherwise it would be corrupt — not merely without benefit. And it is not hidden from you that this interpretation — though it is precise and corrects the intended meaning — because its gist is that the restriction 'only' when combined with what preceded yields the meaning that each of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other — yet omitting the word 'each', while being clearly indicative of the intended meaning, and turning instead to introducing this restriction which requires close scrutiny and interpreting the wording contrary to its apparent meaning, is an obvious burden. But then the defect pertains to the expression, not the meaning (Sharif).
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the original text (matn) regarding the relation of partial opposition (tabayun juz'i) between two universals that have a relationship of 'general and specific in some respect' (عموم من وجه). The commentator first clarifies the definition of partial opposition: it is when each of two things can be true without the other. He then applies this to their contradictories (naqidayn). He explains that the contradictories of two mutually exclusive things (mutabayinayn) are also partially opposed. He gives examples: 'not-human' and 'not-horse' both apply to an inanimate object, while 'not-existence' and 'not-nonexistence' apply to nothing. In either case, partial opposition holds. He then notes that if the two contradictories apply to nothing, that is total opposition, but partial opposition still holds between them? Actually he says if they don't apply to anything, there is total opposition, but partial opposition is definitely realized; if they do apply to something, partial opposition holds because each of the mutually exclusive things is true with the other's contradictory. Then the commentator critiques the original text: it included an unnecessary restriction 'only' (فقط) and omitted the crucial word 'each' (كل). The original text said 'necessarily, one of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other', but the commentator argues it should say 'each', because partial opposition requires both contradictories to be true without the other, not just one. The marginal gloss (Sharif) defends the original wording by interpreting 'only' as meaning the mutually exclusive thing is not true with the other itself, so combined with the rest of the text, the intended meaning that each is true with the other's contradictory is understood. However, the commentator still finds this interpretation forced and considers the defect in wording, not meaning.

Key Terms
مباينة جزئية (mubāyana juz'iyya): Partial opposition: a relation between two things where each can be true without the other (they are not necessarily coexclusive). [Note: Stable term across logical works; no significant shift.]
نقيض (naqīḍ): Contradictory: a proposition or term that is the negation of another, such that they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. [Note: No significant shift.]
متباينان (mutabāyinān): Two mutually exclusive things: terms that are exclusive (e.g., human and horse) but not necessarily exhaustive. [Note: In some texts, mutabayin may refer to contraries; here it is used for terms that are simply different and not overlapping in extension.]
تباين كلي (tabāyun kullī): Total opposition: when two terms do not apply to anything (impossible), leading to their contradictories being universal in a sense. [Note: Less common; some texts treat it as a subtype of partial opposition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of two contradictories that both apply to something?
A) 'not-human' and 'not-horse'
B) 'human' and 'horse'
C) 'not-existence' and 'not-nonexistence'
D) 'existence' and 'nonexistence'

Key Takeaway
Partial opposition is the relation where each of two things can be true without the other; this applies to both mutually exclusive terms and their contradictories.

Critique of the Matn: The Superfluous 'Only' and the Omitted 'Each'
Author clarifies partial opposition and critiques the original wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a logical definition is incomplete or unnecessarily verbose, using the critique of the matn as a model.

Core Idea
Precision in logical wording is essential: a missing word can change the entire logical relation.

In this part of the passage, the commentator critiques the original text (matn) for including an unnecessary restriction 'only' and omitting the crucial word 'each'. He argues that the definition of partial opposition requires both contradictories to be true without the other, not just one. The marginal gloss by Sharif defends the original wording, but the commentator finds the interpretation forced. This exchange highlights the importance of precise language in logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original statement in the matn
Find the phrase that the commentator is critiquing: 'ضرورة صدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر فقط' (necessarily, one of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other only).
The 'only' (فقط) is considered superfluous.
2
Understand the commentator's objection
The commentator says the word 'each' (كل) is missing. He argues that partial opposition requires each of the contradictories to be true without the other, not just one. The original word 'one' (أحد) is insufficient.
3
Examine the Sharif's defense
Sharif interprets 'only' as meaning that the mutually exclusive thing is not true together with the other itself. Combined with the rest of the text, this implies both conditions, so the word 'each' is not needed.
Sharif says the criticism is about expression, not meaning.
4
Evaluate the logical necessity
Determine whether the original wording can be salvaged. The commentator concedes that the meaning can be understood with careful interpretation, but the wording is less clear than it could be.
The final verdict: defect in expression, not meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أن النسبة بينهما المباينة الجزئية ، لأن العينين إذا كان كل واحد منهما بحيث يصدق بدون الآخر، كان النقيضان أيضاً كذلك، ولا نعني بالمباينة الجزئية إلا هذا القدر ؛ ونقيضا المتباينين متباينان تبايناً جزئياً، لأنهما إما أن يصدقا معاً على شيء - كاللا إنسان واللافـرس الصادقين على الجماد - أو لا يصدقا - كاللاوجود واللاعدم – فلاشيء مما يصدق عليه اللاوجود» يصدق عليه اللاعـدم» وبالعكس؛ وأيا ما كان يتحقق التباين الجزئي بينهما.
أما إذا لم يصدقا على شيء أصلاً، كان بينهما تباين كلّي، فيتحقق التباين الجزئي بينهما قطعاً.
وأما إذا صدقا على شيء كان بينهما تباين جزئي، لأن كل واحد من المتباينين يصدق مع نقيض الآخر، فيصدق كل واحد من نقيضيهما بدون نقيض الآخر، فالتباين الجزئي لازم جزماً.
وقد ذكر في المتن هاهنا ما لا يحتاج إليه وترك ما يحتاج إليه :
أما الأول فلأن قيد «فقط» بعد قوله «ضرورة صدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر» زائد لاطائل تحته.
·
وأما الثاني فلانه وجب أن يقول «ضرورة صدق كل واحد من المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر»، لأنّ التباين الجزئي بين النقيضين صدق كلّ واحد منهما بدون الآخر، لاصدق واحد منهما بدون الآخر، وليس يلزم من صدق أحد الشيئين مع نقيض الآخر صدق كل واحد من النقيضين بدون الآخر؛ فترك لفظ «كل» ولابد منه.
(1) أجيب عنه بأن معنى كلام المصنف : «إن أحد المتباينين يصدق مع نقيض الآخر فقط» أي لا يصدق مع عين الآخر، فبصدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر ظهر صدق أحد النقيضين بدون النقيض الآخر ؛ وبعدم صدق أحد المتباينين مع عين الآخر ظهر صدق نقيضه مع عين الآخر. فمن مجموع كلام المصنف ظهر صدق كل من نقيضي المتباينين بدون الآخر، فقيد «فقط» لابد منه ؛ وليس معناه أن المباين الآخر لا يصدق مع نقيض الأول، وإلا لكان فاسداً - لا خالياً عن الفائدة فقط - ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا التوجيه - وإن كان دقيقاً مصححاً للمطلوب - إذ حاصله أن قيد «فقط» منضماً إلى ما تقدم يفيد معنى صدق كل من المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر - إلا أن ترك لفظ «كل» مع كونه مفيداً للمعنى المقصود إفادة ظاهرة والعدول إلى إيراد هذا القيد المحوج إلى تدقيق النظر وحمل اللفظ على خلاف المتبادر تكلّف ظاهر لكن الخلل حينئذ متعلق بالعبارة دون المعنى (شريف).
Know that the relation between them is partial opposition, because when each of the two mutually exclusive things is such that it is true without the other, then their contradictories are also likewise. And we mean by partial opposition nothing but this measure. And the contradictories of two mutually exclusive things are mutually exclusive with partial opposition, because they either both apply to something — like 'not-human' and 'not-horse' applying to an inanimate object — or they do not both apply — like 'not-existence' and 'not-nonexistence' — so nothing that 'not-existence' applies to has 'not-nonexistence' applied to it, and vice versa. Whichever it is, partial opposition is realized between them. As for if they do not apply to anything at all, then between them is total opposition, so partial opposition is definitely realized between them. And as for if they apply to something, then between them is partial opposition, because each of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other, so each of their two contradictories is true without the contradictory of the other. Thus partial opposition is necessarily entailed. And the original text mentioned here what is not needed and omitted what is needed. As for the first: because the restriction 'only' after his statement 'necessarily, one of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other' is superfluous with no benefit underneath it. As for the second: because he had to say 'necessarily, each of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other', because partial opposition between the two contradictories is that each of them is true without the other, not that one of them is true without the other. And it does not follow from the truth of one of two things together with the contradictory of the other that each of the two contradictories is true without the other. So he omitted the word 'each', and it is necessary. (1) It was answered that the meaning of the author's statement is: 'One of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other only', that is, it is not true together with the other itself. So by the truth of one of the two mutually exclusive things together with the contradictory of the other, it appears that one of the two contradictories is true without the other contradictory. And by the lack of truth of one of the two mutually exclusive things together with the other itself, it appears that its contradictory is true together with the other itself. So from the total of the author's statement, it appears that each of the contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things is true without the other. So the restriction 'only' is necessary. And it does not mean that the other mutually exclusive thing is not true together with the contradictory of the first; otherwise it would be corrupt — not merely without benefit. And it is not hidden from you that this interpretation — though it is precise and corrects the intended meaning — because its gist is that the restriction 'only' when combined with what preceded yields the meaning that each of the two mutually exclusive things is true together with the contradictory of the other — yet omitting the word 'each', while being clearly indicative of the intended meaning, and turning instead to introducing this restriction which requires close scrutiny and interpreting the wording contrary to its apparent meaning, is an obvious burden. But then the defect pertains to the expression, not the meaning (Sharif).

Critique and Defense of Wording in Partial Contrast Definition
Critique of and response to the author's omission of 'كل' and inclusion of 'فقط'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the necessity of each word in a logical definition by analyzing a critique and its defense.

Core Idea
Every word in a logical definition must be justified; even 'only' can be crucial.

In logical definitions, every term matters. A single missing or extra word can change the meaning. This lesson examines a real debate over the wording of partial contrast (التباين الجزئي): does the word 'only' (فقط) add anything, and should 'each' (كل) be included? You will learn how to dissect such critiques and defenses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's wording
Locate the exact phrasing the author used: 'one of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other only'. Note the words 'one' and 'only'.
2
Analyze the critic's objection
The critic claims two issues: (1) 'only' is redundant because the meaning is already captured; (2) 'each' (كل) is missing because partial contrast requires each contradictory to be true without the other, not just one.
Identify which part of the definition is attacked as superfluous and which as deficient.
3
Understand the defense
The defender (Sharif) reinterprets 'only' to mean 'not with the other itself'. Combining this with the statement that one is true with the contradictory, we infer that each contradictory is true without the other. Thus 'only' is necessary.
See how the defender links the two clauses to derive the missing meaning.
4
Evaluate the defense's soundness
Check if the defense fully addresses the critic: does it logically entail 'each' without saying it explicitly? The defender admits the wording is forced but argues the meaning is correct. Decide if the flaw is in expression or in logic.
Consider whether a clearer definition would include 'كل'.
5
Apply the lesson to other definitions
Think of a definition in an Islamic science (e.g., Usul al-Fiqh) and test whether each term is necessary. This skill of critiquing definitions is used widely.
For example, the definition of qiyas: 'إلحاق فرع بأصل في حكم لعلة جامعة' — is 'جامعة' needed?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر في المتن هاهنا ما لا يحتاج إليه وترك ما يحتاج إليه :
أما الأول فلأن قيد «فقط» بعد قوله «ضرورة صدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر» زائد لاطائل تحته.
·
وأما الثاني فلانه وجب أن يقول «ضرورة صدق كل واحد من المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر»، لأنّ التباين الجزئي بين النقيضين صدق كلّ واحد منهما بدون الآخر ، لاصدق واحد منهما بدون الآخر، وليس يلزم من صدق أحد الشيئين مع نقيض الآخر صدق كل واحد من النقيضين بدون الآخر؛ فترك لفظ «كل» ولابد منه.
(1) أجيب عنه بأن معنى كلام المصنف : «إن أحد المتباينين يصدق مع نقيض الآخر فقط» أي لا يصدق مع عين الآخر، فبصدق أحد المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر ظهر صدق أحد النقيضين بدون النقيض الآخر ؛ وبعدم صدق أحد المتباينين مع عين الآخر ظهر صدق نقيضه مع عين الآخر. فمن مجموع كلام المصنف ظهر صدق كل من نقيضي المتباينين بدون الآخر، فقيد «فقط» لابد منه ؛ وليس معناه أن المباين الآخر لا يصدق مع نقيض الأول، وإلا لكان فاسداً - لا خالياً عن الفائدة فقط - ولا يخفى عليك أن هذا التوجيه - وإن كان دقيقاً مصححاً للمطلوب - إذ حاصله أن قيد «فقط» منضماً إلى ما تقدم يفيد معنى صدق كل من المتباينين مع نقيض الآخر - إلا أن ترك لفظ «كل» مع كونه مفيداً للمعنى المقصود إفادة ظاهرة والعدول إلى إيراد هذا القيد المحوج إلى تدقيق النظر وحمل اللفظ على خلاف المتبادر تكلّف ظاهر لكن الخلل حينئذ متعلق بالعبارة دون المعنى (شريف).
And he mentioned in the text here what is not needed and left out what is needed:
As for the first, because the restriction 'only' after his saying 'necessarily one of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other' is redundant and useless.
·
As for the second, because he should have said 'necessarily each one of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other', because partial contrast between the two contradictories entails that each one of them is true without the other, not that one of them is true without the other. And it is not necessitated from the truth of one of two things with the contradictory of the other that each one of the two contradictories is true without the other; so he omitted the word 'each', but it is indispensable.
(1) It is answered: That the meaning of the author's statement is: 'One of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other only' i.e., it is not true with the other itself. So by the truth of one of the two mutually exclusive things with the contradictory of the other, the truth of one of the two contradictories without the other contradictory is shown; and by the falsity of one of the two mutually exclusive things with the other itself, the truth of its contradictory with the other itself is shown. So from the totality of the author's statement, the truth of each of the contradictories of the two mutually exclusive things without the other is shown. Thus the restriction 'only' is indispensable; and it does not mean that the other mutually exclusive thing is not true with the contradictory of the first, otherwise it would be invalid - not merely useless. And it is not hidden from you that this explanation - though precise and corrective of the intended - since its gist is that the restriction 'only' combined with what precedes yields the meaning of the truth of each of the two mutually exclusive things with the contradictory of the other - except that omitting the word 'each' while it obviously conveys the intended meaning, and instead introducing this restriction requiring careful consideration and interpreting the word contrary to what is immediately understood, is an apparent affectation. Nevertheless, the flaw then pertains to the expression, not the meaning. (Sharif)
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of the author's definition of partial contrast (التباين الجزئي). The critic points out two issues: first, the inclusion of 'only' (فقط) is redundant; second, the author omitted 'كل' (each) and should have said 'each one of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other' instead of 'one'. The critic argues that partial contrast between contradictories means each contradictory is true without the other, not just one. The answer defends the author, explaining that 'only' means the thing is not true with the other itself. By combining the two clauses (one with the contradictory, the other not with the other), it follows that each contradictory is true without the other. Thus 'only' is necessary. The defender admits the wording is somewhat forced but insists the meaning is correct.

Key Terms
التباين الجزئي
نقيض

A) It is redundant and should be removed.
B) It indicates that one of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other and not with the other itself.
C) It means that the other mutually exclusive thing is false with the contradictory of the first.
D) It emphasizes that the relation is total contrast.

Key Takeaway
Precision in logical definitions often requires careful justification of each word, and apparent redundancies may be essential when combined with other elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Partial Contrast Between Contradictories of Mutually Exclusive Things
Demonstration that the contradictories of partially contrasting things are themselves partially contrasted, and rebuttal of an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the contradictories of two terms are partially contrasted and explain what 'partial contrast stripped of particularities' means.

Core Idea
The contradictories of partially contrasting things are themselves partially contrasted, but not in a fixed way.

This passage tackles a subtle logical point: when two terms are mutually exclusive in a partial way (e.g., horse and human, or animal and white), what is the relation between their contradictories? The answer is not simply total contrast or generality/specificity, but a more nuanced partial contrast that varies depending on the types. Let's unpack this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that the claim 'contradictories of two mutually exclusive things are partially contrasted' is established by the premise that each of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other.
2
Understand the meaning of 'partial contrast stripped of particularities'
The author clarifies that 'partial contrast' here means stripped of the particularity of total contrast or generality/specificity. That is, it is not that the contradictories are always totally contrasted or always in a generality/specificity relation; rather, they may appear as either in different cases.
3
Examine the examples
The passage gives examples: horse and human (total contrast) and animal and white (generality/specificity). In both cases, the contradictories exhibit partial contrast, but under different forms.
4
Follow the objection and response
An objector notes that the author showed that contradictories of things related by generality/specificity may be totally contrasted in some cases (e.g., non-animal and non-white). The response argues that this actually supports the claim because it shows the relation is partial contrast stripped of particularities: sometimes total, sometimes generality/specificity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت تعلم أن الدعوى تثبت بمجرد المقدمة القائلة «كل واحد من
(1)
أجيب عن ذلك بأن معنى قولهم «نقيضا المتباينين متباينان تبايناً جزئياً» أن النسبة بين هذين النقيضين هي التباين الجزئي مجرداً عن خصوصية كل واحد من فرديه - أعني التباين الكلي والعموم من وجه - إذ لو كان التباين الجزئي بينهما في جميع الصور في ضمن إحدى الخصوصيّتين كالتباين الكلي مثلا لكان النسبة بينهما هي تلك الخصوصية، إذ لا يقال أن النسبة بين الفرس والانسان أو بين الحيوان والأبيض هي التباين الجزئي، مع ثبوته هناك قطعاً. بل يقال : إن النسبة بين الأولين هي التباين الكلي، وبين الأخيرين هي العموم من وجه، ويعلم من ذلك ثبوت التباين الجزئي في الموضعين؛ ولاشك أن المدعى بهذا المعنى لا يتم إلابان يبين أن نقيضي المتباينين قد لا يتصادقان أصلاً وقد يتصادقان، فلايكون التباين الجزئي بينهما مقيداً بخصوص التباين الكلي في جميع الصور، ولا بخصوص العموم من وجه في جمعيها، بل يثبت في بعضها في ضمن المباينة الكلية، وفي بعضها في ضمن العموم من وجه ؛ فالنسبة بين نقيضي المتباينين هي التباين الجزئي مجرداً عن خصوصية كل واحد من فرديه وهو المطلوب. وهذا الكلام لاشبهة فيه.
قيل : إن المصنف بيّن أن نقيضي الأمرين اللذين بينهما عموم من وجه قديتباينان في بعض الصور تبايناً كلّيّاً، وظاهر أن بينهما قد يكون عموم من وجه - كاللاحيوان واللا أبيض - فإذا ضمّ ذلك إلى ماذكره في نقيضي المتباينين من صدق عين كلّ واحد منهما مع نقيض الآخر فإنه جار فيهما أيضاً – ظهر أن النسبة بينهما التباين الجزئي مجرداً عن خصوصية كل من فرديه. أو نقول : نفى أولاً أن يكون النسبة بينهما هي العموم من وجه، لأن الوهم يتبادر إلى أن النسبة بين النقيضين هي العموم من وجه أيضاً، فبالغ في نفيه حيث ضم إليه نفي العموم مطلقاً، ولم يتعرّض للنسبة بينهما هناك، لأنها تعلم مما ذكر في نقيضي المتباينين بعينه، لأن نقيضيهما إن لم يتصادقا على شيء
And you know that the claim is established by the mere premise saying 'Each one of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other.'
(1) It is answered: That the meaning of their statement 'The contradictories of two mutually exclusive things are partially contrasted' is that the relation between these two contradictories is partial contrast stripped of the particularity of each of its two individuals - i.e., total contrast and generality/specificity - for if the partial contrast between them in all forms were within one of the two particularities, such as total contrast, then the relation between them would be that particularity, since it is not said that the relation between 'horse' and 'human' or between 'animal' and 'white' is partial contrast, while it certainly holds there. Rather, it is said that the relation between the first two is total contrast, and between the last two is generality/specificity, and from that the existence of partial contrast in both places is known. And undoubtedly the claim in this sense is not completed unless it is shown that the contradictories of two mutually exclusive things may not coincide at all or may coincide, so that the partial contrast between them is not restricted to the particularity of total contrast in all forms, nor to the particularity of generality/specificity in all forms, but rather it holds in some forms within total contrast, and in some forms within generality/specificity. Thus the relation between the contradictories of two mutually exclusive things is partial contrast stripped of the particularity of each of its two individuals, and that is what is desired. And this statement has no ambiguity.
It was said: The author showed that the contradictories of two things that have a generality/specificity relation may be totally contrasted in some forms, and it is apparent that between them there may be generality/specificity - like 'non-animal' and 'non-white' - so if that is added to what he mentioned about the contradictories of two mutually exclusive things that each of them is true with the contradictory of the other, and that also applies to them, it becomes clear that the relation between them is partial contrast stripped of the particularity of each of its two individuals. Or we say: He first negated that the relation between them is generality/specificity, because the mind immediately assumes that the relation between contradictories is also generality/specificity, so he emphasized the negation by adding absolute negation of generality, and he did not address the relation between them there, because it is known from what was mentioned about the contradictories of mutually exclusive things themselves, because their contradictories, if they do not coincide on anything
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage explains that the claim that the contradictories of two mutually exclusive (partially contrasting) things are themselves partially contrasted is proven by the premise that each of the two mutually exclusive things is true with the contradictory of the other. The answer clarifies that 'partial contrast' here means stripped of specific types: it is neither total contrast nor generality/specificity in all cases, but rather in some cases it manifests as total contrast and in others as generality/specificity. This is illustrated with examples: 'horse' and 'human' have total contrast, while 'animal' and 'white' have generality/specificity, yet in both cases partial contrast holds. The objection (قيل) notes that the author showed that contradictories of things related by generality/specificity may be totally contrasted in some cases (like 'non-animal' and 'non-white' which have generality/specificity). The response argues that this actually supports the claim: the relation between them is partial contrast stripped of particularities, because sometimes they are totally contrasted and sometimes they share generality/specificity.

العموم من وجه

The contradictories of mutually exclusive things are partially contrasted, but the type of contrast varies: it can be total contrast or generality/specificity depending on the case, and not just one fixed type.
