Implication of 'Not Every' to Partial Negation
Author proves that 'ليس كل' entails partial negation by implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how 'ليس كل' (not every) entails a partial negative proposition by implication, and defend this entailment against the objection that the general does not imply the particular.

Core Idea
The phrase 'not every' does not explicitly state partial negation, but necessarily implies it as a logical concomitant.

This lesson examines a subtle but crucial logical inference: the relationship between the universal negative phrase 'ليس كل' (not every) and the partial negative proposition (السلب الجزئي). The author argues that while 'ليس كل' does not directly assert partial negation, it necessarily implies it by way of implication (بالالتزام). We will dissect the argument, the objection, and the resolution to understand how this immediate inference works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key propositional forms
Recognize that 'ليس كل' corresponds to the denial of a universal affirmative proposition. In the passage, the author refers to 'رفع الإيجاب الكلي' (removal of universal affirmation). This is the starting point.
2
Enumerate the logical possibilities when universal affirmation is false
The author states that when universal affirmation is removed, either the predicate is negated from all individuals (universal negation) or from some individuals and affirmed of others. Both cases are covered.
3
Check for a common element in all possibilities
Notice that in both cases, partial negation (negation from some) holds true. In universal negation, it's true that some are not (since all are not, some are not). In the mixed case, some are not directly. Thus partial negation is true in both scenarios.
4
Understand the objection against implication
The objector claims that 'not every' is more general than both universal and partial negation, so it cannot indicate partial negation specifically by implication, because the general does not indicate the particular.
5
Apply the author's resolution
The author refines the definition: 'not every' is not more general than partial negation alone, but rather more general than two divisions: universal negation and negation of some with affirmation of some. Partial negation is common to both divisions, hence it is a necessary concomitant of 'not every'. The author also provides a proof by contradiction to seal the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنه دالّ على السلب الجزئي بالالتزام : فلأنه إذا ارتفع الإيجاب الكلّيّ، فإما أن يكون المحمول مسلوباً عن كل واحد واحد وهو السلب الكلي، أو يكون مسلوباً عن البعض ثابتاً للبعض، وعلى كلا التقديرين يصدق السلب الجزئي جزماً؛ فالسلب الجزئي من ضروريات مفهوم «ليس كل» - أي رفع الإيجاب الكلّي - ومن لوازمه، فتكون دلالته عليه بالالتزام.
لا يقال : مفهوم «ليس كل وهو رفع الإيجاب الكلي - أعم من السلب عن الكل - أي السلب الكلّي - والسلب عن البعض - أي السلب الجزئي - فلايكون دالا على السلب الجزئي بالالتزام، لأن العام لادلالة له على الخاص بإحدى الدلالات الثلاث.
لانا نقول : رفع الإيجاب الكلّي ليس أعم من السلب الجزئي، بل أعم من السلب عن الكل والسلب عن البعض مع الإيجاب للبعض. والسلب الجزئي هو السلب عن البعض سواء كان مع الإيجاب للبعض الآخر أو لا يكون، فهو مشترك بين ذلك القسم وبين السلب الكلي، فيكون لازماً لهما، وإذا انحصر العام في القسمين وكل منهما يكون ملزوماً لأمر، كان ذلك الأمر اللازم لازماً للعام أيضاً، فيكون السلب الجزئي لازماً لمفهوم رفع الإيجاب الكلي.
و بعبارة أخرى «ليس كلّ» يلزمه السلب الجزئي، فإنه متى ارتفع الإيجاب الكلّي صدق السلب عن البعض، لأنه لولم يكن المحمول مسلوباً عن شيء من الأفراد لكان ثابتاً للكلّ، والمقدر خلافه - هذا خلف.
And as for its indicating partial negation by implication: because when universal affirmation is removed, then either the predicate is negated from every single one, which is universal negation, or it is negated from some and affirmed of some, and in both cases partial negation is necessarily true; so partial negation is among the necessities of the concept of 'not every' – i.e., the removal of universal affirmation – and among its concomitants, so its indication of it is by implication.
It should not be said: The concept of 'not every' – which is the removal of universal affirmation – is more general than negation from all, i.e., universal negation, and negation from some, i.e., partial negation; so it does not indicate partial negation by implication, because the general does not indicate the particular by any of the three modes of indication.
But we say: The removal of universal affirmation is not more general than partial negation; rather it is more general than negation from all and negation from some along with affirmation of some. And partial negation is negation from some whether or not it is accompanied by affirmation of the other [part], so it is common between that division and universal negation, so it is a concomitant of both. And when the general is confined to two divisions and each of them is a necessary condition for something, that necessary thing is also a necessary concomitant of the general; so partial negation is a necessary concomitant of the concept of the removal of universal affirmation.
And in other words, 'not every' necessarily entails partial negation, because whenever universal affirmation is removed, negation from some is true; for if the predicate were not negated from any individuals, it would be affirmed of all, but the opposite is assumed – this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the immediate inference from the phrase 'ليس كل' (not every) to partial negation, i.e., 'some are not'. The author, a commentator on the Sham'iyya (a treatise on logic), aims to prove that 'ليس كل' necessarily implies partial negation by way of implication (al-dalala bi al-iltizam), not by explicit statement.
The argument proceeds: If the universal affirmative (e.g., 'every A is B') is false, then either the predicate is negated from all individuals (universal negative) or from some individuals and affirmed of others. In both cases, a partial negation – that some individuals are not B – holds true. Thus, partial negation is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the concept of 'not every'.
The passage then presents an objection: The concept of 'not every' (removing universal affirmation) is more general than both universal negation and partial negation, so it cannot indicate the specific (partial negation) by implication, because the general does not necessarily indicate its particular instances.
The author answers by refining the definition: 'Not every' is not more general than partial negation per se, but rather it is more general than two scenarios: (a) negation from all (universal negation) and (b) negation from some together with affirmation of the remaining some. Partial negation (negation from some) is common to both scenarios – it is present in both universal negation (since universal negation implies partial negation) and in the mixed scenario. Hence, partial negation is a necessary concomitant of both divisions, and therefore of the general concept 'not every' as well.
Finally, a concise proof by contradiction: If 'not every' were true (i.e., universal affirmative false) yet partial negation false, then no individual would have the predicate negated, meaning all individuals would have it affirmed – contradicting the assumed falsehood of the universal affirmative. Thus, partial negation must be true.

Key Terms
السلب الجزئي
الإيجاب الكلّي
السلب الكلي
دلالة بالالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'ليس كل فقيه عالم' (Not every faqih is a scholar), what partial negation is necessarily implied?
A) Some fugaha are not scholars.
B) No faqih is a scholar.
C) All fugaha are scholars.
D) Some fugaha are scholars.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ليس كل' (not every) necessarily implies partial negation, as argued by the immediate inference from the denial of the universal affirmative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Task 1: Consider the statement 'ليس كل حديث صحيح' (Not every hadith is authentic). Using the method from this lesson, explain what partial negation is necessarily implied and why this inference is by implication (dalala bi al-iltizam) and not explicit.

Direct and Implied Meanings of 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس'
Author explains direct and implied indications of 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' for partial negation and universal affirmation removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the direct and implied logical indications of 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس', and identify when 'ليس بعض' can be interpreted as universal negation.

Core Idea
Word order and negation scope determine whether 'not some' means partial or total denial.

This passage from al-Qutbi examines two negative quantified expressions and their logical behavior. We learn that 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' both directly indicate partial negation, but they differ in whether they can imply universal negation. The hashia adds a crucial refinement: the scope of negation matters—negating the subject-predicate relation vs. negating the proposition itself changes the logical meaning. This lesson will give you the tools to analyze such expressions in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two expressions
Notice the two phrases 'ليس بعض' (not some) and 'بعض ليس' (some not) in the Arabic. They appear in examples like 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان' and 'بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان'.
2
Understand direct indication of partial negation
The explicit meaning of both phrases is that some individuals of the subject are denied the predicate. This is partial negation (salb juz'i) because 'some' is explicitly mentioned and the negation particle is applied to it.
Direct indication (bi al-mutabaqa) means the word's explicit sense.
3
Understand implied removal of universal affirmation
If the predicate is negated from some individuals, it cannot be affirmed of all. Therefore, the universal affirmative statement is false. This implication (bi al-iltizam) is necessary.
The implication is from partial negative to false universal affirmative: from 'some not' to 'not every'.
4
Analyze the difference between the two phrases
In 'ليس بعض', the word 'بعض' is in the scope of negation (it follows the negation). In Arabic, an indefinite noun in a negative context can convey universality. So 'ليس بعض' can be interpreted as universal negation ('no animal is human'). In contrast, 'بعض ليس' has 'بعض' before the negation, so it remains particular and cannot imply universality.
This is a key distinction based on word order and the rule of nakira fi siyaq al-nafi.
5
Apply the hashia's refinement on scope
The hashia clarifies that 'ليس بعض' is ambiguous. If the negation particle negates the predicate from the subject (i.e., the statement is about 'some'), it is partial negation. But if the negation negates the whole proposition (i.e., the claim that 'some animal is human' is false), then it implies universal negation because negating a partial affirmative yields its contradictory universal negative.
This nuance applies also to 'ليس كل', as the commentator shows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأماّ أن «ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس» يدلان على السلب الجزئي بالمطابقة فظاهر، لأنا إذا قلنا بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان» أو «ليس بعض الحيوان إنساناً يكون مفهومه الصريح سلب الإنسان عن بعض أفراد الحيوان للتصريح بالبعض وإدخال حرف السلب عليه وهو السلب الجزئي وأما أنهما يدلان على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالالتزام، فلأن المحمول إذا كان مسلوباً عن بعض الأفراد لا يكون ثابتاً لكل الأفراد، فيكون الإيجاب الكلّي مرتفعاً؛ هذا هو الفرق بين «ليس كلّ» وبين الأخيرين.
وأما الفرق بين الأخيرين فهو أن ليس بعض» قد يذكر للسلب الكلي، لأن البعض غير معين ؛ فإن تعين بعض الأفراد خارج عــن مفهوم الجزئية، فأشبه النكرة في سياق النفي؛ فكما أن النكرة في سياق النفي تفيد العموم كذلك هاهنا أيضاً؛ لأنه احتمل أن يفهم منه السلب في أي بعض كان، وهو السلب الكلّي؛ بخلاف «بعض ليس» فإن البعض هاهنا وإن كان أيضاً غير معيّن، إلا أنه ليس واقعاً في سياق النفي، بل
(۱) هذا كلام ظاهري، والتحقيق فيه أنك إذا قلت : ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان» فإن أردت بحرف السلب سلب المحمول عن الموضوع : كان سلباً جزئياً، وإن أردت به سلب القضية على معنى أنها ليست بمتحققة في نفس الأمر : كان سلباً كلياً - لأن سلب الإيجاب الجزئي يستلزم السلب الكلي - فعلى هذا «ليس كلّ يحتمل أن يكون سلباً كلياً بأن يقصد بحرف السلب سلب المحمول عن الموضوع المذكور " وهو كل واحد واحد - وأن يكون سلباً جزئياً بأن يقصد به سلب القضية كما حققه الشارح في الشرح حيث بين أن «ليس كل» تدل على رفع الإيجاب.
And as for that 'not some' and 'some not' indicate partial negation by correspondence, it is apparent: because when we say 'some animal is not a human' or 'not some animal is a human', its explicit meaning is the negation of human from some individuals of animal, due to the explicit mention of 'some' and entering the particle of negation upon it, which is partial negation. And as for that they both indicate the removal of universal affirmation by implication: because if the predicate is negated from some individuals, it is not affirmed of all individuals, so the universal affirmation is removed; this is the difference between 'not every' and the latter two.
And as for the difference between the latter two: that 'not some' may be used for universal negation, because 'some' is indefinite; for the specification of some individuals is outside the concept of partiality, so it resembles the indefinite in the context of negation; for just as the indefinite in the context of negation conveys generality, likewise here: because it is possible to understand from it negation in any 'some' that may be, which is universal negation; unlike 'some not', for here 'some' although also indefinite, yet it does not occur in the context of negation, but rather
(1) This is a superficial statement, and the investigation is that when you say 'not some animal is a human', if you intend by the particle of negation the negation of the predicate from the subject, it is a partial negation; and if you intend by it the negation of the proposition in the sense that it is not actually true, it is a universal negation – because the negation of a partial affirmative entails the universal negative. Accordingly, 'not every' can be a universal negation if one intends by the negation particle the negation of the predicate from the mentioned subject, which is every single one; and can be a partial negation if one intends by it the negation of the proposition, as the commentator verified in the commentary where he showed that 'not every' indicates the removal of affirmation.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage continues the analysis of negative quantified expressions, now focusing on 'ليس بعض' (not some) and 'بعض ليس' (some not). The author first establishes that both expressions directly (bi al-mutabaqa) indicate partial negation. For example, saying 'بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان' (some animal is not a human) or 'ليس بعض الحيوان إنساناً' (not some animal is a human) explicitly negates humanity from some animals. Additionally, both imply the removal of universal affirmation (i.e., the falsity of 'every animal is human'), because if the predicate is negated from some, it cannot be true of all. This is the key difference from 'ليس كل' (not every), which indicates partial negation only by implication, not directly.
The author then differentiates between 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس'. 'ليس بعض' can be used to express universal negation (i.e., 'no animal is human') because the word 'بعض' (some) is indefinite and when placed in the scope of negation (like the indefinite in Arabic in a negative context, e.g., 'ما جاءني رجل' meaning 'no man came'), it can convey generality. Thus, 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان' might be understood as 'no animal is human', i.e., universal negation. In contrast, in 'بعض ليس', the word 'بعض' is not in the scope of negation (it comes before the negation), so it remains particular and cannot imply universality.
The marginal note (hashia) from Sharif clarifies a deeper point: When you say 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان', if you take the negation particle as negating the predicate from the subject (i.e., 'not some animal is human' = 'some animals are not human'), it is a partial negation. But if you take the negation as negating the entire proposition (i.e., 'the proposition 'some animal is human' is not true'), then it implies universal negation because the negation of a partial affirmative entails its contradictory, which is a universal negative. This note refines the previous apparent statement by showing an ambiguity in 'ليس بعض' depending on the scope of negation.

Key Terms
بالمطابقة
بالالتزام
نكرة في سياق النفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only partial negation: some students are not diligent.
B) Only universal negation: no student is diligent.
C) It could be partial or universal negation depending on the intended scope of negation.
D) It means all students are diligent.

Key Takeaway
The logical meaning of negative quantified expressions depends not only on the quantifier but also on the syntactic position of the negation particle and the intended scope, leading to ambiguity between partial and universal negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Distinguishing 'Not Some' from 'Some Not' in Categorical Propositions
Author compares two negated quantifier phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical implications of 'ليس بعض' and 'بعض ليس' in categorical propositions and identify when each indicates particular vs universal negation or privative affirmation.

Core Idea
The position of negation changes the scope: 'ليس بعض' can negate universally, while 'بعض ليس' remains particular.

In classical logic, the order of words matters—especially when negation meets quantification. This passage unpacks the subtle but crucial difference between 'ليس بعض' (not some) and 'بعض ليس' (some not). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting legal and theological arguments that rely on negated propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the direct implication (mutabaqa)
Both phrases explicitly indicate particular negation: they negate the predicate from some individuals of the subject. For example, 'ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان' explicitly denies humanity of some animals.
This is the primary meaning by direct implication.
2
Recognize the secondary implication (iltizam)
If the predicate is denied for some, it cannot be affirmed for all. Therefore, both phrases imply the removal of universal affirmation (رفع الإيجاب الكلي).
This distinguishes these phrases from 'ليس كل' which directly negates the universal.
3
Contrast the two phrases: universal negation in 'ليس بعض'
'ليس بعض' can be interpreted as universal negation because 'بعض' is indefinite and appears in the context of negation—similar to how indefinite nouns in negative contexts convey generality. Thus, 'ليس بعض' may negate for every individual.
This ambiguity does not apply to 'بعض ليس' because negation follows the quantifier.
4
Note the privative affirmation reading for 'بعض ليس'
'بعض ليس' can also be used for privative affirmation (إيجاب عدولي), where the negation is about affirming a privative predicate (e.g., 'some animal is non-human') rather than simply negating humanity.
This reading is impossible for 'ليس بعض' because negation precedes the subject.
5
Apply the analysis to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'ليس بعض الفقراء مستحق للزكاة' (Not some poor are deserving of zakat). According to the passage, this could mean either particular negation (some poor are not deserving) or universal negation (no poor are deserving), depending on the intended scope of negation. A jurist must be aware of this ambiguity.
Similarly, 'بعض الفقراء ليس مستحقا للزكاة' (Some poor are not deserving) is strictly particular and could also be read as privative affirmation: 'some poor are non-deserving'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن «ليس بعض» و «بعض ليس» يدلان على السلب الجزئي بالمطابقة فظاهر، لأنا إذا قلنا بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان» أو «ليس بعض الحيوان إنساناً يكون مفهومه الصريح سلب الإنسان عن بعض أفراد الحيوان للتصريح بالبعض وإدخال حرف السلب عليه وهو السلب الجزئي وأما أنهما يدلان على رفع الإيجاب الكلي بالالتزام، فلأن المحمول إذا كان مسلوباً عن بعض الأفراد لا يكون ثابتاً لكل الأفراد، فيكون الإيجاب الكلّي مرتفعاً؛ هذا هو الفرق بين «ليس كلّ» وبين الأخيرين. وأما الفرق بين الأخيرين فهو أن ليس بعض» قد يذكر للسلب الكلي، لأن البعض غير معين ؛ فإن تعين بعض الأفراد خارج عــن مفهوم الجزئية، فأشبه النكرة في سياق النفي؛ فكما أن النكرة في سياق النفي تفيد العموم كذلك هاهنا أيضاً؛ لأنه احتمل أن يفهم منه السلب في أي بعض كان، وهو السلب الكلّي؛ بخلاف «بعض ليس» فإن البعض هاهنا وإن كان أيضاً غير معيّن، إلا أنه ليس واقعاً في سياق النفي، بل السلب إنما هو وارد عليه، و بعض ليس قديذكر للإيجاب العدولي حتى إذا قيل : بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان أريد إثبات اللا إنسانية لبعض الحيوان - لا سلب الإنسانية ع عنه - وفرق مابينهما كما ستقف عليه - بخلاف «ليس بعض » إذ لا يمكن تصوّر الإيجاب مع تقدم حرف السلبـ على الموضوع. (۱) هذا كلام ظاهري، والتحقيق فيه أنك إذا قلت : ليس بعض الحيوان بإنسان» فإن أردت بحرف السلب سلب المحمول عن الموضوع : كان سلباً جزئياً، وإن أردت به سلب القضية على معنى أنها ليست بمتحققة في نفس الأمر : كان سلباً كلياً - لأن سلب الإيجاب الجزئي يستلزم السلب الكلي - فعلى هذا «ليس كلّ يحتمل أن يكون سلباً كلياً بأن يقصد بحرف السلب سلب المحمول عن الموضوع المذكور " وهو كل واحد واحد - وأن يكون سلباً جزئياً بأن يقصد به سلب القضية كما حققه الشارح في الشرح حيث بين أن «ليس كل» تدل على رفع الإيجاب (شريف).
As for the fact that 'not some' and 'some not' indicate particular negation by direct implication, it is evident, because if we say 'some animal is not human' or 'not some animal is human', its explicit meaning is the negation of human from some individuals of animal due to mentioning 'some' and entering the particle of negation upon it, which is particular negation. As for that both indicate the removal of universal affirmation by implication, it is because when the predicate is negated from some individuals, it is not established for all individuals, so the universal affirmation is removed. This is the difference between 'not every' and the two latter. As for the difference between the two latter, it is that 'not some' may be mentioned for universal negation because 'some' is indeterminate. Indeed, the determination of some individuals is outside the concept of particularity, so it resembles an indefinite noun in the context of negation. Just as an indefinite noun in the context of negation conveys generality, so too here; because it is probable that negation is understood from it for any 'some', which is universal negation. Unlike 'some not', for here 'some' is also indeterminate, but it is not situated in the context of negation; rather, negation is only entered upon it. And 'some not' may be mentioned for privative affirmation, such that if one says 'some animal is not human', one intends to establish non-humanity for some animal—not to negate humanity from it—and the difference between them will be understood. Unlike 'not some', for it is impossible to conceive affirmation with the particle of negation preceding the subject. (1) This is an apparent statement, and the precise investigation in it is that if you say 'not some animal is human', if you intend by the negation particle the negation of the predicate from the subject, it is a particular negation; and if you intend by it the negation of the proposition in the sense that it is not realized in reality, it is a universal negation—because the negation of a particular affirmation entails universal negation. So on this, 'not every' can be a universal negation by intending with the negation particle the negation of the predicate from the mentioned subject (which is every individual), and can be a particular negation by intending with it the negation of the proposition, as the commentator verified in the commentary where he explained that 'not every' indicates the removal of affirmation (Sharif).
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expository analysis of two negated quantifier phrases: 'ليس بعض' (not some) and 'بعض ليس' (some not). The author explains their logical implications and differences. First, he states that both phrases straightforwardly indicate particular negation (salb juz'i) by direct implication (mutabaqa). The example 'some animal is not human' or 'not some animal is human' explicitly negates humanity from some animals. Second, he points out that both also imply the removal of universal affirmation (raf' al-ijab al-kulli) by secondary implication (ilitzam): if the predicate is negated from some individuals, it cannot be true for all. This distinguishes 'ليس كل' (not every) from the two phrases. Third, he contrasts the two phrases: 'ليس بعض' can be used for universal negation because 'بعض' (some) is indefinite; it resembles an indefinite noun in a negation context, which often conveys generality. Thus, 'ليس بعض' may be interpreted as negating for any individual, i.e., universal negation. In contrast, 'بعض ليس' cannot imply universal negation because the negation particle follows 'بعض', so it remains particular. Additionally, 'بعض ليس' can be used for privative affirmation (ijab 'aduli), e.g., 'some animal is not human' meaning 'some animal is non-human' (asserting a privative property). This is not possible in 'ليس بعض' because negation precedes the subject. The footnote by Sharif clarifies that the interpretation depends on intention: if the negation particle negates the predicate from the subject, it is particular; if it negates the entire proposition (i.e., asserting the proposition is false), it is universal. Thus, 'ليس كل' can also be ambiguous. The key takeaway is that 'ليس بعض' is ambiguous between particular and universal negation, while 'بعض ليس' is strictly particular and can have a privative reading.

Key Terms
ليس بعض
بعض ليس
سلب جزئي
سلب كلي
إيجاب عدولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both phrases always indicate universal negation.
B) 'ليس بعض' can be interpreted as universal negation, while 'بعض ليس' is always particular.
C) 'بعض ليس' can imply universal negation if the subject is indefinite.
D) Both phrases are identical in meaning and usage.

Key Takeaway
The position of the negation particle relative to the quantifier determines whether a negated proposition can be interpreted as universal or particular; 'ليس بعض' is ambiguous, while 'بعض ليس' is strictly particular and can also serve as privative affirmation.

5 min
The Indefinite Proposition and Its Equivalence to the Particular
Proof of equivalence between indefinite and particular, plus note on singular vs natural

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the equivalence between indefinite and particular propositions in logical inference and distinguish the singular from the natural proposition in syllogistic contexts.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition is logically equivalent to a particular, not a universal.

This passage clarifies a common confusion: indefinite statements (e.g., 'Man is in loss') are not universal but are equivalent to particular statements ('Some man is in loss'). The author provides a proof of mutual entailment and then notes how singular propositions function like universals in the first figure, unlike natural propositions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct syllogistic reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proposition types
Locate the definitions: indefinite (المهملة), particular (الجزئية), and singular (الشخصية). Note that indefinite lacks a quantifier, particular uses 'some', and singular refers to a specific individual.
The natural proposition (طبيعية) is also mentioned but not defined here.
2
Understand the equivalence argument
Follow the proof: if an indefinite is true, it applies to all individuals; that implies it applies to some individuals (particular). Conversely, if particular is true, it applies to some individuals, so it applies absolutely (indefinite). Thus they are mutually entailing.
This is an immediate inference: indefinite ↔ particular.
3
Apply the equivalence
Given an indefinite proposition like 'الإنسان في خسر' (Man is in loss), derive the particular 'بعض الإنسان في خسر' (Some man is in loss). Test with other examples from Islamic sciences.
4
Compare singular and natural propositions in syllogisms
The singular proposition can serve as a universal major premise in the first figure (e.g., 'هذا زيد وزيد حيوان' yields 'هذا حيوان'). The natural proposition does not (e.g., 'زيد إنسان والإنسان نوع' does not yield 'زيد نوع'). Understand why: singular is concrete, natural is abstract.
This shows the practical difference in syllogistic validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وهي في قوة الجزئية، لأنه متى صدق الإنسان في خسر» صدق بعض الإنسان في خسر» و بالعكس. أقول : المهملة في قوة الجزئية، بمعنى أنهما متلازمان، فإنه متى صدقت المهملة صدقت الجزئية ،وبالعكس فإذا صدق قولنا : «الإنسان في خسر صدق بعض الإنسان في خسر وبالعكس. أما أنه كلما صدقت المهملة صدقت الجزئية : فلأن الحكم فيها على أفراد الموضوع، ومتى صدق الحكم على أفراد الموضوع، فإما أن يصدق ذلك الحكم على جميع الأفراد أو على بعضها، وعلى كلا التقديرين يصدق الحكم على بعض الأفراد وهو الجزئي. وأما بالعكس : فلأنه متى صدق الحكم على بعض الأفراد صدق الحكم على الأفراد مطلقاً وهو المهملة. وأيضاً : الشخصية قد تقوم في الظاهر مقام الكلية، فتنتج من كبرى الشكل الأول، نحو هذا زيد وزيد حيوان فهذا حيوان بخلاف الطبيعية، فإنها لا تنتج في كبرى الشكل الأول كقولنا : زيد إنسان والإنسان نوع» مع أنه لا يصدق «زید نوع».
He said: The indefinite is equivalent to the particular, because whenever 'Man is in loss' is true, 'Some man is in loss' is true, and vice versa. I say: The indefinite is equivalent to the particular, meaning they are mutually entailing. For whenever the indefinite is true, the particular is true, and vice versa. So if our statement 'Man is in loss' is true, 'Some man is in loss' is true, and vice versa. As for whenever the indefinite is true the particular is true: that is because the judgment in it applies to the individuals of the subject, and whenever the judgment applies to the individuals of the subject, either it applies to all individuals or to some of them; in both cases the judgment applies to some individuals, which is the particular. As for the converse: because whenever the judgment applies to some individuals, the judgment applies to the individuals absolutely, which is the indefinite. Also: the singular proposition sometimes stands in place of the universal in appearance, so it yields a conclusion from the major premise of the first figure, like 'This is Zayd and Zayd is an animal, therefore this is an animal', unlike the natural proposition, for it does not yield a conclusion in the major premise of the first figure, like our saying 'Zayd is a human and the human is a species', while 'Zayd is a species' is not true.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage discusses the indefinite proposition (المهملة) and its logical equivalence to the particular proposition (الجزئية). The commentator first restates the claim from the matn that the indefinite is equivalent to the particular, meaning they are mutually entailing: whenever the indefinite is true, the particular is true, and vice versa. He proves the first direction: if the indefinite is true, the judgment applies to all individuals of the subject; then either it applies to all or some, so in both cases it holds for some individuals, which is the particular. The converse: if the particular is true (judgment applies to some individuals), then it applies to individuals absolutely (i.e., without restriction), which is the indefinite. Then he adds a note about the singular proposition (الشخصية): in some contexts, it functions like a universal in the first figure's major premise, producing a valid conclusion (e.g., 'This is Zayd, and Zayd is an animal, therefore this is an animal'). But the natural proposition (طبيعية) does not work that way, as in 'Zayd is a human, and the human is a species' does not yield 'Zayd is a species'. This illustrates the difference between singular and natural propositions in syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
المهملة
الجزئية
الشخصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Every man has a spirit.
C) No man has a spirit.
D) Some man does not have a spirit.

Key Takeaway
The indefinite and particular propositions are mutually entailing; singular propositions can act as universal in first figure, unlike natural propositions.

The Error of Restricting 'Law Wujida' to Necessary Connection
Author criticizes interpreting 'law wujida' as necessary connection and notes scribal error

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish necessary (luzum) from coincidental (ittifaq) connection in categorical propositions and critique the interpretation that restricts 'law wujida' to necessary connection.

Core Idea
Interpreting 'law wujida' as necessary connection excludes most propositions and reveals a grammatical error.

The author of the commentary (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani) attacks an interpretation of the conditional phrase 'law wujida' in the definition of universal categorical propositions. He argues that connection (ittisal) can be either necessary (luzum) or coincidental (ittifaq), and that restricting it to necessity excludes the majority of propositions. He also identifies a scribal error that adds a conjunction, violating Arabic grammar.

Guided Path
1
Distinguishing necessary vs. coincidental connection
The passage explains that connection between subject/description or predicate/description can be necessary (e.g., sun rising entails daytime) or coincidental (e.g., man's rationality and donkey's braying). Notice the examples.
2
Understanding the critique of restriction to necessity
The author criticizes those who interpret 'law wujida kana j' as meaning 'everything that necessitates (j) also necessitates (b)'. He argues this excludes propositions where descriptions are accidental.
3
Identifying the consequence of restriction
Restricting to necessary connection reduces all propositions to necessary ones (dharuriyya) or even a narrower class, which is too limiting.
4
Spotting the scribal error
The author notes a variant with 'wa kana j' (and it was j). This is a gross error because 'kana j' is a necessary condition for the subject, and a conjunction cannot link a condition and its conditioned; also, grammar requires 'kana j' to be the answer of 'law', not conjoined.
The error is grammatically impossible for Arabic speakers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما اعتبر في عقد الوضع الاتصال - وهو قولنا : «لو وجد كان (ج)» - وكذا في عقد الحمل - وهو قولنا : «لو وجد كان (ب)»- والاتصال قديكون بطريق اللزوم كقولنا إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وقد يكون بطريق الاتفاق كقولنا : إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق» فسره صاحب الكشف ومن تابعه باللزوم، فقالوا : معنى قولنا : «كل ما لووجد كان (ج) فهو بحيث لووجد كان (ب)» : أن كل ماهو ملزوم لـ(ج) فهو ملزوم لـ(ب)». وليت شعري لم لم يكتفوا بمطلق الاتصال حتى لزمهم خروج أكثر القضايا عن تفسيرهم، لأنه لا ينطبق إلا على قضية يكون وصف موضوعها ووصف محمولها لازمين لذات الموضوع، وأما القضايا التي أحد وصفيها - أو كلاهما - غير لازم فخارجة عن ذلك؛ ولزمهم أيضاً حصر القضايا في الضرورة - إذ لا معنى للضرورية إلا لزوم وصف المحمول لذات الموضوع - بل في أخص من الضرورية : لاعتبار لزوم وصف الموضوع في مفهوم القضية وعدم اعتباره في مفهوم الضرورية. وقد وقع في بعض النسخ : «كل ما لووجد وكان ج» - بالواو العاطفة – وهو خطأ فاحش، لأنّ «كان ج» لازم لوجود الموضوع على مافسره به، ولا معنى للواو العاطفة بين اللازم والملزوم؛ على أن ذلك ليس بمشتبه أيضاً على أهل العربية، فإن «لو» حرف شرط ولابد له من جواب، وجوابه ليس قولنا : فهو بحيث لأنه خبر المبتدء، بل «كان ج»، وجواب الشرط لا يعطف عليه. وأما الثاني فيراد به كلّ (ج) في الخارج فهو (ب) في الخارج، والحكم فيه على الموجود في الخارج، سواء كان اتصافه ب(ج) حال الحكم أو قبله أو بعده، لأن مالم يوجد في الخارج أزلاً وأبداً يستحيل أن يكون (ب) في الخارج.
And when connection was considered in the subject term -- which is our saying 'if it existed, it would be (j)' -- and likewise in the predicate term -- which is our saying 'if it existed, it would be (b)' -- and connection may be by way of necessity, like our saying 'if the sun is rising, then daytime exists', and may be by way of coincidence, like our saying 'if man is rational, then the donkey is braying', the author of al-Kashshaf and his followers interpreted it as necessity, saying: the meaning of our saying 'everything that if it existed would be (j) is such that if it existed would be (b)' is that everything that is a necessary condition for (j) is a necessary condition for (b). I wish I knew why they were not content with absolute connection, so that the exclusion of most propositions from their interpretation became necessary for them, because it applies only to a proposition in which the description of its subject and the description of its predicate are necessary to the essence of the subject. As for propositions in which one or both descriptions are not necessary, they are excluded from that. And it also became necessary for them to restrict propositions to necessary ones -- since the meaning of necessary is only the necessity of the predicate's description to the essence of the subject -- indeed to what is more specific than necessary: because of considering the necessity of the subject's description in the concept of the proposition while it is not considered in the concept of the necessary. And in some copies it occurs: 'everything that if it existed and was (j)' with the conjunctive 'wa', and this is a gross error, because 'was (j)' is a necessary condition for the existence of the subject according to their interpretation, and there is no meaning for the conjunctive 'wa' between the condition and the conditioned. Moreover, this is not ambiguous to those proficient in Arabic, for 'law' is a conditional particle and must have an answer, and its answer is not our saying 'it is such that' because it is the predicate of the subject, but rather 'was (j)', and the answer of a conditional is not conjoined. As for the second, by it is meant: every (j) in the external world is (b) in the external world, and the judgment is about that which exists in the external world, whether its being described by (j) is at the time of judgment, before it, or after it, because that which never exists in the external world, eternally, is impossible to be (b) in the external world.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This is a critical passage where the author (likely al-Sharif al-Jurjani in a commentary) attacks an interpretation of the conditional phrasing used in defining universal categorical propositions. The passage begins by establishing that connection (ittisal) in the subject term and predicate term can be either necessary (luzum) or coincidental (ittifaq). The author of al-Kashshaf and his followers, however, interpreted it exclusively as necessity, meaning that 'every (j) that if it existed would be (b)' is paraphrased as 'everything that necessitates (j) also necessitates (b)'. The author expresses bewilderment ('I wish I knew why') at this restriction, arguing that it causes most propositions to fall outside their interpretation. Only those where both subject and predicate descriptions are essential to the subject's essence are covered; any proposition with accidental descriptions is excluded. Furthermore, this reduces all propositions to necessary ones (dharuriyya) or even a narrower class. The passage then notes a scribal error in some manuscripts: adding a conjunction 'wa' before 'kana j', turning it into 'law wujida wa kana j'. This is a gross error (khat' fahish) because 'kana j' is a necessary condition (lazim) for the existence of the subject, and there is no conjunction between a condition and its conditioned. Arabic grammar also requires that the answer of 'law' be a clause; in the correct reading, 'kana j' is the answer, not 'fahuwa bihaythu'. The author concludes by clarifying the meaning of the second part of the interpretation (the predicate side): it refers to actual existence, past, present, or future, but not to non-existent things.

Key Terms
عقد الوضع
عقد الحمل
اتصال
لزوم
اتفاق
قضية منحرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author criticize the interpretation of 'law wujida' as necessary connection (luzum)?
A) It makes all propositions false.
B) It excludes propositions with accidental descriptions, which are common.
C) It contradicts the teachings of Aristotle.
D) It leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The connection in universal categorical propositions can be necessary or coincidental; restricting it to necessity excludes most propositions and ignores Arabic grammatical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why Quantity Must Differ in Contradictory Propositions
Author explains the need for difference in quantity for quantified contradiction and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why difference in quantity is a necessary condition for contradiction in quantified propositions and refute the objection that unity in the particularity of the subject could replace it.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires difference in quantity because quantity is part of the proposition's meaning, not an external detail.

In the previous lesson, you learned that for two quantified propositions to be contradictory, they must differ in quantity (e.g., one universal, one particular). But a thoughtful objection arises: if two particular propositions refer to the same specific 'some', wouldn't they become contradictory? This lesson explores the answer, which hinges on the distinction between what is internal to the proposition's concept and what is external.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's challenge
The objector argues that just as difference in quantity produces contradiction, so too does unity in the specific 'some'. Find where he says this.
The objector's claim is that if two particular propositions share the same specific part, they contradict.
2
Understand the objector's reasoning
He claims that if two particulars share the same specific subject part, they contradict. Note that he is treating 'some' as if it picks out a definite group.
The objector assumes that 'some' can be specified to a fixed group, making the propositions about the same individuals.
3
Locate the commentator's answer
The answer states that judgments are based on concepts (mafhūmāt), and the particularity of 'some' is external to the concept of a particular proposition.
The commentator argues that the specific reference of 'some' is not part of the proposition's inherent meaning.
4
Distinguish internal vs. external conditions
Quantity is part of the concept; the specific reference of 'some' is not. Therefore, requiring unity in that external particularity is invalid, so the original condition of quantity difference stands.
This distinction is crucial: only conditions internal to the proposition's concept can serve as conditions for contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاقض
فإن قلت : الجزئيتان إنما تتصادقان لاختلاف الموضوع، لالاتحاد (1) يعنى أن انتفاء التناقض في الجزئيتين كما أنه مقارن لعدم الاختلاف في الكمية، كذلك مقارن لعدم الاتحاد في خصوصية الموضوع، و إذا اعتبر الاختلاف مع سائر الشرائط حصل التناقض، كذلك إذا اعتبر الاتحاد في خصوصية الموضوع، مع باقي الشرائط حصل التناقض أيضاً ، فلم لا يكون الاتحاد في الموضوع شرطاً دون الاختلاف في الكمية.
أجاب بأن مناط أحكام القضايا إنما هو (ن :+ في) مفهوماتها، وخصوصية البعض خارجة عن مفهوم القضية الجزئية، فلا يمكن اعتبار اشتراط الاتحاد فيها وإلا لكان التناقض في الجزئيات باعتبار أمر خارج عنها - فلذلك لم يعتبر - بخلاف الكمية فإنّها داخلة في مفهومات القضايا فوجب اعتبار الاختلاف فيها ليتحقق التناقض (شريف).
al-Ḥāqiḍ / The conclusion? If you say: The two particular propositions are only true together due to the difference of subject, because of non-unity. (1) Meaning that the absence of contradiction in the two particulars, just as it is concomitant with the lack of difference in quantity, so too it is concomitant with the lack of unity in the particularity of the subject. And if difference is considered along with the other conditions, contradiction occurs; similarly, if unity in the particularity of the subject is considered with the remaining conditions, contradiction also occurs. So why is not unity in the subject a condition instead of difference in quantity? The answer: Because the basis of judgments about propositions is their concepts, and the particularity of 'some' is external to the concept of the partial proposition. So it is not possible to require unity in it, otherwise contradiction in particulars would depend on something external to them—therefore it is not considered. This is unlike quantity, which is internal to the concepts of propositions, so difference in it must be considered for contradiction to be realized. (al-Sharīf)
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning the condition of quantity for quantified contradictory propositions. The objector points out that when two particular propositions like 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human' are both true, it is because the subject 'animal' is taken differently: in the first, it refers to some part, in the second to another part. So if we require unity of the subject in its particularity (i.e., the same 'some'), then the two would become contradictory. Thus, unity in the specific subject (the particular instance) could serve as a condition instead of difference in quantity. The answer given by al-Sharīf (the commentator) is that the basis of propositional judgments is their conceptual content (mafhūmāt). The particularity indicated by 'some' is not part of the concept of a particular proposition; it is external. Therefore, requiring unity in that external particularity would make contradiction depend on something outside the proposition's meaning, which is not legitimate. In contrast, quantity (universal/particular) is internal to the proposition's concept, so difference in quantity is a valid condition. Thus, the original requirement of difference in quantity stands, and the objection fails.

A) Because particular propositions are always true together.
B) Because the particularity of 'some' is external to the concept of the proposition.
C) Because quantity is more important.
D) Because the subject must be the same word.

Key Takeaway
Difference in quantity is essential for contradiction in quantified propositions because quantity is part of the proposition's concept, while the specific referent of 'some' is external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the propositions: 'Every Muslim is just' (A) and 'Some Muslim is not just' (O). Are they contradictory? Explain using the condition of quantity. Then, consider 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human'. Are they contradictory? Why or why not? Your answer should reference the distinction between internal and external conditions from this lesson.

Why Quantified Contradictories Must Differ in Quantity
Qutbi explains the necessity of quantity difference for contradiction in quantified propositions, with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a pair of quantified propositions are contradictory by checking their quantity, and explain why the logical concept, not external specification, is the basis for contradiction.

Core Idea
Same quantity can't contradict; only opposite quantities ensure true opposition.

You already know the eight conditions for contradiction, such as unity of subject, predicate, time, place, etc. But when propositions are quantified (universal or particular), an additional condition is required: they must differ in quantity. This passage from Qutbi's commentary explains why this is necessary, using examples from logic and addressing objections about subject unity.

Guided Path
1
State the Rule
Read the opening lines: for quantified propositions, after all eight conditions, they must also differ in quantity (one universal, one particular). This is the claim.
2
Test Same Quantity
Examine the examples: two universals ('Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human') are both false, so not contradictory. Two particulars ('Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human') are both true, so not contradictory.
The subject 'animal' is more general than 'human' – a key point.
3
Analyze the First Objection
An objector argues that two particulars could be contradictory if the subject referred to the same individuals. Understand why: if we specify the same individual, then one says it is human and the other says it is not, which is contradictory. So why not enforce unity of subject instead of quantity difference?
The answer: logical judgments consider the concept (mafhūm), not external specifications. The concept of 'some animal' does not fix which individual.
4
Analyze the Second Objection
Another objection: the logicians already include unity of subject among the eight conditions. So why add quantity? Qutbi distinguishes: unity of subject is only in mention (the wording 'some animal'), not in the logical concept. Since unity in mention already holds but no contradiction occurs, quantity difference is needed.
This shows the necessity of the extra condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت القضيتان محصورتين فلابد مع ذلك – أي مع اتحادهما في الأمور الثمانية – من اختلافهما في الكم، أي في الكلية والجزئية، فإنهما لو كانتا كليتين أو جزئيتين لم يتناقضا لجواز كذب الكليتين وصدق الجزئيتين في كل مادة يكون الموضوع فيها أعم من المحمول، كقولنا: كل حيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان فإنهما كاذبتان، وكقولنا: بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان فإنهما صادقتان.

فإن قلت: الجزئيتان إنما تتصادقان لاختلاف الموضوع، لا لاتحاد الكمية فإن البعض المحكوم عليه بالإنسانية غير البعض المحكوم عليه بسلب الإنسانية. فنقول: النظر في جميع الأحكام إنما هو إلى مفهوم القضية، ولما لوحظ مفهوم الجزئيتين – وهو الإيجاب لبعض الأفراد والسلب عن بعض – لم تتناقضا؛ وأما تعين الموضوع فأمر خارج عن المفهوم.

فإن قلت: أليس اعتبروا وحدة الموضوع فما الحاجة إلى اعتبار شرط آخر في المحصورات؟ أجاب بأن المراد ما اعتبروه وحدة الموضوع في الذكر، وهذه الوحدة حاصلة في الجزئيتين ولا تناقض، فلابد من اعتبار شرط آخر هو اختلاف الكمية كما بينا. فحاصل السؤال الأول: لم اعتبرت الاختلاف في الكمية ولم تعتبر الاتحاد في الموضوع مع أنه مغن عن الاختلاف في الكمية؟ أجاب بأنه لا يمكن اعتبار الاتحاد، لأنه اعتبار أمر خارج. وحاصل السؤال الثاني: أن القوم قد اعتبروا الاتحاد، سواء قلت إنه اعتبار أمر خارج فيلزم بطلان ما ذكرت من أن النظر في أحكام القضايا إلى مفهوماتها، أو قلت إنه ليس كذلك فيبطل ما ذكرت من أن اعتباره اعتبار أمر خارج، ومع اعتبارهم الاتحاد في الموضوع لا حاجة إلى اشتراط الاختلاف في الكمية في تناقض الجزئيات.
And if the two propositions are quantified, then besides that – i.e., besides their unity in the eight matters – it is necessary that they differ in quantity, i.e., in universality and particularity. For if both were universal or both particular, they would not be contradictory, because it is possible for both universals to be false and both particulars to be true in any matter in which the subject is more general than the predicate, like our saying: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human' – both are false; and like our saying: 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human' – both are true.

If you say: The two particular propositions are only true together due to difference in subject, not due to unity of quantity; for the individual affirmed of humanity is other than the individual denied of humanity. We say: Consideration in all judgments is only to the concept of the proposition; and when the concept of the two particulars – which is affirmation for some individuals and negation for some – is considered, they are not contradictory. As for the specification of the subject, it is a matter external to the concept.

If you say: Did they not consider unity of subject? So what is the need to consider another condition in quantified propositions? He answered: What they considered is unity of subject in the mention, and this unity is present in the two particulars yet there is no contradiction; so it is necessary to consider another condition, which is difference in quantity, as we explained. Thus the gist of the first question: Why did you consider difference in quantity and not consider unity of subject, although it would dispense with difference in quantity? He answered: It is not possible to consider unity, because it is consideration of an external matter. And the gist of the second question: The [logicians] did consider unity – whether you say it is consideration of an external matter, then your statement that consideration in judgments is to their concepts would be invalid; or you say it is not so, then your statement that considering it is consideration of an external matter would be invalid; and with their consideration of unity of subject, there is no need to stipulate difference in quantity in the contradiction of particulars.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage from Qutbi's commentary defines the condition for contradiction (tanāquḍ) between quantified categorical propositions (al-qadiyyatān al-mahṣūratān). The author begins by stating that even if two propositions agree in all eight matters (the conditions for contradiction) – which include unity of subject, predicate, time, place, condition, etc. – they still must differ in quantity (al-kamm), meaning one must be universal and the other particular. He explains why: if both are universal, they could both be false, as in the example 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human' (both false). If both are particular, they could both be true, as in 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human' (both true). Thus, contradiction requires difference in quantity.

Then comes an objection: the reason two particular propositions are both true is that the subject differs – the individual affirmed as human is different from the one denied humanity. So if we require unity of subject (i.e., the same individual), would that not yield contradiction even without difference in quantity? The author answers that logical judgments consider only the concept (mafhūm) of the proposition. The particular proposition's concept is simply 'affirmation for some individuals' and 'negation for some individuals', without specifying which ones. The specification of the subject is external to the concept, so it cannot be considered.

A second objection follows: but the logicians do consider unity of subject in the conditions for contradiction (the eight matters). So why need difference in quantity? The author distinguishes: what they considered is unity of subject in the mention (i.e., the wording), not in the logical concept. Unity in the mention is already present in two particular propositions (both say 'some animal'), yet no contradiction arises. Therefore, an additional condition – difference in quantity – is necessary. The two objections thus highlight the need to keep logical concepts separate from external specifications, and affirm that the condition of quantity difference is essential for contradiction in quantified propositions.

الكم
التناقض
المفهوم

A) Because they have the same quantity and therefore can both be true.
B) Because they differ in quantity.
C) Because the subject is not unified.
D) Because the predicate is different.

Key Takeaway
For contradiction between two quantified propositions, they must differ in quantity (one universal, one particular), because same quantity can allow both to be true or both false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the pair 'Every Prophet is infallible' and 'No Prophet is infallible'. Are they contradictory? Use the condition of quantity difference to decide. Then construct a pair that is truly contradictory by changing the quantity of one proposition.
?
In a jadal (debate) about the permissibility of riba, a claimant says 'All loans with interest are forbidden' and the opponent says 'Some loans with interest are not forbidden'. Check whether these are contradictory using the eight conditions plus quantity difference. If not, explain why.

Conditions for Contradiction in Quantified and Modal Propositions
Author explains conditions for contradiction beyond unity of subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the two additional conditions for contradiction (difference in quantity and difference in modality) in quantified and modal propositions.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires more than unity of subject; for modal propositions, difference in modality is also necessary.

You already know that two propositions must have the same subject and predicate to contradict. But our author shows that this is not enough when the propositions are quantified or modal. Two particular propositions can have the same subject and predicate yet not be contradictory because their concepts (affirmation for some vs. negation for some) are not opposites. Similarly, two necessary propositions can both be false if the predicate is not necessary for the subject. This lesson unpacks these subtle conditions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the subject 'in mention' from the 'essence of the subject'
The author says unity of subject is required but only for the subject in mention (the term itself), not the essence (the actual individuals). If we considered the essence, a universal and a particular would have different referents (all vs. some) and could never contradict. So unity of subject in mention is a minimal condition, but it alone does not guarantee contradiction.
This distinction resolves an apparent contradiction with earlier teachings.
2
Identify why two particular propositions are not contradictory
The concept of a particular affirmative is 'affirmation for some individuals', and a particular negative is 'negation for some'. These are not opposites: the same individual could be both affirmed and negated by different particulars? Actually, they can both be true (if some are A and some are not A) or both false (if all are A or none are A). So difference in quantity is needed: one must be universal and the other particular.
The passage focuses on two particulars, but the condition extends to any pair that lacks quantity difference.
3
Add the condition of difference in modality for modal propositions
Even with proper quantity, if both propositions share the same modality (e.g., both necessary or both possible), they may not be contradictory. Example: 'Every human is necessarily a writer' and 'Not every human is necessarily a writer' are both false because writing is not necessary for humans. Similarly, 'Every human is possibly a writer' and 'Not every human is possibly a writer' can both be true. So difference in modality is essential.
Modality here refers to necessity (darura) and possibility (imkan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : النظر في جميع الأحكام إنما هو إلى مفهوم القضية، ولما لوحظ مفهوم الجزئيتين - وهو الإيجاب لبعض الأفراد والسلب عن بعض - لم تتناقضا؛ وأما تعين الموضوع فأمر خارج عن المفهوم. فإن قلت : أليس اعتبروا وحدة الموضوع فما الحاجة إلى اعتبار شرط آخر في المحصورات ؟ قلت : المراد بالموضوع الموضوع في الذكر – لاذات الموضوع – وإلا لم يكن بين الكلية والجزئية تناقض، فإن ذات الموضوع في الكلية جميع الأفراد وفي الجزئية بعضها وهما مختلفان. هذا كله إذا لم تكن القضيتان موجهتين، وأما إذا كانتا موجهتين فلابد مع تلك الشرائط من شرط آخر في كل - أي في المخصوصات والمحصورات - وهو الاختلاف في الجهة، لأنهما لو اتحدتا في الجهة لم تتناقضا، لكذب الضروريتين في مادة الإمكان، كقولنا : «كل إنسان كاتب بالضرورة»، و «ليس كل إنسان كاتباً بالضرورة» فإنهما يكذبان لأن إيجاب الكتابة لشيء من أفراد الإنسان ليس بضروري، ولاسلبها نه، وصدق الممكنتين فيها، كقولنا : كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان و ليس كل إنسان كاتباً بالإمكان. فقد بان أن اختلاف الجهة لابد منه في الموجهات.
We say: Consideration in all judgments is only of the concept of the proposition. When the concept of two particulars is observed—which is affirmation for some individuals and negation for some—they are not contradictory. As for the specification of the subject, it is something external to the concept. If you say: Did they not consider the unity of the subject? So what is the need for considering another condition in quantified [propositions]? I say: What is meant by the subject is the subject in mention—not the essence of the subject—otherwise there would be no contradiction between the universal and the particular, for the essence of the subject in the universal is all individuals and in the particular some of them, and they are different. All this if the two propositions are not modalized. But if they are modalized, then along with those conditions there must be another condition in every—i.e., in particular and quantified—which is difference in modality, because if they were the same in modality they would not be contradictory, due to the falsity of two necessary [propositions] in the matter of possibility, like our saying: 'Every human is necessarily a writer' and 'Not every human is necessarily a writer'—for both are false because affirming writing for any individual human is not necessary, nor is its negation; and the truth of two possible [propositions] in it, like our saying: 'Every human is possibly a writer' and 'Not every human is possibly a writer'. So it is clear that difference in modality is necessary in modalized [propositions].
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for contradiction (tanaqud) in quantified propositions and then extends the analysis to modal propositions. The author begins by stating that the judgment about contradiction is based on the concept (mafhum) of the proposition, not on the specific subject matter. For two particular propositions (juz'iyyat), their concepts are affirmation for some individuals and negation for some, which are not opposites—hence no contradiction. The specification of the subject (ta'ayyun al-mawdu') is external to the concept. A hypothetical objection is raised: why is unity of subject not sufficient, given that earlier authorities considered it? The answer distinguishes between the subject in mention (al-mawdu' fi al-dhikr) and the essence of the subject (dhat al-mawdu'). If the latter were meant, a universal and a particular could not contradict because their subject essences differ (all vs. some). So unity of subject in mention is already present in two particulars, yet no contradiction arises; thus an additional condition—difference in quantity—is needed. Then the passage turns to modal propositions (muwajjahat). Even with the earlier conditions, for modalized propositions a further condition of difference in modality (ikhtilaf fi al-jiha) is required. If two propositions share the same modality, they may both be false or both true in certain matters, as shown by the example: 'Every human is necessarily a writer' and 'Not every human is necessarily a writer'—both are false because writing is not necessary for humans. Similarly, two possible propositions ('every human is possibly a writer' and 'not every human is possibly a writer') can both be true. Hence, difference in modality is necessary for contradiction.

موضوع

جزئية

D) Because they are both false.

Key Takeaway
For contradiction, in addition to unity of subject (in mention) and predicate, quantified propositions require difference in quantity, and modal propositions require difference in modality.

8 min
Difference in Modality for Contradiction
Author defines the condition of difference in modality for contradiction in modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two modal propositions satisfy the condition for contradiction by checking the unity of subject (as wording) and difference in modality.

Core Idea
Two modal propositions with the same modality can both be false, so they do not contradict.

The standard conditions for contradiction (unity of subject, predicate, time, etc.) are necessary but not sufficient when propositions are modal. In this lesson, we explore the extra condition of differing modalities, ensuring that contradictory modal propositions cannot be both true or both false. The passage clarifies a key nuance: the subject must be identical in wording, not in essence, to avoid false contradictions between universal and particular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality of each proposition
Check whether each proposition contains a modality (jiha) such as necessity (ḍarūra), possibility (imkān), or others. For example, 'Every human is a writer necessarily' has the modality of necessity.
Modal propositions are called muwajjahāt.
2
Check if the modalities are different
If both propositions have the same modality, they cannot be contradictory because they could both be false (if necessary in a possible matter) or both true (if possible). Only if the modalities differ can one be true and the other false.
Difference in modality is an additional condition beyond unity of subject, predicate, etc.
3
Test with a possible matter example
Take a predicate that is not necessary for any subject, e.g., 'writer' for humans. Two necessary propositions ('Every human is necessarily a writer' and 'Not every human is necessarily a writer') are both false because writing is not necessary for any human. Thus they do not contradict.
Similarly, two possible propositions can both be true.
4
Apply the unity of subject in wording
Ensure the subject is the same in wording (the mentioned term), not in essence (the actual set of individuals). For example, 'every human' and 'some human' have different essences, but if considered in wording, the universal 'human' is the same subject term.
Otherwise, a universal and particular would never be contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناقض
قلت : المراد بالموضوع الموضوع في الذكر – لاذات الموضوع – وإلاّ لم يكن بين الكلية والجزئية تناقض، فإنّ ذات الموضوع في الكلية جميع الأفراد وفي الجزئية بعضها وهما مختلفان.
هذا كله إذا لم تكن القضيّتان موجّهتين، وأما إذا كانتا موجهتين فلابد مع تلك الشرائط من شرط آخر في كل - أي في المخصوصات والمحصورات - وهو الاختلاف في الجهة ، لأنّهما لو اتحدتا في الجهة لم تتناقضا، لكذب الضروريّتين في مادة الإمكان، كقولنا : «كلّ إنسان كاتب بالضرورة»، و «ليس كل إنسان كاتباً بالضرورة» فإنهما يكذبان لأن إيجاب الكتابة لشيء من أفراد الإنسان ليس بضروري، ولاسلبها نه، وصدق الممكنتين فيها، كقولنا : كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان» و ليس كل إنسان كاتباً بالإمكان.
فقد بان أن اختلاف الجهة لابد منه في الموجهات.
Contradiction. I say: what is meant by the subject is the subject mentioned in the utterance, not the essence of the subject; otherwise there would be no contradiction between the universal and the particular, for the essence of the subject in the universal is all individuals and in the particular is some of them, and they are different. All this is when the two propositions are not modal. But if they are modal, then along with those conditions there must be another condition in every - i.e. in the particular and quantified - and it is the difference in modality, because if they were the same in modality they would not contradict, due to the falsity of two necessary propositions in a possible matter, like our saying: 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'Not every human is a writer necessarily,' for both are false because affirming writing for some individual human is not necessary, and its negation is not necessary either, and the truth of two possible propositions in it, like our saying: 'Every human is a writer possibly' and 'Not every human is a writer possibly.' So it has become clear that difference in modality is necessary in modal propositions.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage clarifies the conditions for contradiction, specifically addressing modal propositions. The author first resolves a potential confusion: the unity of subject required for contradiction is unity in the wording (the mentioned subject), not in the essence (the actual set of individuals), because otherwise a universal and a particular proposition would not be contradictory since their subjects are different sets. Then he moves to modal propositions: for non-modal propositions, certain standard conditions (like unity of subject, predicate, time, place, etc.) are sufficient. But when propositions are modal (like necessary, possible), an additional condition is required: the two propositions must differ in modality. The reason is that two propositions with the same modality can both be false (as when both are necessary in a possible matter) or both true (as when both are possible). The example with 'writer' illustrates: 'Every human is necessarily a writer' and 'Not every human is necessarily a writer' are both false because writing is not necessary for any human. Similarly, the two possible propositions are both true because writing is possible for some. Hence they do not form a contradictory pair. Thus, difference in modality is necessary.

Key Terms
التناقض
الجهة
الموجهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are two necessary propositions like 'Every human is necessarily a writer' and 'Not every human is necessarily a writer' not contradictory?
A) Because they have different subjects.
B) Because they can both be false.
C) Because they have different predicates.
D) Because one is universal and the other particular.

Key Takeaway
For modal propositions, contradiction requires that the two propositions differ in modality, otherwise they may both be false (if necessary in a possible matter) or both true (if possible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the two propositions: (1) 'Every created thing is necessarily originated' (kull ḥādith ḥādith bi-ḍarūra) and (2) 'Not every created thing is necessarily originated' (laysa kull ḥādith ḥādith bi-ḍarūra). Evaluate whether they are contradictories. Use the condition of modality difference and the possible matter test: is it possible for something created to be not originated? Unity of Subject in Contradiction: Description vs Essence
Author resolves an objection on quantifier variation and subject unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how difference in quantity (e.g., 'every' vs 'some') is compatible with the condition of unity of subject in contradictory pairs.

Core Idea
Unity of subject means same wording, not same set.

In the conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ), one requirement is that the subject term be identical. But what does 'identical' mean? Does it exclude differences in quantity, like 'every human' vs 'some human'? An objector thought it does, but the author clarifies that what matters is unity in the description (the word) not the actual individuals. This subtle distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of contradiction.

Guided Path
1
State the standard condition
Recall that for two propositions to be contradictory, they must have unity of subject, predicate, time, place, and relation. The objector focuses on subject unity.
2
Identify the objection
The objector argues: if the subject must be one, how can we allow difference in quantity? Because 'every human' and 'some human' refer to different sets, so the subject seems to change.
The objection is that unity of subject is violated if the quantifier changes.
3
Extract the author's answer
The author responds that the required unity is in the 'description' (al-ʿunwān), not in the particular essence. That is, the same word must be used as the subject term, even if the quantifier makes it refer to all or some.
Unity in description means the subject term is identical in wording.
4
Reformulate the objection correctly
The commentator notes that the objector's phrasing 'so what need' is off. The correct challenge is 'how is difference in quantity conditioned?' The point is not to question why it's needed, but to show that it must be preserved despite apparent conflict with subject unity.
This step shows the careful wording required in logical debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجاب بأن ما اعتبروه الاتحاد في العنوان دون خصوصية الذات. وقد يتوهم أن حاصل السؤال الثاني أنهم اعتبروا وحدة الموضوع فكيف يعتبرون الاختلاف في الكميّة فإنّه يوجب عدم الاتحاد في الموضوع؛ إذ يصير الموضوع في إحدى القضيتيتن الجميع و في الأخرى البعض، وعلى هذا فقوله : «فما الحاجة» ليس على ما ينبغي، بل يجب أن يقال بدله : «فكيف يشترط الاختلاف في الكميّة»، وما قررناه في توجيه السؤال الثاني هو المطابق العبارته وهو المنقول عن الشارح (شريف).
He answered: that what they considered is unity in the description, not the particularity of the essence. And it may be imagined that the gist of the second question is that they considered unity of subject, so how can they consider difference in quantity? For that necessitates lack of unity in the subject, since the subject in one proposition becomes 'all' and in the other 'some.' On this, his saying: 'so what need' is not appropriate; rather it should be said instead: 'so how is difference in quantity conditioned?' And what we have established in directing the second question is what matches his expression and is what is transmitted from the commentator (Sharif).
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage contains a dialectical exchange. An objector points out an apparent inconsistency: the standard conditions for contradiction require unity of subject, yet later they allow difference in quantity (e.g., one proposition universal, the other particular). The response clarifies that the unity required is in the 'description' (the wording of the subject term), not in the actual essence (the set of individuals). Thus 'every human' and 'some human' share the same description ('human'), so the condition is met. The commentator then critiques the phrasing of the objection: the objector said 'so what need' (implying why is difference in quantity needed), but the correct phrasing should be 'how is difference in quantity conditioned' (since it seems to violate unity). The commentator notes that his formulation matches the intended meaning and is transmitted from al-Sharif.

Key Terms
الاتحاد في العنوان (al-ittiḥād fī al-ʿunwān): Unity in the description or wording of the subject, not in the particular essence. [Note: Stable in discussions of contradiction conditions.]

D) The propositions must have the same quantifier.

Key Takeaway
Unity of subject in contradiction means the subject term is the same word, even if the quantifier changes from 'every' to 'some'.
