He said: As for compound [propositions], if they are universal, their contradictory is one of the contradictories of their two parts—that is clear after comprehending the realities of compound [propositions] and the contradictories of simple ones. For if you realize that the existential non-permanent [proposition] is composed of two absolute general [propositions], one affirmative and the other negative, and that the contradictory of the absolute is the permanent, you realize that its contradictory is either the permanent contrary [in quality] or the permanent agreeing [in quality]. The statement: The compound proposition is an expression of a sum of two propositions differing in affirmation and negation, so its contradictory is the negation of that sum; but the negation of the sum is only by negating one of its parts indeterminately. For if its two parts are actual, the sum is actual, and the negation of one of the two parts is one of the contradictories of the two parts indeterminately. Thus it is a necessary equivalent to the contradictory of the compound, and it is the concept that is disjunctively between the contradictories of the two parts, because each of the two contradictories is a concept disjunctively between them. So it is said: either this contradictory or that contradictory, and in reality it is an exclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the two parts. Therefore the way to take the contradictory of a compound is to analyze it into its two simples, take the contradictory of each, and compose an exclusive disjunction from the two contradictories. It is equivalent to its contradictory because whenever the original is true, the disjunction is false. For whenever the original is true, its two parts are true, and whenever the two parts are true, their contradictories are false, so the exclusive disjunction is false due to the falsity of its two parts. And whenever the original is false, the disjunction is true, because whenever the original is false, necessarily one of its two parts is false, and whenever one of its two parts is false, its contradictory is true, so the disjunction is true due to the truth of one of its two parts. And that—i.e., the way to take the contradictory of a compound—is clear after comprehending the realities of compounds and the contradictories of simples. For if you realize that the existential non-permanent is composed of two absolute general [propositions], the first of which agrees with the original in quality and the last of which disagrees with it in quality, and you realize that the contradictory of the agreeing absolute general is the permanent contrary, and the contradictory of the disagreeing absolute general is the permanent agreeing, you know that the contradictory of the existential non-permanent is either the permanent contrary or the permanent agreeing. And likewise, if you realize that the existential non-necessary is composed of an absolute general agreeing with the original proposition in quality and a possible general disagreeing with it, and that the contradictory of the agreeing absolute general is the permanent contrary, and the contradictory of the disagreeing possible is the necessary agreeing, then the contradictory of the existential non-necessary is either the permanent contrary or the necessary agreeing. And similarly, the contradictory of the special conditioned is either the temporary possible contrary or the permanent agreeing.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage explains how to find the contradictory (naqid) of a compound proposition. The author begins by stating that for universal compound propositions, the contradictory is one of the contradictories of its two parts, taken indeterminately. This becomes clear once one understands natures of compound propositions and the contradictories of simple ones. He illustrates with the example of the 'existential non-permanent' (al-wujudiyya al-la-da'ima). This proposition is composed of two absolute general (mutlaqa 'amma) propositions: one affirmative and one negative. The contradictory of an absolute proposition is the permanent (da'ima) proposition. Therefore, the contradictory of the existential non-permanent is either the permanent that is opposite in quality (mukhalifa) or the permanent that agrees in quality (muwafiq).

He then gives a general definition: a compound proposition is the conjunction (jumla) of two propositions differing in affirmation and negation. Its contradictory is the negation of that conjunction. The negation of a conjunction is achieved by negating one of its parts, but not determinately—i.e., it is a disjunction of the negations. So the contradictory of the compound is a concept that is disjunctively between the contradictories of the two parts. This is actually an exclusive disjunction (man'iat al-khuluw) composed of the contradictories of the parts.

The method to find the contradictory is: resolve the compound into its two simple propositions, find the contradictory of each, and compose a disjunction that is exclusive (man'iat al-khuluw). This disjunction is equivalent to the contradictory of the original compound. Proof: If the original is true, both its parts are true, so their contradictories are false, making the disjunction false. If the original is false, at least one part is false, so its contradictory is true, making the disjunction true.

Finally, he applies this method to the existential non-permanent, showing its two parts are an absolute general agreeing in quality and another disagreeing. The contradictory of the agreeing absolute is the permanent contrary (da'ima mukhalifa), and of the disagreeing absolute is the permanent agreeing (da'ima muwafiq). Hence, the contradictory is a disjunction of these two. Similarly, for the existential non-necessary and the special conditioned (mashruta khassa), he gives analogous results. The passage thus provides a clear procedure for transforming compound modal propositions into their logical contradictories, which is essential for constructing valid syllogisms and dialectical arguments.

Modal compound propositions like 'existential non-necessary' have contradictories that are exclusive disjunctions of specific modal types, derived by resolving the compound into its modal simples and applying the rules of contradictories.

Finding the Contradictory of Universal Compound Propositions
Author explains the method to find the contradictory of a universal compound proposition via exclusive disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any universal compound proposition by expressing it as an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its simple parts.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound is an exclusive 'either-or' of the contradictories of its parts, not a simple negation.

Understanding how to negate compound propositions is crucial for logical inference in Islamic sciences. The classical method teaches us to break down a compound into its simple components, negate each, and combine them in a special way. This lesson unpacks that method as explained by the author, so you can apply it to real arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two simple propositions
A universal compound proposition is composed of two simple propositions joined by a logical connective. First, isolate these two parts. For example, in 'Every human is laughing actually, not always', the parts are 'Every human is laughing actually' and 'It is not the case that every human is always laughing' (or more precisely, 'Some human is not always laughing').
The first part agrees in quality with the original, the second differs. For universal compounds, the second part is the negation of the permanence.
2
Determine the quality of each simple part
Classify each simple part as affirmative or negative. In the example, the first part 'Every human is laughing actually' is affirmative (universal affirmative). The second part 'not always' implies a negative: 'Some human is not always laughing' (particular negative).
The original compound's overall quality is affirmative if the first part is affirmative and the second is negative? Actually the compound itself is affirmative (the act of laughing exists but not forever). The quality of each part matters for finding its contradictory.
3
Find the contradictory of each simple part
Use the standard rules for contradictories of categorical propositions. For example, the contradictory of 'Every human is laughing actually' is 'Some human is not laughing actually'. For the second part 'Some human is not always laughing', its contradictory is 'Every human is always laughing'.
In the text, they refer to these as 'permanent differing' and 'permanent agreeing' – these are modalized contradictories specific to the modal system.
4
Combine the contradictories into an exclusive disjunction
Take the two contradictories and combine them with a disjunction that precludes both being false (exclusive or). This disjunction is the full contradictory of the original compound. For the example, it becomes 'Either some human is not always laughing or every human is always laughing'.
The author proves that this disjunction is equivalent to the contradictory: if the original is true, both parts false; if false, at least one part true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناقض النقيضين مفهوم مردّد بينهما، فيقال إمّا هذا النقيض وإما ذلك النقيض، وبالحقيقة هو منفصلة مانعة الخلو مركبة من نقيضي الجزأين. فيكون طريق أخذ نقيض المركبة أن تحلّل إلى بسيطيها، ويؤخذ لكلّ منهما نقيض وتركب منفصلة مانعة الخلو من النقيضين، فهي مساوية لنقيضها، لأنه متى صدق الأصل كذبت المنفصلة، لأنه متى صدق الأصل صدق جزاه، ومتى صدق الجزآن كذب نقيضاهما فتكذب المنفصلة المانعة الخلو لكذب جزأيها ، ومتى كذب الأصل صدقت المنفصلة، لأنه متى كذب الأصل فلابد أن يكذب أحد جزأيه ، ومتى كذب أحد جزأيه صدق نقيضه فتصدق المنفصلة لصدق أحد جزأيها. وذلك – أي طريق أخذ نقيض المركبة - جلي بعد الإحاطة بحقائق المركبات ونقائض البسائط، فإنّك إذا تحققت أن الوجودية اللادائمة مركبة من مطلقتين عامتين أولاهما موافقة للأصل في الكيف وأخراهما مخالفة له في الكيف، وتحققت أن نقيض المطلقة العامة الموافقة «الدائمة المخالفة»، ونقيض المطلقة العامة المخالفة «الدائمة الموافقة» : علمت أن نقيض الوجودية اللادائمة إما الدائمة المخالفة أو الدائمة الموافقة ; فإذا (۱) ولما تحققت أن الوجودية اللاضروريّة مركبة من مطلقة عامة موافقة لأصل القضية في الكيف وممكنة عامة مخالفة له، وأن نقيض المطلقة العامة الموافقة الدائمة المخالفة ونقيض الممكنة المخالفة الضرورية الموافقة; فنقيض الوجودية اللاضرورية إما الدائمة المخالفة أو الضرورية الموافقة. وعلى هذا فنقيض المشروطة الخاصة إما الحينيّة الممكنة المخالفة أو الدائمة الموافقة. قلنا : «كل إنسان ضاحك بالفعل لادائمـاً» يكون نقيضه «إنـه لـيـس كذلك، بل إمّا ليس بعض الإنسان ضاحكاً دائماً أو بعض الإنسان ضاحك دائماً»، فقولنا : ليس كذلك»، وهو رفع المجموع ونقيضه الصريح، وقولنا : بل إمّا كذا وإما كذا المنفصلة المساوية للنقيض، وعلي هذا القياس في سائر المركبات.
Contradiction: the two contraries are a concept divided between them, so it is said either this contrary or that contrary. In reality, it is a disjunction that precludes emptiness composed of the two contraries of the two parts. Thus the method of taking the contrary of a compound is to analyze it into its two simples, take for each a contrary, and combine them in a disjunction that precludes emptiness of the two contraries. So it is equivalent to its contrary, because when the original is true, the disjunction is false; because when the original is true, both its parts are true, and when both parts are true, their two contraries are false, so the disjunction that precludes emptiness is false due to the falsity of both its parts. And when the original is false, the disjunction is true; because when the original is false, necessarily one of its parts is false, and when one of its parts is false, its contrary is true, so the disjunction is true due to the truth of one of its parts. And that—i.e., the method of taking the contrary of the compound—is clear after mastering the realities of compounds and the contraries of simples. For if you realize that the existential non-permanent is composed of two absolute general propositions, the first agreeing with the original in quality and the last differing from it, and you realize that the contrary of the agreeing absolute general is the permanent differing, and the contrary of the differing absolute general is the permanent agreeing, then you know that the contrary of the existential non-permanent is either the permanent differing or the permanent agreeing. Then (1) and when you realize that the existential non-necessary is composed of an absolute general agreeing with the original proposition in quality and a possible general differing from it, and that the contrary of the agreeing absolute general is the permanent differing and the contrary of the differing possible is the necessary agreeing, then the contrary of the existential non-necessary is either the permanent differing or the necessary agreeing. And similarly the contrary of the conditional special is either the temporal possible differing or the permanent agreeing. We said: 'Every human is actually laughing not always' has as its contrary 'It is not so, but rather either not every human is always laughing or some human is always laughing.' So our saying 'It is not so' is the removal of the whole and its explicit contrary, and our saying 'but rather either this or that' is the disjunction equivalent to the contrary. And upon this analogy in all compounds.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage explains the method for finding the contradictory (nāqiḍ) of a compound proposition. It begins by defining a contradictory as a concept that stands between two options, such that one says 'either this contradictory or that contradictory'. In logical reality, it is a disjunction that precludes emptiness (manī'at al-khuluw) composed of the contradictory of each part. The method then: analyze the compound into its two simple propositions, take the contradictory of each, and combine them with an exclusive disjunction (one must be true, not both false). The author proves that this disjunction is equivalent to the original's contradictory: if the original is true, both parts true, so both contraries false, making the disjunction false (since its two parts are false); if the original is false, at least one part false, so its contrary true, making the disjunction true. The author then applies this to modal propositions. For example, the 'existential non-permanent' (al-wujūdiyya lā dā'ima) is compounded from two absolute general (muṭlaqa 'āmma) propositions—one agreeing in quality, one differing. Its contradictory is a disjunction of the permanent differing (dā'ima mukhalifa) and the permanent agreeing (dā'ima muwāfiqa). Similarly for 'existential non-necessary' (al-wujūdiyya lā ḍarūriyya) and 'conditional special' (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa). The example given: the universal affirmative compound 'Every human is actually laughing, not always' (kull insān ḍāḥik bi'l-fi'l lā dā'iman) has as its contradictory the disjunction 'Either not every human is always laughing or some human is always laughing'. This illustrates the general rule: the contradictory of a universal compound is a disjunction of the contraries of its parts. The method applies to all compounds.

The contradictory of a universal compound is not a single negation but a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, ensuring at least one contradictory is true.

Determining contraries of partial compound propositions
Author specifies how to find the contrary of partial compound propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contrary of a partial compound modal proposition by applying the per-individual disjunction method.

Core Idea
Contrary of a partial compound is a disjunction holding for each individual.

In modal logic, partial (particular) compound propositions require a different method for finding their contrary than universal ones. Simply negating each part fails because the sum of contraries can be false. The author provides a precise technique: for each individual, assert a disjunction of the two opposite predicates. This method is essential for handling modal propositions in Islamic logical analysis, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh where contradictory checking is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the proposition type
Identify that the proposition is partial (juz'iyya) and compound (contains two simple parts). Example: 'Some body is animal not always' has parts: 'body is animal' and 'not always'.
Partial means it applies to some individuals, not all.
2
Recall why universal method fails
For universal compounds, the contrary is formed by taking the contraries of each part. But for partial compounds, this yields a false statement. The text shows that 'Some body is animal not always' is false even when each contrary part is false.
This demonstrates the insufficiency of the simple method.
3
Apply the per-individual disjunction
The correct contrary is a disjunction that holds for each individual: 'Each single individual of body is either animal always or not animal always'.
This is equivalent to a universal proposition covering all individuals.
4
Adapt to specific modal types
For modal types like 'urfiyya, waqtiyya, muntashira, mumkina, the contrary takes specific forms involving two modal propositions (e.g., 'possible temporal' vs 'permanent agreeing').
The pattern is always a disjunction of two opposites.
5
Enumerate the resulting simple propositions
From the analysis, six simple non-well-known propositions emerge: absolute temporal, absolute extended, possible temporal, absolute temporal (again? actually listed separately), etc. This shows the richness of modal logic.
These are not commonly used but are logically valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن كانت جزئيّة فلا يكفي في نقيضها ماذكرنا، لأنه يكذب بعض الجسم حيوان لادائماً مع كذب كل واحد من نقيضي جزايها، بل الحق في نقيضها أن يردّد بين نقيضي الجزاين لكل واحد واحد – أي كـلّ واحد واحد لا يخلو عن نقيضهما، فيقال: كلّ واحد واحد من أفراد الجسم إما حيوان دائماً أو ليس بحيوان دائماً». ونقيض العرفية الخاصة إمّا الحينية المطلقة المخالفة أو الدائمة الموافقة. ونقيض الوقتية إما الممكنة الوقتية - وهي ما سلب فيها الضرورة الوقتية ولابد أن تكون مخالفة للأصل في الكيف - وإما الدائمة الموافقة. ونقيض المنتشرة إما الممكنة الدائمة - وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المنتشرة وتكون مخالفة للأصل - وإما الدائمة الموافقة. ونقيض الممكنة الخاصة إما الضرورية المخالفة أو الضرورية الموافقة. فحصل هاهنا قضيتان بسيطتان هما نقيضا الجزأين الأولين من الوقتية والمنتشرة أعني الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، وليس شيء من هذه الأربع من القضايا المشهورة، فثبت ست قضايا بسيطة غير مشهورة : هذه الأربع والحينية الممكنة والحينية المطلقة (شريف).
He said: And if it is partial, then what we mentioned is not sufficient for its contrary, because 'Some body is animal not always' is false while each one of the contraries of its two parts is false. Rather the correct in its contrary is that it be divided between the two contraries of the two parts for each one individually—i.e., each single individual is not free from its two contraries, so it is said: Each single individual of the individuals of body is either animal always or not animal always. And the contrary of the conditional special is either the temporal absolute differing or the permanent agreeing. And the contrary of the temporal is either the possible temporal—which is that in which temporal necessity is negated and must be differing from the original in quality—or the permanent agreeing. And the contrary of the extended is either the possible permanent—which is that in which the extended necessity is denied and is differing from the original—or the permanent agreeing. And the contrary of the possible special is either the necessary differing or the necessary agreeing. So here two simple propositions are obtained which are the two contraries of the first two parts of the temporal and extended, namely the absolute temporal and absolute extended; and none of these four are among the well-known propositions; thus six simple non-well-known propositions are established: these four and the possible temporal and absolute temporal (Sharif).
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage deals with finding the contradictory (nāqiḍ) of partial (juz'iyya) compound propositions. The author begins by stating that for partial compounds, the earlier method for universal compounds is insufficient. He gives a counterexample: the partial proposition 'Some body is animal not always' (ba'd al-jism hayawān lā dā'iman) is false even though each of the contraries of its two simple parts is false. Therefore, the correct method is to formulate the contrary as a disjunction that holds for each individual separately: 'Each single individual of body is either animal always or not animal always' (kull wāḥid wāḥid min afrād al-jism immā ḥayawān dā'iman aw laysa bi-ḥayawān dā'iman). This means that for every instance, one of the two contraries applies. The passage then lists the specific contrary forms for various types of partial modal compounds: the 'urfiyya khāṣṣa (special conditional), waqtiyya (temporal), muntashira (extended), and mumkina khāṣṣa (special possible). For each, the contrary is given as a disjunction of two simple modal propositions. Finally, the author notes that from these, two simple propositions emerge that are the contraries of the first parts of the temporal and extended, namely the absolute temporal (al-waqtiyya al-muṭlaqa) and absolute extended (al-muntashira al-muṭlaqa). None of these four are among the well-known (mashhūra) propositions. Thus six simple non-well-known propositions are established: these four plus the possible temporal (al-ḥīniyya al-mumkina) and absolute temporal (al-ḥīniyya al-muṭlaqa). The commentator (Sharif) is credited with this final point.

The Failure of Simple Disjunction for Particular Composites
Author explains the failure of simple disjunction and provides correct rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the simple disjunction of universal contradictories fails for particular composite propositions and construct the correct contradictory using disjunction per individual.

Core Idea
For particular composites, a simple disjunction of universals fails; you must disjoin per individual.

You have learned that the contradictory of a universal composite is a disjunction between the contradictories of its two parts. But does the same rule apply to particular composites? The author shows with an example that it does not, because the subject individuals may be divided: some always have the predicate, others always lack it. He then provides the correct method: a disjunction applied to each individual subject.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the proposition type
Identify that the proposition is a particular composite (e.g., 'Some body is animal not-eternally'). It has a particular quantifier and a temporal/modal modifier making it composite.
2
Recall the simple disjunction rule for universals
The rule for universal composites was a disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts (e.g., 'Either every S is P eternally or no S is P eternally'). Keep this in mind.
3
Understand why the simple rule fails for particulars
Consider a situation where the predicate is eternally true of some individuals and eternally false of others. Then the particular composite is false (no individual has the predicate sometimes and not sometimes), but both universal contradictories are also false (one is false because of the eternally false individuals, the other because of the eternally true individuals). So the simple disjunction would be false together with the original, meaning it is not the contradictory.
Reflect on the example: 'Some body is animal not-eternally' fails when some bodies are always animal and others are never animal.
4
Apply the correct rule: disjunction per individual
The correct contradictory is: 'Every single individual of the subject is either eternally P or eternally not P.' This is a disjunction applied to each individual separately, ensuring that the original is true exactly when this is false.
The author phrases it as 'ترديد بين نقيضي الجزأين لكل واحد واحد' – a disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts for each single individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وإن كانت جزئيّة فلا يكفي في نقيضها ماذكرنا، لأنه يكذب بعض الجسم حيوان لادائماً مع كذب كل واحد من نقيضي جزايها، بل الحق في نقيضها أن يردّد بين نقيضي الجزاين لكل واحد واحد – أي كـلّ واحد واحد لا يخلو عن نقيضهما، فيقال: كلّ واحد واحد من أفراد الجسم إما حيوان دائماً أو ليس بحيوان دائماً». ونقيض العرفية الخاصة إمّا الحينية المطلقة المخالفة أو الدائمة الموافقة. ونقيض الوقتية إما الممكنة الوقتية - وهي ما سلب فيها الضرورة الوقتية ولابد أن تكون مخالفة للأصل في الكيف - وإما الدائمة الموافقة. ونقيض المنتشرة إما الممكنة الدائمة - وهي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المنتشرة وتكون مخالفة للأصل - وإما الدائمة الموافقة. ونقيض الممكنة الخاصة إما الضرورية المخالفة أو الضرورية الموافقة. فحصل هاهنا قضيتان بسيطتان هما نقيضا الجزأين الأولين من الوقتية والمنتشرة أعني الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، وليس شيء من هذه الأربع من القضايا المشهورة، فثبت ست قضايا بسيطة غير مشهورة : هذه الأربع والحينية الممكنة والحينية المطلقة (شريف). القول : مامرٌ كان المركبات الكلية. وأما المركبات الجزئية فلا يكفي في نقيضها ماذكرناه من المفهوم المردّد بين نقيضي الجزأين، لجواز كذب المركبة الجزئية مع كذب المفهوم المردد ، فإن من الجائز أن يكون المحمول ثابتاً دائماً لبعض أفراد الموضوع ومسلوباً دائماً عن الأفراد الباقية فتكذب الجزئية اللادائمة ، لأن مفهومها أن بعض أفراد الموضوع يكون بحيث يثبت له المحمول تارة ويسلب عنه أخرى، ولافرد من أفراد الموضوع في تلك المادة كذلك. ويكذب أيضاً كل واحد من نقيضي جزأيها - أي كليتين - أما الكلية الموجبة فلدوام سلب المحمول عن بعض الأفراد. وأما الكلية السالبة فلدوام إيجاب المحمول لبعض الأفراد، كقولنا : «بعض الجسم حيوان لادائماً»، فإن الحيوان ثابت لبعض أفراد الجسم دائماً ومسلوب عن أفراده الباقية دائماً، فتلك الجزئية كاذبة مع كذب قولنا : «كل جسم حيوان دائماً، ولاشيء من الجسم بحيوان دائماً». بل الحق في نقيضها أن يردّد بين نقيضي الجزأين لكل واحد واحد، لأنا إذا قلنا : «بعض (ج) (ب) لادائماً» كان معناه أن بعض (ج) بحيث يثبت له (ب) في وقت ولا يثبت له (ب) في وقت آخر، فنقيضه : «آنه ليس كذلك»، وإذا لم يكن بعض أفراد (ج) بحيث يكون (ب) في وقت ولا يكون (ب) في وقت آخر يكون كلّ واحد واحد من أفراد (ج) إما (ب) دائماً أو ليس (ب) دائماً ؛ وهو الترديد بين نقيضي الجزأين لكلّ واحد واحد - أي كلّ واحد واحد لا يخلو عن نقيضهما - فيقال في تلك المادة : «كل جسم إما حيوان دائماً أو ليس بحيوان دائماً»، ويشتمل على
He said: If [the proposition] is particular, then what we mentioned [previously] is not sufficient for its contradictory, because the statement 'Some body is animal not-eternally [la da'iman]' is false while each of the contradictories of its two parts [i.e., the two universal propositions] is also false. Rather, the correct [rule] for its contradictory is that it is a disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts for each single individual—i.e., each single individual is not free from [one of] their contradictories—so it is said: 'Every single individual of the individuals of body is either eternally animal or not eternally animal.' And the contradictory of the particular 'urfiyya [conditional] is either the absolute hīniyya [temporal] that is opposite in [modal] quality, or the perpetual [dā'ima] that agrees. And the contradictory of the particular waqtīyya [time-restricted] is either the possible-waqtīyya—which is that wherein the time-necessity is negated and must be opposite in [modal] quality to the original—or the perpetual that agrees. And the contradictory of the particular muntashira [diffuse] is either the possible-perpetual—which is that which judges the negation of the diffuse necessity and is opposite to the original—or the perpetual that agrees. And the contradictory of the particular mumkina khāssa [special possible] is either the necessary [dartiriyya] that is opposite or the necessary that agrees. Thus there arise here two simple propositions which are the contradictories of the first two parts of the waqtīyya and the muntashira, namely the absolute waqtīyya and the absolute muntashira. And none of these four are among the well-known [mashhūra] propositions. So six simple non-well-known propositions are established: these four, the possible-hīniyya, and the absolute hīniyya (Sharīf). Statement: What has been said was concerning universal composites. As for particular composites, what we mentioned [i.e., the concept of a disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts] is not sufficient for their contradictory, because it is possible that the particular composite is false while the disjunctive concept is also false. For it is possible that the predicate is eternally affirmed of some individuals of the subject and eternally negated of the remaining individuals, so the non-perpetual particular is false—because its concept is that some individuals of the subject are such that the predicate is sometimes affirmed of them and sometimes negated, and no individual of the subject in that matter is like that. Also each one of the contradictories of its two parts—i.e., the two universal [propositions]—is false: as for the universal affirmative, because of the eternal negation of the predicate from some individuals; and as for the universal negative, because of the eternal affirmation of the predicate to some individuals, as in our saying: 'Some body is animal not-eternally,' for animal is eternally affirmed of some individuals of body and eternally negated of its remaining individuals. So that particular is false while our saying: 'Every body is eternally animal' and 'Nothing of body is eternally animal' are also false. Rather, the correct [rule] for its contradictory is that it is a disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts for each single individual, because when we say: 'Some J is B not-eternally,' its meaning is that some J is such that B is affirmed of it at a time and not affirmed at another time. So its contradictory is: 'It is not the case.' And if some individuals of J are not such that B is at a time and not B at another time, then every single individual of J is either B eternally or not B eternally. And that is the disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts for each single individual—i.e., each single individual is not free from their contradictories—so it is said in that matter: 'Every body is either eternally animal or not eternally animal.' And it includes...

Commentary
This passage defines the correct method for finding the contradictory of a particular composite proposition. The author begins by stating that the previous method (a simple disjunction between the contradictories of the two parts) works for universal composites but fails for particular ones. He illustrates with the example 'Some body is animal not-eternally' (بعض الجسم حيوان لادائماً). This particular composite is false when the subject individuals are divided: some have the predicate eternally, others lack it eternally—so no individual has it sometimes and not sometimes. In such a case, the simple disjunction 'Either every body is eternally animal or nothing is' (the two universals) is also false. Therefore, the correct contradictory is a disjunction applied to each individual: 'Every single individual of body is either eternally animal or not eternally animal.' The author then lists the contradictories for specific types of particular composite propositions: 'urfiyya khāssa (special conditional), waqtīyya (time-restricted), muntashira (diffuse), and mumkina khāssa (special possible). For each, he gives two alternatives: one opposite in modal quality and one that agrees. Finally, he summarizes that six simple non-well-known propositions emerge from these rules.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a particular composite proposition is a disjunction applied to each individual subject, not a simple disjunction of the two universal contradictories.

Contradictories of Specific Particular Composite Types
Author lists contradictories for four specific particular composite types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct contradictory forms for each of the four particular composite types: 'urfiyya khāssa, waqtīyya, muntashira, and mumkina khāssa.

Core Idea
Each particular composite type has two possible contradictories: one opposite in modal quality, one agreeing.

After establishing the general rule that the contradictory of a particular composite is a per-individual disjunction, the author applies this to four specific types: the special conditional ('urfiyya khāssa), the time-restricted (waqtīyya), the diffuse (muntashira), and the special possible (mumkina khāssa). For each, he gives two forms: one opposite in quality (e.g., necessary vs. possible) and one that agrees (the perpetual).

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of particular composite
The passage lists four types: 'urfiyya khāssa (special conditional), waqtīyya (time-restricted), muntashira (diffuse), and mumkina khāssa (special possible). Recognize which one you are dealing with.
Each has a distinct modal character (conditionality, time necessity, diffuse necessity, possibility).
2
Recall the two categories: opposite and agreeing
For each type, the contradictory is either a different modal proposition that is opposite in modal 'quality' (e.g., absolute vs. necessary) and typically opposite in truth value, or it is a perpetual (dā'ima) proposition that agrees in quality but is always either affirmed or denied universally.
The perpetual form is the same across all types: a universal categorical with eternal modality.
3
Apply the rule to 'urfiyya khāssa
The contradictory is either the absolute hīniyya (temporal) that is opposite in quality (i.e., universal negative with temporal modality) or the perpetual (dā'ima) that agrees (universal affirmative with eternal modality).
Hīniyya refers to a proposition about a specific moment; absolute here means without necessity.
4
Apply to waqtīyya and muntashira
For waqtīyya, the contradictory is the possible-waqtīyya (negation of time necessity, opposite in quality) or the perpetual. For muntashira, it is the possible-perpetual (negation of diffuse necessity, opposite) or the perpetual.
The possible forms negate the necessity component and change the quality.
5
Apply to mumkina khāssa
For the special possible, the contradictory is either the necessary (dartiriyya) that is opposite (i.e., universal affirmative with necessity) or the necessary that agrees (universal negative with necessity).
Interestingly, both options are necessary; only the quality differs.

Contradicting Partial Compound Propositions
Author explains the correct method for contradicting partial compound propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the correct contradictory of any partial compound proposition using the distributive disjunction method.

Core Idea
Partial compounds need a distributive per-individual contradiction, not a simple global disjunction.

Contradicting a universal compound is straightforward: it's the disjunction of the contradictories of its parts. But partial compounds (like 'Some S is P not always') are trickier. The predicate might hold constantly for some individuals and be constantly absent for others, making the partial false while the simple disjunction of universal contradictories also false. The correct contradictory must distribute over each individual, and can even be expanded into a tripartite exclusive disjunction. This lesson unpacks why and how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound type
Determine whether the compound is universal (e.g., 'Every S is P always') or partial (e.g., 'Some S is P not always'). The contradiction method differs.
Partial compounds are often marked by 'some' and temporal modifiers like 'not always'.
2
Test the simple disjunction method
For universal compounds, the contradictory is the disjunction of the two universal contradictories (e.g., 'Every S is P always' or 'No S is P always'). But for partials, this fails: consider 'Some liquids are intoxicating not always'. If some liquids are always intoxicating and others never, the partial is false, and both universal contradictories are also false.
The simple disjunction can be false when the partial is false, so it is not the correct contradictory.
3
Apply the distributive method per individual
The meaning of 'Some S is P not always' is that some individual of S has P at one time and lacks P at another. Its contradictory is that no individual has that property. That means every individual is either P always or not P always. Thus the contradictory is a disjunction over each individual: 'Every S is either P always or not P always'.
This distributive disjunction (li-kull wāḥid wāḥid) ensures that for every individual, one of the two contradictories holds.
4
Expand to the tripartite exclusive disjunction
The 'not P always' branch splits into two possibilities: never P, or P sometimes and not P sometimes. So the contradictory can be expressed as a three-option exclusive disjunction: 'Either every S is P always, or no S is P always, or some S is P always and some S is not P always'.
This tripartite form (man'iat al-khuluw) is equivalent to the distributive method and is sometimes easier to apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناقض
القول : مامرٌ كان المركبات الكلية. وأما المركبات الجزئية فلا يكفي في نقيضها ماذكرناه من المفهوم المردّد بين نقيضي الجزأين، لجواز كذب المركبة الجزئية مع كذب المفهوم المردد ، فإن من الجائز أن يكون المحمول ثابتاً دائماً لبعض أفراد الموضوع ومسلوباً دائماً عن الأفراد الباقية فتكذب الجزئية اللادائمة ، لأن مفهومها أن بعض أفراد الموضوع يكون بحيث يثبت له المحمول تارة ويسلب عنه أخرى، ولافرد من أفراد الموضوع في تلك المادة كذلك.
ويكذب أيضاً كل واحد من نقيضي جزأيها - أي كليتين - أما الكلية الموجبة فلدوام سلب المحمول عن بعض الأفراد. وأما الكلية السالبة فلدوام إيجاب المحمول لبعض الأفراد، كقولنا : «بعض الجسم حيوان لادائماً»، فإن الحيوان ثابت لبعض أفراد الجسم دائماً ومسلوب عن أفراده الباقية دائماً، فتلك الجزئية كاذبة مع كذب قولنا : «كل جسم حيوان دائماً، ولاشيء من الجسم بحيوان دائماً».
بل الحق في نقيضها أن يردّد بين نقيضي الجزأين لكل واحد واحد، لأنا إذا قلنا : «بعض (ج) (ب) لادائماً» كان معناه أن بعض (ج) بحيث يثبت له (ب) في وقت ولا يثبت له (ب) في وقت آخر، فنقيضه : «آنه ليس كذلك»، وإذا لم يكن بعض أفراد (ج) بحيث يكون (ب) في وقت ولا يكون (ب) في وقت آخر يكون كلّ واحد واحد من أفراد (ج) إما (ب) دائماً أو ليس (ب) دائماً ؛ وهو الترديد بين نقيضي الجزأين لكلّ واحد واحد - أي كلّ واحد واحد لا يخلو عن نقيضهما - فيقال في تلك المادة : «كل جسم إما حيوان دائماً أو ليس بحيوان دائماً»، ويشتمل على ثلاثة مفهومات : لأنّ كلّ واحد واحد من أفراد الموضوع لا يخلو : إما أن يثبت له المحمول دائماً، أو لا يثبت له دائماً؛ وإذا لم يثبت له فلا يخلو : إما أن يكون مسلوباً عن كلّ واحد دائماً أو مسلوباً عن البعض دائماً، ثابتاً للبعض دائماً، فالجزء الثاني مشتمل على مفهومين، فلو ركبت منفصلة مانعة الخلو من هذه المفهومات الثلاث لكانت مساوية أيضاً لنقيضها، كقولنا : إما كلّ (ج) ب) دائماً أو لاشيء من (ج ب) دائماً أو بعض (ج ب دائماً وبعض (ج) ليس (ب) دائماً » فهو طريق ثان في أخذ النقيض. فإن قلت : كما أن المركبة الكلية عبارة عن مجموع قضيتين فكذلك المركبة الجزئية، ورفع المجموع إنما هو برفـع أحد الجزأين – أي أحد نقيضي الجزأين الذي هو المفهوم المراد - فكما يكفي في نقيض الكلية فليكف في نقيض الجزئية، وإلا فما الفرق ؟
قلت : مفهوم الكلية المركبة هو بعينه مفهوم الكليتين المختلفتين بالإيجاب والسلب، فإذا أخذ نقيضاهما يكون أحد نقيضيهما مساوياً لنقيضها ؛ وأما مفهوم الجزئية المركبة فهو ليس بعينه مفهوم الجزئيتين المختلفتين إيجاباً وسلباً، لأن موضوع الإيجاب في المركبة الكلية بعينه موضوع السلب، وموضوع الجزئية الموجبة لا يجب أن يكون موضوع الجزئية السالبة الجواز تغايرهما، بل مفهوم الجزئيتين أعم من مفهوم المركبة الجزئية، لأنه متى صدقت الجزئيتان المختلفتان بالإيجاب والسلب مع اتحاد الموضوع صدق الجزئيتان المختلفتان بالإيجاب والسلب مطلقاً بدون العكس، فيكون أحد نقيضيهما أخص من نقيض مفهوم الجزئية، لأن نقيض الأعمّ أخص من نقيض الأخص، فلايكون مساوياً لنقيضه
The contradiction: The statement: What passed was with respect to universal compounds. As for partial compounds, what we mentioned of the disjunctive concept between the two contradictories of the two parts is not sufficient in their contradiction, because it is possible for the partial compound to be false while the disjunctive concept is false. For it is possible that the predicate is always affirmed of some individuals of the subject and always denied of the remaining individuals, so the non-permanent partial is false, because its concept is that some individuals of the subject are such that the predicate is sometimes affirmed and sometimes denied, and no individual of the subject in that matter is like that. And also each one of the two contradictories of its two parts—i.e., two universals—is false: the affirmative universal because of the perpetual denial of the predicate from some individuals, and the negative universal because of the perpetual affirmation of the predicate for some individuals, like our saying: 'Some body is animal not always.' For animal is always affirmed of some individuals of body and always denied of its remaining individuals, so that partial is false along with the falsehood of our saying: 'Every body is animal always, and No body is animal always.' Rather, the truth in its contradiction is that it be disjoined between the two contradictories of the two parts for each individual, because when we say 'Some (J) is (B) not always,' its meaning is that some (J) is such that (B) is affirmed for it at one time and not affirmed for it at another time. Its contradictory is: 'It is not so.' And if some individuals of (J) are not such that (B) is affirmed at one time and not affirmed at another time, then every single individual of (J) is either (B) always or not (B) always. And that is the disjunction between the two contradictories of the two parts for each individual—i.e., every single individual is not free from their two contradictories. So it is said in that matter: 'Every body is either animal always or not animal always,' and it includes three concepts: because every single individual of the subject is not free: either the predicate is affirmed for it always, or not affirmed for it always; and if not affirmed, then either it is denied of it always or denied of some and affirmed of some always. So the second part contains two concepts. So if you construct an exclusive disjunction (man'iat al-khuluw) from these three concepts, it would also be equivalent to its contradictory, like our saying: 'Either every J is B always, or nothing of J is B always, or some J is B always and some J is not B always.' So that is a second way of taking the contradictory. If you say: Just as the universal compound is a sum of two propositions, so also the partial compound is a sum of two propositions, and the removal of the sum is only by removing one of the two parts—i.e., one of the two contradictories of the two parts which is the intended concept—so just as it suffices in the contradiction of the universal, let it suffice in the contradiction of the partial. Otherwise, what is the difference? I say: The concept of the universal compound is identical to the concept of the two universals differing in affirmation and negation. So when their two contradictories are taken, one of their two contradictories is equivalent to its contradictory. As for the concept of the partial compound, it is not identical to the concept of the two partials differing in affirmation and negation, because the subject of affirmation in the universal compound is identical to the subject of negation, whereas the subject of the affirmative partial need not be the subject of the negative partial—it is possible that they differ. Rather, the concept of the two partials is more general than the concept of the partial compound, because whenever the two partials differing in affirmation and negation are true with unity of subject, the two partials differing in affirmation and negation are true absolutely, without the converse. So one of their two contradictories is more specific than the contradictory of the concept of the partial, because the contradictory of the more general is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific, so it is not equivalent to its contradictory.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the correct method for finding the contradictory (naqid) of a partial compound proposition (murakkaba juz'iyya). The author first explains why the simple disjunctive method used for universal compounds fails for partials. For universal compounds, the contradictory is a disjunction between the two contradictories of the parts (e.g., 'Every S is P always' or 'No S is P always'). For partials like 'Some body is animal not always,' the predicate may be always true for some individuals and always false for others, making the partial false even though the disjunction of the two universal contradictories is also false (both 'Every body is animal always' and 'No body is animal always' are false). Thus the simple disjunction is not sufficient. The correct contradictory is a disjunction for each individual (kull wāhid wāhid) of the subject: each individual either has the predicate always or does not have it always. This yields a three-concept disjunction: always affirmed, never affirmed, or sometimes affirmed and sometimes denied. An objection is raised: since partial compounds are also sums of two propositions, why not treat them like universal compounds? The answer: in universal compounds, the subject of affirmation is identical to the subject of negation, so the two universal contradictories cover all possibilities. In partial compounds, the subject of the affirmative partial may differ from that of the negative partial, making the concept of two partials (affirmative and negative with possibly different subjects) more general than the partial compound's concept. Hence the contradictory of that more general concept is more specific and not equivalent to the contradictory of the partial compound. The correct method is to use a tripartite exclusive disjunction (man'iat al-khuluw) that exhausts the possibilities for each individual.

Key Terms
نقيض
مركبة جزئية
مركبة كلية
مانعة الخلو
لكل واحد واحد

A) كل جسم حيوان دائماً أو ليس حيوان دائماً
B) كل جسم إما حيوان دائماً أو ليس بحيوان دائماً
C) كل جسم حيوان دائماً أو لا شيء من جسم بحيوان دائماً
D) كل جسم إما حيوان دائماً أو لا شيء من جسم بحيوان دائماً أو بعض جسم حيوان دائماً وبعضه ليس بحيوان دائماً

Key Takeaway
To contradict a partial compound proposition, you must use a distributive disjunction over individuals, not a simple global disjunction of universal contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Simple Conversion and Its Proof Conditions
Author defines simple conversion and states two requirements for proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of simple conversion, list the two requirements for proving a conversion, and identify which proposition types can be simply converted.

Core Idea
Simple conversion is not a blanket operation—it requires logical necessity and minimal specificity.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of simple conversion (العكس المستوي). The author defines it as swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth and quality. But not every proposition converts: only those that satisfy two conditions. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments that rely on conversion, especially in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two senses of conversion
The author notes that 'conversion' can refer to the act of exchanging parts or to the resulting proposition. Make sure you understand which sense is being used in a given context.
2
Extract the definition of simple conversion
Primary definition: making the first part second and the second first, while preserving truth and quality. 'Preserving quality' means the proposition remains affirmative or negative as in the original.
The second sense defines conversion as the most specific proposition necessarily entailed by the original via exchange.
3
Identify the two requirements for proof
First requirement: the converted must be necessarily entailed by the original (لازمة), proven by a general demonstration. Second requirement: no more specific proposition than the converted is necessary for the original; otherwise a counterexample would show it fails.
The second requirement ensures the conversion is not too strong.
4
Apply the rule for particular negatives
The author says a particular negative does not convert except in the case of 'the two specifics' (الخاصتين). Test this with examples: e.g., 'Some animals are not humans' is true but 'Some humans are not animals' is false, showing failure.
The two specifics likely refer to propositions where both terms are species or proper names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال :
البحث الثاني
في العكس المستوي
وهو عبارة عن جعل الجزء الأول من القضية ثانياً والثاني أولاً، مع بقاء الصدق والكيف بحالهما.
القول : من أحكام القضايا العكس المستوي، وهو عبارة عن جعل
(١) كما أن العكس المستوى يطلق على المعنى المصدري المذكور - وهو تبديل الجزء الأول من القضية بالثاني، والثاني بالأول... الخ - كذلك يطلق على القضية الحاصلة بالتبديل، فيقال - مثلاً - : عكس الموجبة الكلية موجبة جزئية»، فيشتق من العكس بالمعنى الأول دون الثاني.
ويعرف العكس بالمعنى الثاني بأنها أخص قضيّة لازمة للقضية بطريق التبديل موافقة لها في الكيف والصدق
فلابد في إثبات العكس من أمرين :
أحدهما أن هذه القضيّة لازمة للأصل وذلك بالبرهان المنطبق على المواد كلها.
والثاني أن ما هو أخص من تلك القضية ليست لازمة لذلك الأصل، ويظهر ذلك بالتخلف في بعض الصور.
والضابط في السوالب أنّ السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس إلا في الخاصتين، فإنهما ينعكسان عرفية خاصة.
He said: The second inquiry: On simple conversion. It is defined as making the first part of the proposition the second, and the second the first, while preserving truth and quality as they are. The discourse: From the rulings of propositions: simple conversion. And it is defined as making... (1) Just as 'simple conversion' is applied to the infinitive meaning mentioned—which is the exchange of the first part of the proposition with the second, and the second with the first, etc.—likewise it is applied to the proposition resulting from the exchange, so it is said, for example: 'The conversion of a universal affirmative is a particular affirmative,' which is derived from conversion in the first sense, not the second. And conversion in the second sense is defined as the most specific proposition necessary for the original proposition by way of exchange, agreeing with it in quality and truth. So to prove conversion, two things are required: First, that this proposition is necessary for the original, by a proof that applies to all matters. Second, that what is more specific than that proposition is not necessary for that original, and this is shown by its failure in some cases. The rule for negatives: the particular negative does not convert except in the case of the two specifics (al-khassatayn), for they convert as a specific customary conversion.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion (العكس المستوي) and its conditions. The author distinguishes two senses: conversion as the act of exchanging parts (المعنى المصدري), and conversion as the resulting proposition (القضية الحاصلة). The definition given first is: swapping the subject and predicate while keeping truth and quality unchanged. The author then adds a second definition for the resulting proposition: it is the most specific (أخص) proposition that is necessarily entailed by the original via exchange and agrees in quality and truth. To prove a conversion, two things are required: (1) the converted proposition must be necessary for the original (i.e., it follows logically from it), proven by a general demonstration, and (2) no more specific proposition than that converted is necessary for the original, shown by counterexample where a more specific proposition fails to follow. For negative propositions, the particular negative does not convert except in the case of 'الخاصتين' (the two specifics), which convert as a 'customary specific' conversion. This likely refers to propositions where both terms are species or proper names, allowing conversion of particular negatives in certain contexts. The student should understand that simple conversion is not universally valid; only certain types convert.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
الخاصتين
لازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The converted is necessary and agrees in quality

C) The converted is necessary and truth is preserved
D) The converted is necessary and the original is false

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion requires the converted proposition to be necessarily entailed by the original and to be the most specific necessary consequence; particular negatives generally do not convert except in special cases.

Conversion Rules: Negative and Affirmative Propositions with Temporal Modalities
Author lays out detailed conversion rules for negatives and affirmatives including temporal modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid conversion of a negative or affirmative categorical proposition given its temporal modality (descriptive perpetuity, essential perpetuity, general absoluteness).

Core Idea
Conversion of universal negatives and affirmatives depends on temporal perpetuity and absoluteness—it is not always simple.

This passage from al-Qutbi's commentary lays out precise rules for converting categorical propositions, distinguishing between negative and affirmative types. You will learn how temporal conditions like descriptive perpetuity (al-dawām al-waṣfī) and essential perpetuity (al-dawām al-dhātī) affect the validity and form of conversion. By mastering these rules, you will be able to correctly apply immediate inference in Islamic theological and legal reasoning, ensuring that derived propositions preserve truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type and quantifier
Read the original proposition and determine if it is a negative or affirmative, and whether it is universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz’iyya). This is the starting point for applying the conversion rules.
For example, 'No intoxicant is permitted' is a universal negative.
2
Determine temporal modality for universal negatives
If the proposition is a universal negative, check if it has descriptive perpetuity (dawām waṣfī). If it does not, it never converts. If it does, further check for essential perpetuity (dawām dhātī) to decide between conversion to essential or descriptive perpetuity.
Descriptive perpetuity means the predicate holds of the subject permanently as a description; essential perpetuity means it holds by virtue of essence.
3
Apply conversion rule for affirmatives
For affirmatives, first check if the proposition has general absoluteness (iṭlāq ‘āmm). If not, its conversion status is unknown. If it does, then test for descriptive perpetuity and non-perpetuity (lā-dawām) to determine the temporal qualification of the converse.
General absoluteness means the proposition is not temporally restricted (applicable to both possible propositions).
4
Understand the reductio justification
The author defends conversion validity with a proof by contradiction: if the original is true and the converse false, then the contradictory of the converse would be true with the original, leading to an impossibility. Reflect on this logical structure.
The objection about Zayd's standing and not standing is answered by distinguishing the impossibility of the conjunction from impossibility of parts.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Take concrete propositions from Islamic sciences (e.g., 'No wine is halal', 'Every human is mortal') and apply the rules to determine their valid converse.
Use the passage's categories to check your work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضابط في السوالب أنّ السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس إلا في الخاصتين، فإنهما ينعكسان عرفية خاصة. وأما السالبة الكلية، فإن لم يصدق عليها الدوام الوصفي - أعني العرفي العام - فلا تنعكس أصلاً، وهي السوالب السبع المذكورة، إن صدق عليها الدوام الوصفي؛ فإن صدق عليها الدوام الذاتي أيضاً انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الذاتي، وإلا انعكست كليّة إلى الدوام الوصفي إن لم تكن مقيدة باللادوام ، وإن كانت مقيدة به انعكست كلّيّة إلى الدوام الوصفي مع قيد اللادوام في البعض. وإذا قلنا : إنه إذا صدق الأصل صدق العكس معه وإلا لصدق نقيضه معه أردنا أنه يجب صدق العكس مع صدق الأصل، وإلا لأمكن صدق نقيضه معه، ويلزم معه إمكان المحال - وهو محال. فإن قيل : جاز أن يكون المحال لازماً لمجموع الأصل ونقيض العكس - لالهيأة التركيب ولا لخصوصية شيء منهما - فلايلزم استحالة النقيض، الاترى أن اجتماع قيام زيد مع عدم قيامه يستلزم اجتماع النقيضين، وليس شيء منهما محالاً ؟ قلنا: المراد استحالة اجتماع نقيض العكس مع الأصل، وذلك حاصل لاستلزامه المحال، وجاز مع ذلك أن يكون نقيض العكس أمراً ممكناً في نفسه، لكنه مستحيل الاجتماع مع الأصل، فيجب صدق العكس مع الأصل وهو المطلوب. والضابط في الموجبات على ما ذكره : أن ما لا يصدق عليه الإطلاق العام - و هو الممكنتان - فحاله غير معلوم. وما يصدق عليه الإطلاق العام : فإن لم يصدق عليه الدوام الوصفي انعكس موجبة جزئية مطلقة عامة، سواء كان الأصل كلّيّاً أو جزئياً . قضايا. وإن صدق عليه الدوام الوصفي، فإن لم يكن مقيداً باللادوام العكس موجبة جزئية حينية مطلقة - وهي أربع قضايا. وإن كان مقيداً به انعكس موجبة جزئية حينيّة مطلقة لادائمة - وهما قضيتان (شريف).
And the rule for negatives is that the particular negative does not convert except in the two special cases, for they convert as a special customary. As for the universal negative, if descriptive perpetuity - meaning general customary - does not apply to it, then it does not convert at all, and they are the seven mentioned negatives. If descriptive perpetuity applies to it, then if essential perpetuity also applies to it, it converts as universal to essential perpetuity; otherwise it converts as universal to descriptive perpetuity if it is not qualified by non-perpetuity; and if it is qualified by it, it converts as universal to descriptive perpetuity with the qualification of non-perpetuity in some. And when we say: if the original is true, the converse is true with it, otherwise its contradictory would be true with it, we mean that the converse must be true with the truth of the original, otherwise its contradictory could be true with it, and the possibility of the impossible would follow - which is impossible. If it is said: It is possible that the impossible is entailed by the conjunction of the original and the contradictory of the converse - not due to the composite form nor to the particularity of either - so the impossibility of the contradictory does not follow. Do you not see that the conjunction of Zayd's standing with his not standing entails the conjunction of two contradictories, yet neither of them is impossible? We say: What is meant is the impossibility of the conjunction of the contradictory of the converse with the original, and that is obtained because it entails the impossible. And it is possible that the contradictory of the converse is a possible thing in itself, but its conjunction with the original is impossible, so the truth of the converse with the original is necessary, and that is what is sought. And the rule for affirmatives according to what he mentioned: That which does not have general absoluteness - which are the two possibilities - its case is unknown. And that which has general absoluteness: if descriptive perpetuity does not apply to it, it converts as a particular affirmative absolute general, whether the original is universal or particular - propositions. And if descriptive perpetuity applies to it, then if it is not qualified by non-perpetuity, the converse is a particular affirmative temporal absolute - and they are four propositions. And if it is qualified by it, it converts as a particular affirmative temporal absolute non-perpetual - and they are two propositions (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed rules for conversion of negative and affirmative propositions, considering temporal modalities. For negatives: A particular negative (e.g., 'Some A is not B') does not convert at all except in two special cases (specified elsewhere, likely when the terms are 'special' or co-extensive). A universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B') fails to convert if it lacks 'descriptive perpetuity' (al-dawām al-waṣfī, also called 'general customary'), meaning the predicate is not permanently true of the subject in a descriptive sense. If it has descriptive perpetuity but not essential perpetuity (al-dawām al-dhātī, permanent by essence), it converts to a universal negative with descriptive permanence. If it also has essential permanence, it converts to a universal negative with essential permanence. If it is qualified by non-perpetuity (al-lā-dawām), the conversion adds that qualification. The author then defends the entailment condition with a reductio: If the converse were false while the original is true, then its contradictory would be true with the original, leading to an impossibility. A possible objection (that the impossible might be entailed by the conjunction without either being impossible, like Zayd standing and not standing) is answered: the impossibility is in the conjunction, not in each separate part. For affirmatives: Those that do not have 'general absoluteness' (al-iṭlāq al-‘āmm) — i.e., the two possible propositions (al-mumkinatān: possibly-A is B and possibly-A is not B) — their conversion status is unknown. Those that have general absoluteness: if they lack descriptive perpetuity, they convert as particular affirmative absolute general (both universal and particular originals become some B is A). If they have descriptive perpetuity but no non-perpetuity, they convert as particular affirmative temporal absolute (ḥīniyya muṭlaqa). If they are qualified by non-perpetuity, they convert as particular affirmative temporal absolute non-perpetual. There are specific counts of these propositions (four, two, etc.). An Islamic example: 'No wine is permissible' (universal negative) has descriptive perpetuity because it is always true descriptively; it converts to 'No permissible thing is wine' (universal negative). But 'No human is a stone' has essential perpetuity, converting as universal essential.

Key Terms
سالبة جزئية
سالبة كلية
دوام وصفي
دوام ذاتي
إطلاق عام
حينية

Key Takeaway
Conversion of categorical propositions in Islamic logic is not mechanical; it requires evaluating temporal modalities like descriptive perpetuity, essential perpetuity, and general absoluteness to ensure truth-preserving inference.

Understanding Simple Conversion and Its Parts
Author defines simple conversion as swapping the two parts with truth and quality preserved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion on categorical propositions and distinguish between uttered and real parts.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps roles in utterance, not in reality.

The author defines simple conversion as swapping the two parts of a proposition while preserving truth and quality. He gives examples from universal affirmative and universal negative statements. Crucially, he warns that the swap applies only to the uttered order, not to the logical subject and predicate. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying conversion in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define simple conversion
In your own words, state the author's definition: making the first part second and second part first while preserving truth and quality.
2
Examine the examples given
The author provides two examples: 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human'; 'No human is a stone' converts to 'No stone is a human'. Notice that in the first case the quantity changes from universal to particular.
3
Distinguish uttered parts from real parts
The author clarifies that the swap is only in the uttered order (الجزآن في الذكر), not in the real logical parts. The real subject is the essence (dhat) and the predicate is the description (wasf). Conversion does not change these roles.
This guards against a common misunderstanding that conversion changes the logical subject-predicate relation.
4
Apply conversion to an Islamic example
Take a fiqh proposition: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Convert it: 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant'. Verify that truth and quality (affirmative) are preserved.
5
Verify the distinction in the converse
In the converse, the subject is the essence of the original predicate, and the predicate is the description of the original subject. Confirm that the real logical parts are not swapped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزء الأول من القضيّة ثانياً والجزء الثاني أولاً، مع بقاء الصدق والكيف بحالهما ؛ كما إذا أردنا عكس قولنا : «كلّ إنسان حيوان» بدلنا جزأيه وقلنا : «بعض الحيوان إنسان أو عكس قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بحجر» قلنا : «لاشيء من الحجر بإنسان». فالمراد بالجزء الأول والثاني الجزءان في الذكر، لا في الحقيقة، فإن الجزء الأول والثاني من القضيّة في الحقيقة هو ذات الموضوع ووصف المحمول، فالعكس لا يصير ذات الموضوع محمولاً ووصف المحمول موضوعاً، بل موضوع العكس هو ذات المحمول في الأصل، ومحموله هو وصف الموضوع، فالتبدل ليس إلا في الجزأين في الذكر- أي في الوصف العنواني ووصف المحمول - لا في الجزأين الحقيقيين.
Making the first part of the proposition second and the second part first, while preserving truth and quality as they were; as when we wish to convert our statement 'Every human is an animal', we swap its two parts and say 'Some animal is a human', or to convert our statement 'No human is a stone', we say 'No stone is a human'. So what is meant by the first and second parts is the parts in utterance, not in reality, for the first and second parts of the proposition in reality are the essence of the subject and the description of the predicate. Thus conversion does not make the essence of the subject a predicate nor the description of the predicate a subject; rather, the subject of the converse is the essence of the predicate in the original, and its predicate is the description of the subject. So the exchange is only in the two parts in utterance—i.e., in the attributive description and the description of the predicate—not in the real two parts.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) as the operation of swapping the two parts of a proposition while maintaining truth (sidq) and quality (kayf). The author gives two examples from Fiqh-style reasoning: converting a universal affirmative ('Every human is an animal') yields a particular affirmative ('Some animal is a human'); converting a universal negative ('No human is a stone') yields another universal negative ('No stone is a human'). Critically, the author clarifies that the 'first part' and 'second part' refer to the uttered order, not the real logical subject and predicate. In reality, the subject is the essence (dhat) and the predicate is a description (wasf). Conversion only swaps the uttered descriptors, not the real essences. This precision guards against a misunderstanding: one might think conversion changes the logical role of subject and predicate, but it only changes their expression.

الجزآن في الذكر
الجزآن الحقيقيان

Does the Disjunctive Have a Converse?
Author raises and answers an objection about conversion of disjunctive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a disjunctive proposition has a converse and explain why logicians typically omit it.

Core Idea
Disjunctives do have a converse, but it is ignored as useless.

In classical logic, conversion is a key immediate inference, but logicians often assert that disjunctives lack a converse. An objector challenges this claim, and the author provides a nuanced answer: a genuine converse exists, but it is pragmatically ignored. This lesson unpacks that dialectical exchange and shows the balance between formal possibility and practical utility in the Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection (naqd)
Read the opening sentence. The objector argues that since the two disjuncts are distinguished in utterance and position, swapping them should produce a converse according to the definition of conversion. Yet logicians deny this. Why?
2
Grasp the definition of 'aks
Recall that conversion means swapping the two parts while preserving truth and quality. The objector uses this definition to claim that disjunctives qualify. Do you see the logic?
The definition of conversion is the same for categoricals and disjunctives here.
3
Analyze the author's response (jawab)
The author disagrees: 'We do not concede that disjunctives have no converse.' He gives an example: 'Either the number is even or odd' vs. 'Either the number is odd or even.' The meaning changes because the contrariety (mu'anada) is directed. So the converse exists but differs in meaning.
The key is that the judgment of contrariety is directional: evenness contrary to oddness is not the same as oddness contrary to evenness.
4
Distinguish logical possibility from practical utility
The author concludes that logicians ignored it because it yields no benefit—not because it's impossible. Thus, when they said 'no converse', they meant 'no useful converse'.
This highlights the pragmatic dimension of classical logic: not every valid inference is studied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون للمنفصلة عكس، لأن جزأيها متميزان في الذكر والوضع - وإن لم يتميزا بحسب الطبع – فإذا تـبدل أحدهما بالآخر يكون عكساً لها لصدق التعريف عليه، لكنهم صرّحوا بأنها لا عكس لها . لانا نقول : لا نسلم أن المنفصلة لا عكس لها، فإن المفهوم من قولنا : «إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً» الحكم على زوجية العدد بمعاندة الفردية، ومن قولنا : «إما أن يكون العدد فرداً أو زوجاً» الحكم على فردية العدد بمعاندة الزوجية؛ ولا شك أن المفهوم من معاندة هذا لذاك غير المفهوم من معاندة ذاك لهذا، فيكون للمنفصلة أيضاً عكس مغاير لها في المفهوم؛ إلا أنه لما لم يكن فيه فائدة لم يعتبروه؛ فكأنهم ماعنوا بقولهم لا عكس للمنفصلات» إلا ذلك.
It should not be said: On this basis, it would follow that the disjunctive has a converse, because its two parts are distinguished in utterance and position—even if not distinguished by nature—so if one of them is exchanged with the other, it would be a converse of it, since the definition applies to it. But they [the logicians] stated that it has no converse. For we say: We do not concede that the disjunctive has no converse. For the understanding from our statement 'Either the number is even or odd' is a judgment that the evenness of the number is contrary to oddness, and from our statement 'Either the number is odd or even' is a judgment that the oddness of the number is contrary to evenness. There is no doubt that the understanding of the contrariety of this to that is different from the understanding of the contrariety of that to this. So the disjunctive also has a converse different from it in concept; except that since there is no benefit in it, they did not consider it. Thus it is as if by their statement 'There is no converse for disjunctives' they meant only that.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange: an objection (naqd) and an answer (jawab). The objector argues that according to the definition of conversion (swapping the two uttered parts with preservation of truth and quality), disjunctive propositions should have a converse because their two parts are distinguished in utterance. Yet logicians deny that disjunctives have a converse. The author responds: we do not accept that disjunctives have no converse. In fact, the meaning of 'Either the number is even or odd' (judging that evenness is contrary to oddness) differs from 'Either the number is odd or even' (judging that oddness is contrary to evenness). So there is a genuine converse, but it is ignored because it yields no theoretical benefit. The author concludes that when logicians say 'no converse' they merely mean it is not considered useful, not that it is logically impossible. This lesson shows the precision of classical logic: formal conversion is defined strictly, but practical utility governs which conversions are studied.

Key Terms
منفصلة
عكس
معاندة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive proposition does have a formal converse, but logicians omit it because it yields no new insight—a lesson in the pragmatics of logic.
